Ta'zir as the Guarantor of Siyasa Shar'iyya
Definition of ta'zir and the maxim linking it to siyasa shar'iyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the relationship between ta'zir and siyasa shar'iyya and apply the maxim that ta'zir is the guarantor of siyasa shar'iyya to new penal cases.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is the flexible penal tool through which the ruler implements siyasa shar'iyya in the public interest.

This lesson explores the concept of ta'zir as a dynamic instrument of Islamic penal law, contrasting it with fixed hadd punishments. The text presents a key legal maxim: 'The chapter of ta'zir is the guarantor responsible for the rulings of siyasa shar'iyya.' Understanding this maxim is essential for grasping how Islamic law adapts to new circumstances while remaining rooted in Shari'ah principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand ta'zir as flexible penal law
Note that ta'zir is not restricted to fixed forms or amounts of punishment; it can adapt to the requirements of each situation.
This flexibility is the hallmark of ta'zir and distinguishes it from hadd.
2
Connect ta'zir to siyasa shar'iyya
Siyasa shar'iyya allows the ruler to act for public interest even without specific textual evidence. Ta'zir is the primary mechanism through which siyasa shar'iyya is implemented in penal matters.
Siyasa shar'iyya is broader than ta'zir, but ta'zir is its penal arm.
3
Learn the maxim: 'Ta'zir is the guarantor of siyasa shar'iyya'
The jurists state that the chapter of ta'zir ensures that siyasa shar'iyya's penal rulings can be realized. This maxim (qawida) is central to understanding the scope of ta'zir.
It is a wusta-level dabt maxim governing penal law.
4
Identify the scope and exceptions
Ta'zir applies to all crimes except those with fixed punishments (hudud, qisas). For those, the ruler cannot alter the penalty. This is an exception (istithna') to the maxim.
The exceptions are based on nass from Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧/٥٠ - يتضح مما سلف بيانه أن قاعدة التعزير الإسلامية هي قانون جزائي عام مرن غير مقيد ولا محدود، بل هو صالح لأن يلبس في كل ما يقتضيه من صور العقوبات ومقاديرها .
عصر
ومن ثم يعتبر الفقهاء التعزير داخلاً في نطاق السياسة الشرعية التي يعرفونها بأنها فعل شيء من الحاكم لمصلحة يراها وإن لم يرد فيه دليل شرعي خاص.
فالسياسة الشرعية هي من قبيل الاستصلاح الذي سلف بيانه في
المصادر التبعية لأحكام الشريعة .
وقد قالوا: إن باب التعزير هو الضامن المتكفل لأحكام السياسة الشرعية (ر: رد المحتار ١٤٨/٣ نقلاً عن البحر الرائق لابن نجيم)..
7/50 - It is clear from the preceding explanation that the Islamic ta'zir rule is a general, flexible penal law, not restricted or limited, but rather capable of taking whatever forms and amounts of punishment are required by the situation. Hence, the jurists consider ta'zir to fall within the scope of siyasa shar'iyya, which they define as the ruler's action for an interest he perceives, even if there is no specific textual evidence. Siyasa shar'iyya is of the type of istislah (public interest) that was previously explained among the secondary sources of Shari'ah rulings. And they said: The chapter of ta'zir is the guarantor responsible for the rulings of siyasa shar'iyya (R: Radd al-Muhtar 3/148, quoting from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq by Ibn Nujaym).
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This lesson summarizes the nature of ta'zir as a flexible instrument of Islamic penal law. It is not bound by fixed punishments but can adapt to circumstances. Ta'zir falls under siyasa shar'iyya, which allows the ruler to act in the public interest even without nass. This makes ta'zir the mechanism through which siyasa shar'iyya is implemented in penal matters. The jurists call ta'zir the 'guarantor' of siyasa shar'iyya, meaning it ensures that the state can respond to new crimes and situations justly.

Key Terms
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyasa al-shar'iyya): The ruler's governance according to Shari'ah, using discretion to achieve public interest even without specific textual evidence.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Consideration of public interest (maslaha mursala) as a source of law in the absence of nass.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for crimes that have no fixed hadd or expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the relationship between ta'zir and siyasa shar'iyya?
A) Ta'zir is independent of siyasa shar'iyya.
B) Ta'zir is the mechanism through which siyasa shar'iyya is implemented in penal matters.
C) Siyasa shar'iyya only applies to non-penal matters.
D) Ta'zir is only for minor offenses.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is the flexible penal mechanism that allows the ruler to implement siyasa shar'iyya, adapting punishments to changing circumstances while respecting fixed hadd punishments.

Codification of Ta'zir Punishments in Modern Law
Modern codification of punishments is consistent with ta'zir flexibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that ta'zir delegation permits codification of punishments, and identify where modern penal codes conflict with fixed shar'i penalties.

Core Idea
Codifying ta'zir penalties is valid because the ruler's discretion includes setting fixed legal punishments — unless hadd or qisas are violated.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa addresses a modern concern: does the flexibility of ta'zir in Islamic law forbid fixed penal codes? Al-Zarqa argues that codification is actually a valid exercise of the ruler's delegated authority. However, he warns that modern codes often overstep by neglecting hudud and qisas, which are not subject to ta'zir discretion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal principle
Recognize that ta'zir punishment in Islam is delegated (mufawwad) to the ruler's opinion without specific quantification. This is the basis of flexibility.
The author emphasizes that this delegation does not contradict fixing punishments by law.
2
Understand that specification is included within delegation
The act of specifying punishments in a legal code (taqnin) is itself an exercise of the ruler's discretionary authority. It is not a violation of the flexible nature of ta'zir.
This is the key logical move: discretion includes the power to set rules.
3
Distinguish between ta'zir and fixed shar'i penalties
Hadd punishments and qisas are fixed by nass and are not subject to the ruler's discretion. Codification that neglects or violates these is not permissible.
The author notes that modern codes often fail to implement hudud and may conflict with qisas, which is a criticism.
4
Evaluate modern penal codes
Most provisions of modern penal codes fall within the scope of legitimate ta'zir. The setting of minimum and maximum penalties is a form of regulation consistent with ta'zir.
The author signals that reform should focus on restoring hadd and qisas, not on abolishing codification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨/٥٠ - تقنين العقوبات اليوم يدخل في نظام التعزير الإسلامي:
وإذا كانت عقوبة التعزير في الإسلام مفوّضة لآراء الحكام دون تقدير فإنَّ هذا لا ينافي ولا يمنع تقدير العقوبات وتحديدها بقانون لمختلف أنواع الجرائم، ضبطاً للأحكام، وجَزياً مع الدواعي الزمنية، كما يحصل في قوانين العقوبات في العصر الحاضر، لأنَّ هذا التحديد داخل في ذلك التفويض.
فقانون العقوبات لدينا اليوم تدخل معظم أحكامه في نطاق التعزير المشروع بالنظر الإسلامي، سوى ما فيه من إهمال للحدود، وإخلال بالقصاص في بعض مواطنه .
وإن وضع حدين أدنى وأعلى في قوانين الجزاء الحديثة لعقوبة كل
8/50 - Codification of punishments today falls within the Islamic ta'zir system: Although ta'zir punishment in Islam is delegated to the opinions of rulers without specification, this does not contradict nor prevent specifying and fixing punishments by law for various types of crimes, for the sake of regulating rulings and keeping pace with temporal motivations, as happens in modern penal codes, because this specification is included within that delegation. So our penal code today, most of its provisions fall within the scope of legitimate ta'zir from an Islamic perspective, except for what it contains of negligence of hadd punishments and violations of qisas in some instances. And the setting of minimum and maximum limits in modern penal codes for each punishment...
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This section addresses the compatibility of modern codified penal law with Islamic ta'zir. The author argues that because ta'zir is delegated to the ruler's discretion, setting fixed penalties by law is a valid exercise of that delegation. Legislating ranges (minimum and maximum) is a form of regulation that does not contradict the discretionary nature of ta'zir. However, the author notes that modern codes often neglect hadd punishments and violate qisas, which are not within the scope of ta'zir. Thus, while codified punishments are generally acceptable as ta'zir, they must not undermine fixed Shar'i penalties.

Key Terms
تقنين العقوبات (taqnin al-'uqubat): Codification of punishments: the process of setting fixed legal penalties for crimes in a statutory code.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the ruler or judge for crimes that do not have fixed hadd penalties.
حدود (hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by the Quran and Sunnah for specific crimes (e.g., theft, zina, etc.).
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice, typically for murder or bodily harm, allowing equal retaliation or blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of modern codified penal laws in relation to Islamic ta'zir?
A) They are completely outside Islamic law.
B) They are acceptable as part of the delegated ta'zir system, except where they neglect hadd and qisas.
C) They must be replaced by traditional uncodified ta'zir.
D) They are only valid if they include hadd punishments.

Key Takeaway
Modern codification of punishments is permissible under the ta'zir system, but must not compromise fixed hudud and qisas.

The Flexibility of Ta'zir and Its Role in Modern Legislation
Legal philosophy discussion on ta'zir flexibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view of ta'zir as a flexible penal system and apply it to justify modern codification of criminal penalties.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment delegated to rulers to serve public interest, adaptable to any time or place.

This passage explores the theory of discretionary punishments (ta'zir) in Islamic law, emphasizing its flexibility and role within siyasa shar'iyya. The author argues that ta'zir is not a fixed system but a broad delegation to authorities to determine punishments according to public interest, which makes it compatible with modern codified penal laws. Understanding this theory is crucial for a mufti when addressing contemporary criminal justice issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concept
Read the first paragraph to understand that ta'zir is defined as a general, flexible penal code not fixed in form or degree. It is delegated by Shari'ah to the rulers (wulat al-amr) to be used according to public interest (maslaha).
This is the foundational definition: ta'zir is discretionary, not hadd or qisas.
2
Link ta'zir to siyasa shar'iyya and istislah
The passage states that ta'zir falls within the scope of siyasa shar'iyya, which is defined as the ruler's actions for perceived benefit even without specific textual evidence. This is similar to istislah (public welfare). Note the quote from Radd al-Muhtar that 'the chapter of ta'zir is the guarantor of the rulings of siyasa shar'iyya.'
This shows ta'zir as the practical mechanism for implementing siyasa shar'iyya.
3
Apply ta'zir to modern codification
The author argues that modern codified penal laws, with their minimum and maximum penalties, are an exercise of the same delegated authority. By setting penalties in advance, the state fulfills the need for certainty while remaining within the ta'zir framework. This is not a departure from Islamic law but a valid application of its principles.
This step demonstrates the adaptability of ta'zir to contemporary legal systems.
4
Analyze the conclusion from Subhi Mahmasani
The passage ends with a quote emphasizing ta'zir as a just, flexible means for the judge to act according to the public interest, proving Islamic law's adaptability and refuting claims of rigidity.
This reinforces the main argument that Islamic penal law is dynamic.

فهذا دليل آخر على أن الشريعة الإسلامية ليست من الجمود بحيث رماها بعض الناس؛ بل هي على العكس قابلة للتطور بحسب المصلحة في كل عصر. وإنما التعزير وسيلة كغيرها من الوسائل تجيز للقاضي أن يتوسع في تطبيق الأحكام الجزائية حسب كل واقعة وحسب كل مجرم، للتوصل إلى الغاية المقصودة من كل عقوبة عامة وهي ردع المجرم وزجر أمثاله
عن تقليده .
وهذا بلا ريب باب واسع لاقتباس ما حسن من القوانين الجزائية العصرية إذا اقتضى الأمر، ولمجاراة تيار المدنية والحاجات الاجتماعية المتقلبة" اهـ.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Affirmative Means in Jurisprudence... (full translation from Arabic clean above)
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in Islamic law. The author explains that ta'zir is a general, flexible penal code not fixed in form or degree, delegated by Shari'ah to rulers for use according to public interest. It falls within siyasa shar'iyya, which is defined as actions taken by the ruler for perceived benefit even without specific textual evidence, akin to istislah. Quoting from Radd al-Muhtar and Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author affirms that ta'zir is the guarantor of siyasa shar'iyya. Modern codification of punishments, such as setting minimum and maximum penalties, is permissible within the Islamic ta'zir system because it is an exercise of the delegated authority—now done proactively before crimes occur, so offenders are aware of penalties. The author concludes with a quote from Dr. Subhi Mahmasani, emphasizing ta'zir as a just, flexible tool for the judge to adapt to circumstances, proving Islamic law's adaptability.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zīr): Discretionary punishment not prescribed by fixed texts, left to the judge's discretion to deter and reform.
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyāsa al-shar'iyya): Actions taken by the ruler for public benefit, even without specific textual evidence, as long as not contradicting Shari'ah.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Consideration of public welfare as a basis for legal rulings when no explicit text applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between ta'zir and siyasa shar'iyya?
A) Ta'zir is separate from siyasa shar'iyya.
B) Ta'zir is the guarantor of siyasa shar'iyya's rulings.
C) Siyasa shar'iyya limits the scope of ta'zir.
D) Ta'zir is a type of hadd punishment.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is a flexible, discretionary penal system that allows rulers to adapt punishments to changing times, including through modern codification, as long as public interest is served and fixed Shar'iah limits are respected.

Al-Ta'zir and the Fixed Principles of Shari'ah: A Hanafi Legal Reflection
Analysis of ta'zir as a flexible disciplinary sanction and defense of shari'ah's fixed principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view that ta'zir is a flexible discretionary punishment while shari'ah's core principles are eternal, and critically evaluate the argument that shari'ah evolves.

Core Idea
Shari'ah principles are fixed and perfect; what evolves is human understanding and application, not the law itself.

This passage discusses disciplinary sanctions (al-mu'ayyidat al-ta'dibiyya), focusing on ta'zir as a delegated punishment. The author first quotes Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani, who praises ta'zir as a flexible tool allowing Islamic law to evolve. The author then critiques this defense, arguing that it implicitly concedes stagnation in shari'ah. Instead, he asserts that shari'ah's foundational principles—like the command to judge with justice and the prohibition of harm—are eternal and do not require evolution. What changes is the means of application and juristic understanding. This lesson will help you distinguish between fixed usul and flexible furu' in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and distinguish Al-Mahmasani's view on ta'zir (flexible, evolving) from the author's critique (principles are fixed, only application changes).
Al-Mahmasani is quoted; the author responds with 'I say'.
2
Understand ta'zir as a delegated punishment
Note that ta'zir is left to the ruler's discretion within the framework of public interest. It is not a fixed hadd punishment.
Hanafi fiqh gives broad authority for ta'zir but with constraints.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author argues that Al-Mahmasani's defense is weak because it implies shari'ah has some stagnation or deficiency. Recognize the logical flaw: claiming that flexibility proves evolution concedes that the law needs improvement.
The author calls it a 'shubhat taslim' (apparent concession).
4
Recognize the fixed principles
The author cites Quran 4:58 and the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' as examples of eternal principles that cannot be improved upon.
These principles are perfect in their subject matter.
5
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between the fixed principle (asl) and its temporal application (tatbiq). Only the means of application and juristic understanding evolve.
This aligns with Hanafi usul: texts are qat'i, ijtihad is zanni.

ولكن الشرع الإسلامي جعل هذا التفويض أساساً بنطاق أوسع، وترك التحديد للسلطات الزمنية تتصرف في كل زمان ومكان بحسبهما. فيكون تحديد العقوبات في التقنين الجزائي إنما هو تنفيذ من أولياء الأمر للنظرية الإسلامية ،فيه واستعمال منهم لسلطتهم الشرعية في هذا التحديد ولكن بصورة مسبقة قبل وقوع الجرائم ليكون المجرم على بينة من نوع العقوبة ومقدارها قبل إقدامه ومن البديهي أنَّ هذا التسبيق في التحديد داخل في الصلاحيات والسلطة المفوضة شرعاً إلى أولياء الأمر في هذا الشأن.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
کتاب النظرية العامة للموجبات والعقود في الشريعة الإسلامية للأستاذ الدكتور صبحي المحمصاني ۱۳۱/۱)(۱).
أقول :
إننا مع تقديرنا لهذا الدفاع عن الشريعة الإسلامية من قبل الدكتور المحمصاني نراه دفاعاً ضعيفاً، لأن فيه - عن غير قصد منه - شبه تسليم بأن في الشريعة جموداً لكن ليس بالدرجة التي يزعمونها وكذلك القول بأنها قابلة للتطور فإنه شبه تسليم بالنقص في أصلها .
والواقع أن هذه الشريعة السمحة التي طبقت آفاق الأرض بنظامها القانوني الرائع قد استغنت عن التطور بأنها جاءت بمبادىء قانونية ذات قيم ثابتة خالدة في نصوص عامة مرنة كما أوضحناه في مناسبات سابقة .
فالذي يحتاج إلى التطور ليس هو الشريعة الإسلامية ذاتها، بل هو (أولاً) الإدراك العقلي والتجريبي لدى خصومها الجاهلين. (ثانياً) وسائل التطبيق الزمنية وفقه الفقهاء .
فقول الله سبحانه مثلاً : إِنَّ اللهَ يَأْمُرُكُمْ أَن تُؤَدُّوا الأمني إلى أهلها وإذا حكمتم بين النَّاسِ أَن تَحْكُمُوا بِالْعَدْلِ ) وكذا قول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: لا ضرر ولا بَيْنَ ضرار هما مبدآن تشریعیان خالدان لا يمكن أن يتطورا إلى أكمل منهما في موضوعهما، وإنما تتطور وسائل تطبيقهما والقضاء بهما.
Chapter (50): Disciplinary Sanctions: Delegated Punishments. Crime is the reliance of legislative authorities on the view of rulers, delegating to them to the extent that those authorities find no alternative. But Islamic law made this delegation a foundation on a broader scope, leaving the determination to temporal authorities to act according to each time and place. Thus, determining punishments in penal codification is an implementation by the rulers of the Islamic theory, and their use of their legal authority in this determination, but proactively before crimes occur so that the criminal is aware of the type and amount of punishment before proceeding. It is obvious that this prior determination falls within the discretionary powers and authority delegated legally to the rulers in this matter. 9/50 - This, and we conclude the research on disciplinary sanctions with a statement by Professor Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani, commenting on his presentation of ta'zir, saying: Principle: You see from that that ta'zir in Islamic law is a just and flexible means, giving the ruler room to act according to public interest that requires deterring and disciplining corrupters. This is another proof that Islamic law is not stagnant as some claim; rather, it is capable of evolving according to interest in every age. Ta'zir is a means like other means that allows the judge to expand in applying penal rulings according to each incident and each criminal, to achieve the intended goal of every general punishment: deterring the criminal and deterring his likes from imitating him. This is undoubtedly a wide door for adopting what is good from modern penal laws when necessary, and for keeping pace with civilization and changing social needs." End. Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh. Book: The General Theory of Obligations and Contracts in Islamic Law by Professor Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani (1/131). I say: While we appreciate this defense of Islamic law by Dr. al-Mahmasani, we see it as a weak defense, because it contains – unintentionally – an implicit admission that there is stagnation in the law, though not to the degree they claim, and likewise the statement that it is capable of evolution is an implicit admission of deficiency in its origin. The reality is that this tolerant law, which covered the horizons of the earth with its magnificent legal system, is independent of evolution because it came with legal principles of fixed, eternal values in flexible general texts, as we have explained on previous occasions. What needs development is not Islamic law itself, but (first) the intellectual and empirical understanding of its ignorant opponents, and (secondly) the temporal means of application and the fiqh of jurists. For example, Allah's saying: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners and when you judge between people to judge with justice,' and likewise the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm' – these are two eternal legislative principles that cannot evolve to something more perfect in their subject matter; rather, what evolve are the means of their application and adjudication by them.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage opens by discussing disciplinary sanctions (al-mu'ayyidat al-ta'dibiyya), specifically ta'zir, as delegated punishments where the legislature leaves determination to the ruler's discretion within a broad framework. The author then quotes Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani, who argues that ta'zir is a just, flexible tool allowing judges to adapt punishment to circumstances, proving that Islamic law is not stagnant but capable of evolution according to public interest. Al-Mahmasani sees this as a door to adopt modern penal laws. The author responds critically: while appreciating the defense, he considers it weak because it implicitly concedes that shari'ah has some stagnation or deficiency. He asserts that shari'ah came with eternal, fixed principles in flexible general texts—citing the Quranic verse on justice and the hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm'—and that what evolves is human understanding and application methods, not the law itself. This aligns with the Hanafi usul perspective that the foundational texts (nusus) are immutable, but ijtihad in their application adapts to changing circumstances. The mufti must distinguish between the fixed principle (asl) and its temporal implementation (tatbiq zamani).

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge or ruler for offenses that have no fixed hadd or kaffara, aimed at deterrence and reform.
المؤيدات التأديبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-ta'dibiyya): Disciplinary sanctions in Islamic law, including ta'zir, intended to deter and discipline offenders, distinct from fixed hudud.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): A foundational principle meaning that no harm is to be inflicted or reciprocated in Islam, used here as an example of an eternal principle.
المبادئ الثابتة الخالدة (al-mabadi' al-thabita al-khalida): Fixed, eternal principles of shari'ah, such as the commands to deliver trusts and judge with justice, which are perfect and do not evolve.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following does the author consider an eternal principle of shari'ah that does not need evolution?
A) Ta'zir punishments
B) The hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm'
C) The means of judicial application
D) The fiqh of jurists

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is a flexible tool, but shari'ah's core principles are fixed; evolution occurs only in understanding and application.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Sharia's Completeness and the Evolution of Application
Critical refinement and transitional summary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate arguments about sharia's flexibility and apply the principle that only human understanding and means of application evolve, not the immutable principles themselves.

Core Idea
Sharia is perfect and unchanging; only human understanding and application evolve.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa offers a sharp critique of a modern defense of Islamic law. He argues that claiming sharia is 'capable of evolution' actually implies deficiency. Instead, he affirms that sharia's foundational principles are eternal and complete, and that any need for evolution lies in our understanding and application. This lesson examines that argument and its implications for the mufti's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the critique
Read the author's criticism of Dr. Mahmassani's defense. What specific concession does the author find problematic?
The author says the defense 'unintentionally admits rigidity' and concedes deficiency.
2
State the author's own position
What does al-Zarqa assert about sharia's nature? Use his examples of the Quranic verse and hadith to illustrate.
He says sharia is 'tolerant', 'magnificent', and its principles are 'fixed and eternal in flexible texts'.
3
Distinguish between sharia and its application
How does the author separate the immutable sharia from the evolving elements? Identify the three things he says need evolution.
First: intellectual/empirical understanding of opponents. Second: means of application. Third: fiqh of jurists.
4
Recognize the transition
Note how the author concludes the topic of disciplinary supports and introduces civil supports. What are the four civil supports listed?
Nullity, corruption, suspension, and option.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
كِتَابُ النَّظَرِيَّةِ الْعَامَّةِ لِلْمُوجِبَاتِ وَالْعُقُودِ فِي الشَّرِيعَةِ الْإِسْلَامِيَّةِ لِلْأُسْتَاذِ الدُّكْتُورِ صُبْحِي الْمَحْمَصَانِي ١٣١/١)(١).
أَقُولُ :
إِنَّنَا مَعَ تَقْدِيرِنَا لِهَذَا الدِّفَاعِ عَنِ الشَّرِيعَةِ الْإِسْلَامِيَّةِ مِنْ قِبَلِ الدُّكْتُورِ الْمَحْمَصَانِي نَرَاهُ دِفَاعاً ضَعِيفاً، لِأَنَّ فِيهِ - عَنْ غَيْرِ قَصْدٍ مِنْهُ - شِبْهَ تَسْلِيمٍ بِأَنَّ فِي الشَّرِيعَةِ جُمُوداً لَكِنْ لَيْسَ بِالدَّرَجَةِ الَّتِي يَزْعُمُونَهَا وَكَذَلِكَ الْقَوْلُ بِأَنَّهَا قَابِلَةٌ لِلتَّطَوُّرِ فَإِنَّهُ شِبْهُ تَسْلِيمٍ بِالنَّقْصِ فِي أَصْلِهَا .
وَالْوَاقِعُ أَنَّ هَذِهِ الشَّرِيعَةَ السَّمْحَةَ الَّتِي طَبَّقَتْ آفَاقَ الْأَرْضِ بِنِظَامِهَا الْقَانُونِيِّ الرَّائِعِ قَدِ اسْتَغْنَتْ عَنِ التَّطَوُّرِ بِأَنَّهَا جَاءَتْ بِمَبَادِىءَ قَانُونِيَّةٍ ذَاتِ قِيَمٍ ثَابِتَةٍ خَالِدَةٍ فِي نُصُوصٍ عَامَّةٍ مَرِنَةٍ كَمَا أَوْضَحْنَاهُ فِي مُنَاسَبَاتٍ سَابِقَةٍ .
فَالَّذِي يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى التَّطَوُّرِ لَيْسَ هُوَ الشَّرِيعَةُ الْإِسْلَامِيَّةُ ذَاتُهَا، بَلْ هُوَ (أَوَّلاً) الْإِدْرَاكُ الْعَقْلِيُّ وَالتَّجْرِيبِيُّ لَدَى خُصُومِهَا الْجَاهِلِينَ. (ثَانِياً) وَسَائِلُ التَّطْبِيقِ الزَّمَنِيَّةُ وَفِقْهُ الْفُقَهَاءِ .
فَقَوْلُ اللهِ سُبْحَانَهُ مَثَلاً : إِنَّ اللهَ يَأْمُرُكُمْ أَن تُؤَدُّوا الْأَمَانَاتِ إِلَى أَهْلِهَا وَإِذَا حَكَمْتُم بَيْنَ النَّاسِ أَن تَحْكُمُوا بِالْعَدْلِ ) وَكَذَا قَوْلُ الرَّسُولِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: لاَ ضَرَرَ وَلاَ ضِرَارَ هُمَا مَبْدَآنِ تَشْرِيعِيَّانِ خَالِدَانِ لَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَتَطَوَّرَا إِلَى أَكْمَلَ مِنْهُمَا فِي مَوْضُوعِهِمَا، وَإِنَّمَا تَتَطَوَّرُ وَسَائِلُ تَطْبِيقِهِمَا وَالْقَضَاءِ بِهِمَا.
بِمَا تَقَدَّمَ نَخْتَتِمُ الْكَلَامَ عَنِ الْمُؤَيِّدَاتِ التَّأْدِيبِيَّةِ، وَنَنْتَقِلُ فِيمَا يَلِي إِلَى الْمُؤَيِّدَاتِ الْمَدَنِيَّةِ (وَهِيَ : الْبُطْلَانُ وَالْفَسَادُ وَالتَّوَقُّفُ وَالتَّخْيِيرُ) فِي الْعُقُودِ.
وَقَدْ رَأَيْنَا أَنَّ التَّوَقُّفَ فِي أَحْكَامِ الْعَقْدِ هُوَ مُؤَيِّدٌ يُقْصَدُ بِهِ - غَالِباً - حِمَايَةُ حَقٍّ لِشَخْصٍ عِنْدَمَا يَمَسُّهُ تَصَرُّفٌ صَادِرٌ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ دُونَ نِيَابَةٍ عَنْهُ، وَأَنَّ التَّخْيِيرَ هُوَ أَيْضاً مُؤَيِّدٌ يَمْنَحُ الشَّرْعُ بِمُقْتَضَاهُ أَحَدَ الْمُتَعَاقِدَيْنِ خِيَاراً لِضَمَانِ التَّوَازُنِ الْإِرَادِيِّ عِنْدَمَا يَخْتَلُّ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ بِسَبَبِ طُغْيَانِ مَصْلَحَةِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى مَصْلَحَةِ الْآخَرِ دُونَ تَحَقُّقِ رِضَاهُ (ر: ف ٧/٤٨).
وَبِمَا أَنَّ التَّوَقُّفَ وَالتَّخْيِيرَ قَدْ سَلَفَ بَيَانُهُمَا مُفَصَّلاً فِي نَظَرِيَّةِ الْعُقُودِ عِنْدَ الْبَحْثِ فِي شَوَائِبِ الْإِرَادَةِ وَفِي الْآثَارِ الْعَامَّةِ لِلْعُقُودِ فِي نَظَرِيَّتِي عَدَمِ النَّفَاذِ، وَعَدَمِ اللُّزُومِ، لِذَلِكَ نَكْتَفِي هُنَا بِهَذِهِ الْإِشَارَةِ إِلَى مَوْقِعِهِمَا مِنْ نَظَرِيَّةِ الْمُؤَيِّدَاتِ، اجْتِزَاءً بِمَا تَقَدَّمَ عَنْهُمَا، وَنُحِيلُ الْقَارِىءَ عَلَيْهِ.
وَسَيَقْتَصِرُ كَلَامُنَا فِي فُصُولِ الْبُحُوثِ التَّالِيَةِ عَلَى الْبُطْلَانِ فِي الْفُصُولِ ٥١ - ٥٣/ ثُمَّ عَلَى الْفَسَادِ فِي الْفُصُولِ / ٥٤ - ٥٧ ثُمَّ نَخْتِمُ بَابَ الْمُؤَيِّدَاتِ بِالْفَصْلِ ۵۸/ الَّذِي نُقَارِنُ فِيهِ نَظَرِيَّةَ الْبُطْلَانِ فِي الْفِقْهِ الْأَجْنَبِيِّ بِنَظَرِيَّتِي الْبُطْلَانِ وَالْفَسَادِ فِي الْفِقْهِ الْإِسْلَامِيِّ.
(١) انظر في موضوع المؤيدات التأديبية محاضرتنا عن الحق العام في الإسلام المنشورة في الجريدة الحقوقية التي تصدر بحلب (العدد / ٢٥ السنة (٢٠) وفي مجلة معهد الحقوق السوري بدمشق (العدد/ ۳ السنة الأولى).
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Supports in Fiqh. From the book 'The General Theory of Obligations and Contracts in Islamic Law' by Professor Dr. Subhi Mahmassani (1/131)(1). I say: While we appreciate this defense of Islamic law by Dr. Mahmassani, we see it as a weak defense, because it contains - unintentionally on his part - a semblance of admission that there is rigidity in the law, but not to the degree they claim; and likewise the statement that it is subject to evolution is a semblance of admission of deficiency in its origin. The reality is that this tolerant law, which has covered the horizons of the earth with its magnificent legal system, has dispensed with evolution because it came with legal principles of fixed and eternal values in flexible general texts, as we have explained on previous occasions. What needs evolution is not the Islamic law itself, but (first) the intellectual and empirical understanding of its ignorant opponents; (second) the temporal means of application and the fiqh of jurists. For example, Allah's saying: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to whom they are due and when you judge between people to judge with justice,' and likewise the Prophet's saying: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm' - both are two eternal legislative principles that cannot evolve to something more perfect than them in their subject matter; rather, the means of their application and adjudication evolve. With the above, we conclude the discussion on disciplinary supports, and move in what follows to civil supports (which are: nullity, corruption, suspension, and option) in contracts. We have seen that suspension in the rulings of a contract is a support intended - mostly - to protect a right of a person when an action from another without representation affects him, and that option is also a support whereby the law grants one of the two contracting parties a choice to ensure volitional balance when it is disturbed between the two parties due to the predominance of one's interest over the other's without his consent (see F. 7/48). Since suspension and option have already been explained in detail in the theory of contracts when researching defects of will and in the general effects of contracts in the theories of non-enforceability and non-bindingness, therefore we suffice here with this indication of their position in the theory of supports, contenting ourselves with what has preceded about them, and refer the reader to it. Our discussion in the following chapters will be limited to nullity in chapters 51-53, then to corruption in chapters 54-57, then we conclude the chapter on supports with chapter 58, in which we compare the theory of nullity in foreign jurisprudence with the theories of nullity and corruption in Islamic jurisprudence. (1) See on the subject of disciplinary supports our lecture on public right in Islam published in the legal journal issued in Aleppo (issue 25, year 20) and in the Journal of the Syrian Institute of Law in Damascus (issue 3, first year).
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage is a transitional and philosophical commentary by the author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, within his work 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' (General Introduction to Fiqh). He begins by critiquing a defense of Islamic law by Dr. Subhi Mahmassani. The author argues that Mahmassani's defense is weak because it implicitly concedes that Islamic law is rigid and in need of evolution. Al-Zarqa insists that Islamic law is inherently perfect and unchanging; it contains eternal principles expressed in flexible texts that do not require evolution. What needs development is the understanding and application of these principles by people. He cites Quranic and hadith examples to illustrate fixed principles. Then he concludes the discussion on disciplinary supports (military or penal supports) and transitions to civil supports in contracts: nullity (bạtạlān), corruption (fasād), suspension (tawaqquf), and option (takhyīr). He notes that suspension and option have been detailed earlier, so he only mentions their roles here. Finally, he outlines the upcoming chapters on nullity, corruption, and a comparative study of nullity in foreign and Islamic law. The author's style is critical and defensive, emphasizing the sufficiency and timelessness of sharia.

Key Terms
المؤيدات التأديبية (al-mu'ayyidāt al-ta'dībiyya): Legal supports that are disciplinary in nature, likely penal or punitive measures to enforce law.
المؤيدات المدنية (al-mu'ayyidāt al-madaniyya): Civil legal supports in contracts: nullity (bạtạlān), corruption (fasād), suspension (tawaqquf), and option (takhyīr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the subject of evolution in relation to Islamic law?
A) The principles of Islamic law themselves
B) The intellectual and empirical understanding of opponents and means of application
C) The textual expressions of the Quran and Hadith
D) The eternal values of sharia

Key Takeaway
The mufti must uphold the completeness of sharia; any evolution is only in human understanding and application, not in the divine principles.

التخيير as a Disciplinary Sanction: Definition and Function
Defining التخيير as a protective right and disciplinary sanction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of التخيير as a legal sanction and explain its role in restoring volitional balance in contracts.

Core Idea
التخيير restores volitional balance when one party's interest dominates without proper consent.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa defines التخيير (option/choice) as a legal protection and a disciplinary sanction granted by the Lawgiver to correct imbalance in contractual consent. This sets the stage for his detailed analysis of nullity (butlan) and irregularity (fasad) in subsequent chapters. For a mufti, understanding this concept is essential for applying the khiyarat (options) in contemporary contract disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term defined
Read the first sentence and locate the term التخيير. Note that it is defined as both a protective right and a disciplinary sanction.
The term is singular, indicating a general concept.
2
Analyze the dual nature
Deconstruct the definition: التخيير is (1) protection of a right when an act from another affects you without agency, and (2) a sanction giving one party a choice to restore volitional balance.
The sanction operates specifically when one party's interest overwhelms the other's without consent.
3
Connect to prior discussions
The author refers to earlier treatment of suspension and option in the theory of defects of will and non-enforceability. Understand that التخيير is a remedy for defective consent.
This links to khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Place within the theory of sanctions
Recognize that التخيير is part of a broader set of 'disciplinary sanctions' (al-mu'ayyidat al-ta'dibiyyah) that protect contractual justice.
The upcoming chapters on butlan and fasad are also sanctions; التخيير is a milder corrective.
5
Summarize the author's plan
Note that the author will not repeat the earlier discussion but will proceed to nullity (ch. 51-53), irregularity (ch. 54-57), and a comparison with foreign law (ch. 58).
This frames the current passage as a transitional summary.

التخيير هو حماية حق لشخص عندما يمسه تصرف صادر من غيره دون نيابة عنه، وأنَّ أيضاً مؤيد يمنح الشرع بمقتضاه أحد العاقدين خياراً لضمان التوازن الإرادي عندما يختل بين الطرفين بسبب طغيان مصلحة أحدهما على مصلحة الآخر دون تحقق رضاه (ر: ف ٧/٤٨). وبما أن التوقف والتخيير قد سَلَف بيانهما مفصلاً في نظرية العقود عند البحث في شوائب الإرادة وفي الآثار العامة للعقود في نظريتي عدم النفاذ، وعدم اللزوم، لذلك نكتفي هنا بهذه الإشارة إلى موقعهما من نظرية المؤيدات، اجتزاء بما تقدم عنهما، ونحيل القارىء عليه. وسيقتصر كلامنا في فصول البحوث التالية على البطلان في الفصول ٥١ - ٥٣/ ثم على الفساد في الفصول / ٥٤ - ٥٧ ثم نختم باب المؤيدات بالفصل ۵۸/ الذي نقارن فيه نظرية البطلان في الفقه الأجنبي بنظريتي البطلان والفساد في الفقه الإسلامي.
The option (al-takhyir) is: protection of a person's right when an act from another affects him without representation, and also a sanction by which the Lawgiver grants one of the contracting parties a choice to ensure volitional balance when it is disturbed between the two parties due to the dominance of one's interest over the other's without his consent being realized (ref: F 7/48). And since suspension (al-tawaqquf) and option have already been explained in detail in the theory of contracts when discussing defects of will and the general effects of contracts in the theories of non-enforceability and non-bindingness, we suffice here with this indication of their place in the theory of sanctions, relying on what preceded and referring the reader to it. Our discussion in the following chapters will be limited to nullity (al-butlan) in chapters 51-53, then to irregularity (al-fasad) in chapters 54-57, then we conclude the section on sanctions with chapter 58, in which we compare the theory of nullity in foreign jurisprudence with our theories of nullity and irregularity in Islamic jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage defines التخيير (option/choice) as a protective legal right belonging to a person when an act from another affects him without the latter being an agent. It is also a disciplinary sanction (mu'ayyid) that the Lawgiver grants to one contracting party to restore volitional balance when it is disturbed due to one party's interest overwhelming the other without proper consent. The author refers to earlier discussions on suspension and option in contract theory and outlines the upcoming chapters on nullity, irregularity, and a comparison with foreign law.

Key Terms
التخيير (al-takhyir): A legal right or sanction granting a contracting party a choice to correct volitional imbalance when consent is defective.
المؤيدات التأديبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-ta'dibiyyah): Disciplinary sanctions that protect rights and ensure balance in legal transactions.

Definition of Nullity (al-Butlan) Linguistically and Technically
Introducing the theory of nullity with linguistic and technical definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definition of nullity (al-butlan) and identify its role as a civil sanction in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Nullity means the act is legally non-existent — it falls away due to corruption.

Before a mufti can analyze the validity of a transaction, he must first grasp what nullity means. This lesson traces the Arabic root of al-butlan and locates it within the four civil sanctions that uphold the legal order.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic root
Trace the Arabic root b-t-l and its connotations of falling away, loss, and corruption. Understand how the term is used in pre-Islamic poetry and the Qur'an.
The root implies instability and lack of persistence.
2
Grasp the technical definition
Understand nullity as a legal sanction: a transaction that is void ab initio, having no legal existence and producing no effects.
This definition is foundational for all subsequent discussions on contract validity.
3
Situate within the four sanctions
Identify nullity alongside irregularity, suspension, and option as one of the four civil sanctions that regulate transactions.
In Hanafi fiqh, nullity is distinct from irregularity (fasād), a distinction introduced later.
4
Connect to Hanafi fiqh
Appreciate that nullity applies when a pillar (rukn) is missing, while irregularity applies when a condition (shart) is defective.
This distinction is critical for issuing accurate fatwas.

Hence the saying of Ka'b ibn Zuhayr: 'And whoever calls people to his blame, they blame him with truth and falsehood.' Definition of Nullity (Buṭlān) Linguistically and Terminologically
Defining nullity linguistically and terminologically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of buṭlān and apply the definition to determine whether an act is void or not.

Core Idea
Nullity means the act is legally non-existent—it produces no effects because it lacks an essential requirement.

The term 'nullity' (buṭlān) is foundational to understanding when an act in Islamic law fails to produce its intended consequences. In this lesson, you will trace its meaning from linguistic roots to its precise technical definition in the Hanafi school, as explained by Mustafa al-Zarqa. This clarity is essential for a mufti when classifying transactions and acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic origin
Examine the Arabic root b-t-l and its meanings of falling away, corruption, and falsehood. Note how the term bāṭil is used for anything without stable foundation.
The root also gives batal (brave man) because he risks nullity.
2
Recognize Quranic usage
Observe how the Qur'an uses bāṭil to denote falsehood and invalidity in moral and religious contexts, e.g., consuming wealth unjustly or invalidating good deeds.
Quran verses: al-A'raf 7:139 and al-Baqarah 2:264.
3
Distinguish technical definition
Understand that jurists repurposed the term for a specific legal meaning: an act that the Lawgiver does not consider, so it is like non-existence, producing no legal effects.
The author explicitly states 'waqtana idha kana dhālika al-‘amal ghayru mu‘tabar fī nazar al-shāri‘'.
4
Apply to examples
Consider prayer and fasting: if invalid, they do not discharge the obligation; the same applies to contracts like sale and marriage—no rights or obligations arise.
The passage uses 'bāṭilat al-ṣalāt wa al-ṣiyām' as examples.
5
Identify the cause
Nullity arises from missing a pillar (rukn) or a condition (sharṭ)—elements mandated by the Sharī‘a. The act is regarded as non-existent due to violation of a requirement.
This cause is implied in the definition; the passage does not yet detail rukn vs. sharṭ.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
﴿إِنَّ هَؤُلَاءِ مُتَبر (1) مَا هُمْ فِيهِ وَنَطِل مَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأعراف 7/ 139] ومنه تعبير أكل المال بالباطل أي بغير حق.
وإبطال الشيء إفساده وإزالته حقاً كان الشيء في ذاته أو باطلاً. (ر. المصباح، ومفردات القرآن للراغب الأصفهاني).
- ثم جاء القرآن باستعمال البطلان في معنى ديني اعتقادي محض أن لا يترتب على العمل الحسن في ذاته ثوابه الموعود في الآخرة، لما يصاحبه من سوء. وعلى هذا قوله تعالى :
﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تُبْطِلُوا صَدَقَاتِكُم بِالْمَنِّ وَالْأَذَى ﴾ [سورة البقرة 2/264].
- ثم لما بدأت تتكون لغة الفقه الإسلامي واصطلاحاته، أخذ البطلان في لسان الفقهاء معنى عملياً تشريعياً جديداً، فاستعملوه بمعنى عدم صحة العمل ذي الأثر الشرعي عندما يكون ذلك العمل غير معتبر في نظر الشارع، بحيث يعد وجوده كعدمه لمخالفة ناحية يوجب الشارع مراعاتها فيه فلا يترتب عليه أثره الشرعي الخاص من نشوء حق أو سقوط تكليف. ويجري ذلك في العبادات الدينية والأعمال والمعاملات المدنية على السواء فبطلان الصلاة والصيام مثلاً هو عدم اعتبار صحة لهما فلا يبرئان ذمة المكلف بهما عن الواجب، بل يبقى مكلفاً بإعادتهما.
·
وبطلان التصرف الشرعي من بيع وشراء وزواج وطلاق، وإقرار وإبراء، وأخذ وعطاء، وسائر المعاملات المدنية، هو عدم حصول آثارها المقررة لها شرعاً بين الناس من امتلاك وانتفاع واستباحة واستمتاع وسائر الحقوق والثمرات والمصالح التي جعل ذلك التصرف سبيلاً إليها وسيباً منشئاً لها.
(1) متبر (بفتح الباء الموحدة المشددة صيغة مفعول من التتبير بمعنى الإهلاك والتبار كالهلاك وزناً ومعنى.
702
Chapter Fifty-One: The Theory of Nullity. 1- Definition of nullity linguistically and terminologically. 2- Actual existence and legal existence. 3- Application of that to verbal and physical acts. 4- Determining the cause of nullity. 5- Divisibility of nullity. 6- The difference between nullity of a contract and its dissolution. 

Introduction about the civil invalidators: nullity and irregularity.
1/51 - We have explained previously at the conclusion of Chapter 48 that the civil invalidators of the system of transactions appear in legislation in four forms: nullity, irregularity, suspension, and option.

First: Definition of nullity linguistically and terminologically.
2/51 - Nullity in the original language: The falling away of a thing due to its corruption. It is said: the blood of the slain is null (batala) if it goes to waste without retaliation or blood money, and from this the brave man is called batal (with two fatha), because he exposes his blood or the blood of his opponent to nullity. - And al-bāṭil is that which has no stability when examined, and from this the opposite of truth is called 'bāṭil'. Upon this is the saying of Ka'b ibn Zuhayr: 'Whoever calls people to his blame, they blame him with truth and with falsehood.' And upon this meaning occurs what is in the Qur'an, such as the saying of the Most High about the idol worshippers: 'Indeed, these people are ruined (mutabbar) in what they are in, and null is what they used to do' [Surah al-A'raf 7:139]. And from it is the expression 'consuming wealth by falsehood (bāṭil)' i.e., without right. And invalidating a thing (ibṭāl) means corrupting and removing it, whether the thing is in itself right or false. (See al-Misbah and Mufradāt al-Qur'ān by al-Rāghib al-Isfahānī).
- Then the Qur'an came using nullity in a purely religious, doctrinal meaning: that no promised reward in the afterlife results from a good deed due to accompanying evil. Upon this is the saying of the Most High: 'O you who have believed, do not invalidate your charities with reproach and injury' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:264].
- Then when the language and terminology of Islamic jurisprudence began to develop, nullity took on a new practical legislative meaning in the parlance of jurists. They used it to mean the invalidity of an act that has a legal effect when that act is not considered by the Lawgiver, such that its existence is regarded as non-existence due to its violation of a requirement that the Lawgiver obligates to be observed in it, so it does not produce its specific legal effect of establishing a right or dropping an obligation. This applies equally to religious acts of worship and civil transactions. For example, nullity of prayer and fasting means that they are not considered valid, so they do not discharge the liability of the obliged person from the duty; rather, he remains obliged to repeat them.
·
And nullity of a shar‘ī disposition, such as sale, purchase, marriage, divorce, acknowledgment, acquittal, taking, giving, and all other civil transactions, is the non-occurrence of their effects established by law among people, such as ownership, usufruct, permissibility, enjoyment, and all other rights, fruits, and interests for which that disposition was made a means and a causative factor.
(1) 'Mutabbar' (with a shaddah on the bā') is a passive participle from 'tatbīr' meaning destruction, and 'tabār' is like 'halāk' in form and meaning.
702
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the theory of nullity (buṭlān) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by outlining the topics to be covered: definition of nullity, actual vs. legal existence, application to verbal and physical acts, cause of nullity, divisibility, and difference between nullity and dissolution. He then provides a preface on the four types of civil invalidators: nullity (buṭlān), irregularity (fasād), suspension (tawaqquf), and option (takhyīr).

The main focus is the definition of buṭlān. Linguistically, it means the falling away of something due to corruption, and the term bāṭil (falsehood) refers to anything without real foundation. The Qur'an uses bāṭil to mean falsehood or invalidity in a moral sense, e.g., consuming wealth unjustly or invalidating good deeds through reproach.

Technically, in Islamic jurisprudence, buṭlān takes on a precise legal meaning: it describes an act that has no legal effect because it lacks an essential requirement (rukn or sharṭ) mandated by the Lawgiver. Such an act is considered legally non-existent; it does not produce its intended consequences, e.g., a void prayer does not fulfill the obligation, and a void sale does not transfer ownership. This applies to all areas of fiqh, including worship and transactions.

The distinction between buṭlān (void) and fasād (irregular) is a hallmark of the Hanafi school, though this passage only introduces buṭlān. Later sections will elaborate on the differences.

Key Terms
البطلان (al-buṭlān): The invalidity of an act such that it is legally considered non-existent and produces no effects.
الباطل (al-bāṭil): Anything that has no stable foundation; the opposite of truth (ḥaqq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the technical meaning of buṭlān in Islamic jurisprudence?
A) The act is sinful and prohibited by the Lawgiver.
B) The act is considered legally non-existent and produces no effects.
C) The act must be repeated but still has some effect.
D) The act is valid but requires additional conditions.

Key Takeaway
Nullity is the legal non-existence of an act due to missing an essential element, resulting in no legal effects.

The Concept of Invalidity (Batal) and Its Two Aspects of Existence
Defining invalidity and the two modes of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define invalidity (batal) in Hanafi legal theory and distinguish between sensory existence and legal existence of a legal act.

Core Idea
Invalidity is the absence of legal recognition by the Lawgiver, even though the act occurred physically.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of invalidity (batal) in Islamic legal theory as developed by classical Hanafi jurists and explained by Mustafa al-Zarqa. You will learn how the term evolved from a general meaning to a precise legal term, and understand the critical distinction between the physical occurrence of an act and its legal validity. This distinction is essential for analyzing whether a legal act produces its intended effects or is considered void.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic and Quranic origins
Start by noting how the word 'batal' is used in Arabic language and in the Qur'an, where it often means something being nullified or wasted. In the verse about charity, it refers to losing reward due to accompanying evil.
This shows the term's shift from general usage to technical legal meaning.
2
Understand the shift to a legal meaning
In fiqh, 'batal' took on a practical meaning: an act that does not meet the Lawgiver's requirements, so it is considered legally ineffective despite physically occurring.
Key point: the act exists physically but is not recognized by the Lawgiver.
3
Identify the two aspects of existence
Every human action has two aspects: sensory existence (wujud hissi) – the physical movement and material form; and legal existence (wujud i'tibari) – the Lawgiver's attribution of suitability for legal effects.
Legal existence is what gives the act its normative force.
4
Define invalidity
Invalidity is the failure of a disposition to acquire its legal existence and effects in the view of the Lawgiver, even though its sensory form exists. It is the absence of that legal recognition.
This definition applies to both worship and transactions.
5
Apply the definition to examples
Consider prayer performed incorrectly: it has sensory existence (the body moves), but if a required condition is missing, it lacks legal existence and does not fulfil the obligation. Similarly, a sale with a prohibited condition physically occurs but does not transfer ownership.
This illustrates how invalidity renders the act like a lifeless form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
﴿إِنَّ هَؤُلَاءِ مُتَبر (١) مَا هُمْ فِيهِ وَنَطِل مَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأعراف ٧/ ١٣٩] ومنه تعبير أكل المال بالباطل أي بغير حق.
وإبطال الشيء إفساده وإزالته حقاً كان الشيء في ذاته أو باطلاً. (ر. المصباح، ومفردات القرآن للراغب الأصفهاني).
- ثم جاء القرآن باستعمال البطلان في معنى ديني اعتقادي محض أن لا يترتب على العمل الحسن في ذاته ثوابه الموعود في الآخرة، لما يصاحبه من سوء. وعلى هذا قوله تعالى : ﴿ يايها الذينَ آمَنُوا لَا تُبْطِلُواْ صَدَقَلتِكُم بِالْمَنِ وَالْأَذَى ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢٦٤/٢].
- ثم لما بدأت تتكون لغة الفقه الإسلامي واصطلاحاته، أخذ البطلان في لسان الفقهاء معنى عملياً تشريعياً جديداً، فاستعملوه بمعنى عدم صحة العمل ذي الأثر الشرعي عندما يكون ذلك العمل غير معتبر في نظر الشارع، بحيث يعد وجوده كعدمه لمخالفة ناحية يوجب الشارع مراعاتها فيه فلا يترتب عليه أثره الشرعي الخاص من نشوء حق أو سقوط تكليف. ويجري ذلك في العبادات الدينية والأعمال والمعاملات المدنية على السواء فبطلان الصلاة والصيام مثلاً هو عدم اعتبار صحة لهما فلا يبرئان ذمة المكلف بهما عن الواجب، بل يبقى مكلفاً بإعادتهما.
وبطلان التصرف الشرعي من بيع وشراء وزواج وطلاق، وإقرار وإبراء، وأخذ وعطاء، وسائر المعاملات المدنية، هو عدم حصول آثارها المقررة لها شرعاً بين الناس من امتلاك وانتفاع واستباحة واستمتاع وسائر الحقوق والثمرات والمصالح التي جعل ذلك التصرف سبيلاً إليها وسيباً منشئاً لها.
هذا محصل نظر الفقهاء وكلامهم في ذلك: (ر: «الموافقات» للشاطبي ٢٩٢/١ - ٢٩٦).
وهذا هو البطلان المطلق نفسه بالمعنى القانوني في الاصطلاح الحقوقي (ر ): الموجز في «الالتزامات للأستاذ السنهوري (ف/ ١٦٤).
ثانياً: التعريف الاصطلاحي الذي نختاره للبطلان
٣/٥١ - الوجود الحسي والوجود الاعتباري:
إن كل تصرف من الإنسان له ناحيتان في الوجود
وجود حسي، وهو وقوعه بحركته وصورته المادية.
ووجود اعتباري وهو أن يعتبر الشارع له بعد وقوعه صفة الصلوح لأن تترتب عليه أحكامه وهي آثاره المقررة له شرعاً.
ومن الواضح أن كل ما كان له صفة اعتبارية متوقفة عليها أحكامه فإن عدم اعتباره يجعل وجوده كعدمه فهو كصورة جامدة لا فيها ولا تأثير روح لها، وإنما الاعتبار الشرعي هو الذي ينفخ فيها الروح، ويهبها الحياة والفعالية.
وهذا نظر عام يصدق على جميع التصرفات المدنية من قولية وفعلية،
أي إن البطلان بمعناه المدني يلحق الأفعال كما يلحق الأقوال. وعلى ذلك يمكننا تعريف البطلان في الاصطلاح الفقهي بأنه :
عدم اكتساب التصرف وجوده الاعتباري وآثاره في نظر الشارع أي ان البطلان هو عدم ذلك الوجود الاعتباري للتصرف وإن وجدت صورته الحسية.
ثالثاً: تطبيق ذلك على التصرفات القولية والفعلية
٤/٥١ - أ - بطلان التصرفات القولية : إن التصرفات القولية تكون عقدية وغير عقدية كما تقدم ف ١/٢٧).
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Invalidity in Fiqh. [Quran 7:139] And from this is the expression 'consuming wealth unjustly' meaning without right. And invalidating something is corrupting it and removing it, whether the thing is just in itself or false. Then the Quran used 'invalidity' in a purely religious, doctrinal sense: that no promised reward in the Hereafter follows a good deed due to accompanying evil. Upon this is His saying: 'O you who believe, do not invalidate your charities by reproach and injury' [Quran 2:264]. Then when the language and terminology of Islamic fiqh began to form, 'invalidity' in the speech of jurists took on a new practical, legal meaning. They used it to mean the lack of validity of an action that has a legal effect, when that action is not considered by the Lawgiver, such that its existence is like non-existence due to violating an aspect required by the Lawgiver to be observed in it. Consequently, its specific legal effect does not follow, whether of establishing a right or dropping an obligation. This applies equally to religious acts of worship and civil transactions. For example, invalidity of prayer and fasting is that they are not considered valid, so they do not discharge the obligated person's liability; rather, he remains obliged to repeat them. And invalidity of a legal disposition such as sale, purchase, marriage, divorce, acknowledgment, acquittal, taking, giving, and all civil transactions, is the non-occurrence of their prescribed legal effects among people, such as ownership, usufruct, permissibility, enjoyment, and all rights, fruits, and interests for which that disposition was made a means and a generating cause. This is the summary of the jurists' view and statements on that. And this is absolute invalidity itself in the legal sense according to legal terminology. Secondly: The terminological definition we choose for invalidity. 3/51 - Sensory existence and legal existence: Indeed, every human action has two aspects of existence: sensory existence – its occurrence through movement and material form; and legal existence – that the Lawgiver, after its occurrence, attributes to it the quality of suitability for its rulings to follow, i.e., its prescribed legal effects. It is obvious that anything that has a legal attribute on which its rulings depend, when that attribute is not present, its existence becomes like non-existence. It is like a lifeless form with no spirit or effect. Rather, legal recognition is what breathes spirit into it and grants it life and effectiveness. This is a general view that applies to all civil dispositions, verbal and physical. That is, invalidity in its civil sense attaches to acts as it attaches to words. Based on this, we can define invalidity in juristic terminology as: the disposition's failure to acquire its legal existence and effects in the view of the Lawgiver. In other words, invalidity is the absence of that legal existence for the disposition, even if its sensory form exists. Thirdly: Application to verbal and physical dispositions. 4/51 - A - Invalidity of verbal dispositions: Verbal dispositions are either contractual or non-contractual, as previously mentioned in 1/27.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm introduces the foundational concept of invalidity (batal) in Islamic legal theory. The author begins by tracing the linguistic and Quranic usage of the term, showing its evolution from a general meaning of something being nullified or wasted to a specialized legal term. In fiqh, invalidity means that a legal act – whether an act of worship like prayer or fasting, or a civil transaction like sale or marriage – is not recognized by the Lawgiver as valid, so it produces no legal effects. The key distinction is between sensory existence (wujud hissi) and legal existence (wujud i'tibari). Every human action has a physical occurrence, but it also requires legal recognition by the Lawgiver for its effects to follow. Invalidity is the absence of that legal recognition. The author emphasizes that this concept applies to both verbal acts (e.g., contracts) and physical acts (e.g., possession). This sets the stage for detailed rules on void and voidable transactions in later chapters.

Key Terms
البطلان (al-buṭlān): عدم اكتساب التصرف وجوده الاعتباري وآثاره في نظر الشارع مع وجود صورته الحسية
الوجود الحسي (al-wujūd al-ḥissī): وقوع التصرف بحركته وصورته المادية
الوجود الاعتباري (al-wujūd al-iʿtibārī): اعتبار الشارع للتصرف صفة الصلاحية لترتب الآثار الشرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, what is the definition of 'invalidity' (batal) in juristic terminology?
A) The act is physically nonexistent.
B) The act lacks both sensory and legal existence.
C) The act lacks legal existence and effects in the view of the Lawgiver, though its sensory form exists.
D) The act is sinful and prohibited.

Key Takeaway
Invalidity in Hanafi theory is the absence of legal existence (wujud i'tibari) despite the presence of sensory existence (wujud hissi); the act physically occurs but produces no legal effects.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Theory of Invalidity: Sensory vs Legal Existence
Defining bultan through the distinction between sensory and legal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sensory existence from legal existence and apply this distinction to determine whether a transaction is valid or void in Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
A transaction may exist physically yet be legally void—legal recognition gives it life.

This lesson introduces the fundamental concept of invalidity (bāṭil) in Islamic jurisprudence. We will explore the distinction between sensory existence and legal existence as articulated by Mustafa al-Zarqa. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining whether a contract or acknowledgment produces legal effects. This principle underlies many rulings in transactions and is a cornerstone of legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions of existence
For any human action, recognize that it has a sensory aspect (physical occurrence) and a legal aspect (Shari'ah recognition).
2
Define legal existence
Legal existence is the Lawgiver's attribution of validity to an action, allowing its effects to follow.
3
Connect to invalidity
Bāṭil means the action lacks legal existence despite its sensory existence.
4
Apply to contracts
Contracts like sale or gift are void if they lack essential pillars or conditions (e.g., capacity, lawful subject matter).
Example: sale by a lunatic is void.
5
Apply to acknowledgments
Acknowledgment (iqrār) is void if issued by someone without capacity or if manifestly false.
Example: minor's acknowledgment or acknowledging cutting a hand that is intact.

Chapter (51) Theory of Invalidity: An Overview
This is the essence of the jurists' view and their discourse on that: (See al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, 1/292-296).
And this is absolute invalidity itself in the legal sense in juristic terminology (see: al-Mūjaz fī al-Iltizāmāt by Professor al-Sanhouri, p. 164).

The foreigner.
Secondly: The terminological definition we choose for invalidity.
3/51 - Sensory existence and legal existence:
Every human action has two aspects of existence:
- Sensory existence: its occurrence through physical movement and material form.
- Legal existence: that the Lawgiver deems it, after its occurrence, worthy of having its rulings attached to it, which are its legally prescribed effects.
It is obvious that whatever has a legal attribute upon which its rulings depend, if that attribute is not granted, its existence is like non-existence; it is like a rigid form with no meaning or effective soul. Rather, legal recognition is what breathes spirit into it and gives it life and efficacy.
This is a general view that applies to all civil actions, verbal and physical.
That is, invalidity in its civil sense applies to actions as it applies to statements. Accordingly, we can define invalidity in juristic terminology as:
The action's failure to acquire its legal existence and its effects in the view of the Lawgiver; i.e., invalidity is the absence of that legal existence for the action, even if its sensory form exists.
Thirdly: Application to verbal and physical actions.
4/51 - A - Invalidity of verbal actions: Verbal actions are contractual and non-contractual, as previously mentioned in 1/27.
703

[PAGE_665]
:
Chapter Seven: Theory of Shari'ah Ratifiers in Fiqh
A - Contracts such as sale, lease, and gift: Their sensory existence is the occurrence of offer and acceptance, or what stands in their place by which contracts are normally concluded.
Their legal existence is their being a binding tie connecting the contracting parties with rights and obligations that the Lawgiver has ordained upon the formation of this tie between them.
So if these contracts occur fulfilling their essential elements and basic conditions in the view of the Lawgiver, they are considered concluded, i.e., legally existing just as they exist sensorily, and their rulings and results that the Lawgiver attached to their conclusion follow, such as transfer of ownership or otherwise. But if they occur not fulfilling their pillars and conditions upon which the Lawgiver made their legal existence dependent in His view, such as a sale concluded by a lunatic, or a gift and donation from the property of a waqf or from the property of a minor, then the contract, despite its sensory existence, is legally non-existent, i.e., not concluded. No transfer of ownership or obligation arises from it. This is the meaning of the invalidity of a contract. (See: Badā'i' on Ijārah, 4/218, and on Sales, 5/305). B - The same applies to non-contractual verbal actions, whether they are informatory like acknowledgment (iqrar) or creative like a claim seeking a right.
Its ruling:
1 - Acknowledgment (iqrar) is: Information from a person about a right owed by him to another (Majallah, Article 1572). If acknowledgment is issued from a person fulfilling its conditions, the acknowledgee is held to it, because it is a general legal maxim that a person is held to his acknowledgment: whoever acknowledges a contract is bound by its ruling, and whoever acknowledges an act such as destruction etc. is bound by its consequence of liability and responsibility (Majallah, Articles 79 and 1577).
But if the acknowledgment lacks some of its Shari'ah conditions, such as if it is issued by someone without legal capacity like a minor, or issued by someone with capacity but it is obviously false, like a person acknowledging that he cut off so-and-so's hand while the hand is intact and sound, then it is not considered legally, so its sensory existence is like non-existence, and the acknowledgee is not held to it. Then it is said: The acknowledgment is void.
704
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical exposition on the concept of invalidity (bultan) in Islamic jurisprudence, authored by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. The author begins by presenting the essence of juristic discourse on invalidity, referencing al-Shatibi's al-Muwafaqat and modern Egyptian civil law (al-Sanhouri). He then proposes a terminological definition: invalidity is the absence of legal existence for a transaction despite its sensory occurrence.

Key to this definition is the distinction between sensory existence (wujud hissi) — the physical performance of an action — and legal existence (wujud i'tibari) — the Shari'ah's recognition of the action as valid and capable of producing legal effects. The author stresses that legal recognition is akin to a soul that animates the transaction; without it, the transaction is a lifeless form.

This principle applies to all civil actions, both verbal (aqwal) and physical (af'al). The author then applies it to contracts ('uqud) and acknowledgments (iqrar). For contracts like sale, lease, or gift, if they are performed with proper offer and acceptance (sensory existence) but lack the essential pillars and conditions set by the Lawgiver (e.g., capacity, lawful subject matter), they are void (batil) — legally non-existent. Examples given include a sale by a lunatic or a gift of waqf property. No rights or obligations arise.

Similarly, for acknowledgments (iqrar), if issued by a person without capacity (e.g., a minor) or if the content is manifestly false (e.g., claiming to have cut off a hand that is still attached), the acknowledgment is void and produces no legal consequences. The author references the Majallah articles to support this.

The passage aligns with the Hanafi school's approach, which distinguishes between batil (void ab initio) and fasid (defective but potentially valid). Here, the author equates bultan with absolute nullity, consistent with the general legal theory. The authority weight is the author's own reasoned argument, drawing from classical and modern sources.

Key Terms
الوجود الحسي (al-wujud al-hissi): الوقوع الفعلي للتصرف بحركته وصورته المادية.
الوجود الاعتباري (al-wujud al-i'tibari): اعتبار الشارع التصرف صالحاً لترتب الآثار الشرعية عليه.
البطلان (al-butilan): عدم اكتساب التصرف وجوده الاعتباري وآثاره في نظر الشارع.
العقد (al-'aqd): ما يتم بالإيجاب والقبول أو ما يقوم مقامهما، وينشئ رابطة بين المتعاقدين.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): إخبار الشخص بحق عليه لغيره.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Invalidity (bāṭil) in legal theory means the absence of legal existence; the transaction physically occurred but is legally null.

Nullity of Lawsuits: Conditions for a Valid Da'wa
Conditions for valid lawsuit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid lawsuit and identify when a lawsuit is null according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A lawsuit is legally non-existent if the plaintiff lacks capacity or the claim is uncertain.

This lesson focuses on the first part of al-Zarqa's discussion on nullity: the lawsuit. In Islamic procedural law, a judge only hears a claim if it meets Shariah conditions. These conditions ensure that the judicial process is meaningful and enforceable. Without them, the lawsuit is considered legally non-existent (batil), and the judge ignores it.

Guided Path
1
Define the lawsuit (da'wa)
Understand the definition: a claim of a right against another before a judge.
Majalla 1613
2
Identify the conditions
The passage lists two conditions: the plaintiff must be sane (not minor or insane), and the claimed object must be known and possible.
These are conditions for the judge to hear the case.
3
Apply conditions to determine nullity
If a condition fails (e.g., plaintiff is a minor, claim is unknown), the lawsuit is null.
Nullity means the judge does not hear it, defendant not required to respond.
