Chapter Fifty-Three: Results of Nullity
1/53: Indeed, nullity has a fundamental negative result from which several general rulings branch off, which are like characteristics of nullity. We will explain below, successively and in order: that fundamental negative result, then the exceptional cases regarding it, then the characteristics branching from that fundamental result.
2/53: The Fundamental Result of Nullity: Nullity has a general negative result, which is that nullity deprives the legal act of its causality for its ruling, so that specific effect prescribed by Shariah for it in the case of its validity does not arise from it. So if the void act is a contract, its obligations do not arise; if it is an acknowledgement, it is not binding on its maker; if it is a discharge, the obligation is not dropped, and so on. This is a general view regarding the nullity of all acts absolutely. By this, the meaning of the jurists' statement becomes clear: 'A void contract does not produce any legal effect at all' (see: al-Bada'i', 5/305). Some contemporaries today have objected to this, saying that non-consideration of a void contract is itself a legal ruling for it; it is a negative ruling opposite the positive constitutive ruling of a concluded contract, so how can it be said that the void does not produce any ruling? This is a correct view in itself, but the jurists meant that a void contract does not produce the rulings and results that the Lawgiver prescribed for the state of its conclusion and validity, as we clarified previously. The source of the objection and criticism is the indeterminacy of the word 'ruling' (hukm), for it implies the negation of any ruling whatsoever, not the specific ruling prescribed for a valid contract.
3/53: Exceptional Cases Where a Void Contract Has a Considered Effect: However, this general principle regarding the negative result of nullity is deviated from by exceptional cases where a void contract has an effect on the description of an act based upon it, so that some subsidiary effects that arise from the state of its conclusion do arise. We will see this in the following three cases:
4/53: The First Case: In the Marriage Contract – In marriage, the clearest cases appear where a void marriage contract has some subsidiary effects of a valid contract. A void marriage contract does not establish its original ruling of permissibility of enjoyment, maintenance, and inheritance under any circumstances, but if it is followed by consummation (dukhul), then the woman is entitled to a dower (mahr) by it, the lineage of the child is established from the man, and the woman is obliged to observe the waiting period ('idda) from the date of separation. This is because the Shariah is cautious in establishing lineages and imposing the 'idda by mere shubha (semblance/likeness), and that entails the obligation of the mahr. Indeed, the connection of a man with a woman in Islamic law cannot be devoid of one of two results: either the punishment of hadd if it is pure adultery, or the mahr if it is by a valid marriage or by shubha. The contract, even if not valid, creates a shubha that establishes these precautionary rulings. Indeed, if there is no room for shubha, these rulings are not established, as we will see in (7/54). It is noted here that these exceptional rulings in a void marriage are established only based on the act of consummation, not on the mere contract, but they are not established...
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion on the consequences of nullity (batalan) in legal acts. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating that nullity has a fundamental negative result: a void act does not produce its intended legal effect. For a void contract, no obligations arise; for a void acknowledgement, it is not binding; for a void discharge, the debt remains. He then clarifies a common misunderstanding of the juristic maxim 'A void contract produces no ruling at all.' Critics argue that even non-consideration is a ruling, but the author explains that the maxim refers to the specific substantive rulings of a valid contract, not any possible ruling. Next, he introduces exceptions: in certain cases a void contract can have subsidiary effects. The first exception is the marriage contract. A void marriage does not make intercourse lawful, oblige maintenance, or inheritance. However, if consummation occurs, it triggers three subsidiary effects: the woman gets a dower (mahr), the child's lineage is established, and the woman must observe an 'idda. The reason is that Shariah takes precautions in matters of lineage and 'idda based on shubha (a semblance of validity). The consummation creates a shubha, so these rulings apply to protect rights. The mahr is obligatory because any sexual contact between a man and woman in Islam either results in the hadd punishment (if clear zina) or mahr (if by valid marriage or shubha). The author notes that these effects only follow if there is room for shubha; otherwise they do not apply. He also stresses that these effects depend on actual consummation, not the mere contract.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A semblance or likelihood of validity that leads to precautionary rulings
دخول (dukhul): Consummation of marriage; the act of sexual intercourse

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason given in the passage for the subsidiary effects of a void marriage after consummation?
A) The void contract becomes valid retroactively.
B) The Shariah protects lineage and imposes 'idda based on shubha.
C) The couple agreed to these terms in the contract.
D) The man must pay mahr because of the harm caused.

Key Takeaway
A consummated void marriage triggers mahr, lineage, and 'idda due to shubha, but the marriage remains void for its primary effects.

Exceptional Effects of a Void Marriage Contract
Explaining the exceptional legal effects of a void marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three subsidiary legal effects of a consummated void marriage contract in Hanafi fiqh and explain the rationale based on the concept of shubha (semblance).

Core Idea
Even a void marriage can yield dower, lineage, and iddah if consummated, due to shubha.

We have learned the general principle that a void contract (al-ʿaqd al-bāṭil) produces no legal effects. However, Islamic law makes an exception for marriage (nikah) when consummation occurs. This lesson examines why and how a void marriage contract can still trigger certain subsidiary rulings based on the concept of shubha (semblance).

Guided Path
1
Restate the general rule
Recall that a void contract (bāṭil) has no original rulings; it is as if it never existed.
This is the default principle.
2
Identify the exception in marriage
When a void marriage is followed by consummation, certain effects emerge due to shubha.
Shubha provides a legal semblance of validity.
3
List the three subsidiary effects
Name them: dower (mahr), lineage (nasab) of the child, and waiting period (iddah).
These are the only effects; no inheritance or mutual rights.
4
Explain the rationale (shubha)
The Lawgiver prescribes these effects to protect lineage and avoid treating the union as plain adultery.
Shubha removes the hadd punishment and justifies precautionary measures.
5
Identify the condition
All these effects depend on consummation; the contract alone is insufficient.
Without consummation, no effects arise.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legitimizing Supports in Jurisprudence. A correct view in itself, but the jurists mean that the void contract does not entail the rulings and results that the Lawgiver prescribed for the state of its conclusion and consideration, as we explained in the preceding exposition. The origin of the ambiguity and objection is the indefinite word 'hukm', as it suggests the negation of any ruling, not the specific ruling prescribed for the valid contract. 3/53 - Exceptional cases in which the void contract has a considered effect. However, this general principle in the negative outcome of nullity is departed from by exceptional cases in which the void contract has an effect on the description of the act based upon it, so some subsidiary effects that follow from the state of its conclusion are attached. We will see that in the following three cases: 4/53 - First case: In the marriage contract. In marriage, the most manifest cases appear in which the void contract has some subsidiary effects of the valid contract. The void marriage contract does not establish its original ruling of permissible enjoyment, maintenance, and inheritance under any circumstances, but if it is followed by consummation, then the woman becomes entitled to a dower, the lineage of the child from the man is established, and the woman must observe the waiting period from the date of separation. That is because the Law is cautious in establishing lineages and requiring the waiting period merely on the basis of semblance (shubha), and that necessitates the obligation of the dower. Indeed, the connection of a man with a woman in Islamic law cannot be free of one of two results: either the punishment of the hadd if it is pure fornication, or the dower if it is by a valid marriage or by semblance. And the contract, though not valid, creates a semblance by which these precautionary rulings are established. So much so that if there is no room for semblance, these rulings are not established, as we will see in (7/54). It is noted here that these exceptional rulings in the void marriage were not established except based on the act of consummation, not merely on the contract. But they were not established without this void contract, for then the connection between the man and woman would be mere fornication with no interpretation, were it not for reliance on this contract; hence they are considered an effect of the void contract.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage discusses an exception to the general principle that a void contract produces no legal effects. The author explains that in the case of a void marriage contract (nikah), if consummation (dukhul) occurs, certain subsidiary effects arise due to the presence of shubha (semblance). These effects are: (1) entitlement to a dower (mahr), (2) establishment of lineage (nasab) of the child from the man, and (3) obligation of the waiting period (iddah) on the woman. The rationale is that Islamic law is cautious in matters of lineage and iddah, and the physical union between a man and woman must result either in hadd punishment (if clear zina) or dower (if based on a valid contract or shubha). The void contract provides the necessary shubha to trigger these precautionary rulings. Importantly, these effects depend on consummation, not the contract alone. Without the void contract, the union would be plain fornication with no such effects.

Key Terms
العقد الباطل (al-ʿaqd al-bāṭil): A contract that is null and void ab initio, lacking essential elements or conditions, producing no original legal effects.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance or mistaken belief that a situation is lawful, which suffices to prevent the hadd punishment and to establish certain subsidiary effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The husband inherits from the wife.
B) The wife is entitled to a dower (mahr).
C) The marriage is automatically valid.
D) No legal effects arise at all.

Key Takeaway
A consummated void marriage contract triggers three subsidiary effects—dower, lineage, and iddah—due to the protective principle of shubha.

Transformation of Void Contracts: Qalb al-Aqd in Hanafi Law
Discussion on whether a void contract can transform into another valid contract, with analysis of two opinions and supporting legal maxims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني' to evaluate the possibility of transforming a void contract and distinguish the dominant Hanafi position from the minority view.

Core Idea
A void contract is treated as non-existent and cannot be transformed, but some jurists allow transformation by interpreting words figuratively.

When a contract is void, can it be 'converted' into a different valid contract? This question arises when parties use words of sale but clearly intend a gift. The Hanafi school has a dominant view that rejects such transformation because a void contract is legally non-existent. However, a minority view, supported by the author, argues that contracts should be interpreted by their purposes, allowing transformation. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the governing maxims, and the practical consequences for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage asks whether a void sale can transform into a gift and a void lease into a loan. Note the two scenarios: (1) general transformation, (2) transformation when a time limit and negation of price are stated.
2
State the two positions
First position: transformation is accepted based on the maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني'. Second position (dominant Hanafi): transformation is rejected because the original contract is void (non-existent). Identify which position the author favors.
The author supports the first position, citing Majalla maxims.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dominant view
The dominant view, as found in Radd al-Muhtar, argues that a void contract has no legal existence, so transformation cannot occur in a non-existent entity. This is analogous to the maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' – certainty (that the contract is void) is not removed by doubt (about intention).
This view is considered the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) in the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the supporting maxims for transformation
The author uses 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' – giving effect to speech is better than disregarding it. Since literal meaning fails (sale without price), the words 'sale' and 'lease' should be interpreted figuratively (majaz) as 'gift' and 'loan'. This is supported by the precedent of marriage being concluded with sale wording.
Qiyas: just as marriage can be made by 'I sell' figuratively, so can other contracts.
5
Apply the maxim to case studies
Consider a case: 'I sold you the usufruct of this house for a month without any price.' Under the dominant view, this is a void lease; under the minority view, it becomes a loan (i'arah). The practical difference: ownership and liability.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether property delivered under such an agreement transfers ownership or remains with the original owner.

Chapter (53): Consequences of Nullity. - Does the contract transform so that a sale becomes a gift, and a lease becomes a loan? - And does a sale also transform into a loan if it has a time limit with negation of compensation, as if he said: I sold you the usufruct of this thing for a month without price, or does the contract not transform in any of that? A group of jurists went to this transformation by way of applying the general maxim: Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures. And others went to the non-existence of this transformation, arguing that the original contract here is void, so it is non-existent, and transformation cannot occur in a non-existent thing. This is the apparently preponderant opinion in Hanafi ijtihad (see: Radd al-Muhtar, vol. 4 and 5, beginning of the books of al-‘Ariyah and al-Ijarah). But we see that the first opinion is more appropriate, and legal maxims support it. For among the established maxims is that giving effect to speech is better than disregarding it whenever possible (Majalla, 60). And among the forms of giving effect to speech is interpreting it figuratively when the literal meaning is impossible (Majalla, 61). Thus, the expression of sale and lease is considered figurative for gift and loan. And figurative language in contracts is well-known; even the jurists declared in marriage, despite its seriousness, that it is concluded with the word of sale by way of figurative expression (see: al-Hidayah and its commentaries, 3/107). (1) (1) Modern legal thought in foreign jurisprudence tends to this in what they call: the theory of transformation, a modern theory in German jurisprudence. Its proponents even expand it, saying that a sale transforms into a gift even if the price is mentioned in the contract if the price is trivial relative to the sold item, indicating an intent of donation. See: al-Mujaz fi al-Iltizamat by al-Sanhuri, p. 168 footnote, and his Nazariyat al-‘Aqd, p. 594ff. And the Syrian Civil Code, now enacted with its origin in the new Egyptian Code, adopted this. Article 145 thereof explicitly states:
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage discusses the transformation of void contracts (قلب العقد). Two opinions: The first opinion (preferred by the author) transforms a void sale into a gift and a void lease into a loan, based on the maxim that contracts are judged by purposes, not words (العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني). The second opinion (dominant in the Hanafi school, as in Radd al-Muhtar) rejects transformation because the original contract is void and non-existent—transformation cannot occur in non-existence. The author counters that the first opinion is stronger because of the maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' (giving effect to speech is better than disregarding it); so the words 'sale' and 'lease' should be interpreted figuratively as 'gift' and 'loan' when literal meaning fails. He supports this with examples from marriage contracts where sale wording is accepted. The footnote notes that modern German legal theory and the Syrian Civil Code adopt transformation even when a trivial price is mentioned, indicating donation intent.

Key Terms
قلب العقد (qalb al-‘aqd): Transformation of a void contract into a valid contract of a different type.
مجاز (majāz): Figurative use of language when literal meaning is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the dominant Hanafi position on whether a void sale can be transformed into a gift?
A) It always transforms into a gift.
B) It never transforms; the void contract remains void.
C) It transforms only if the price is trivial.
D) It becomes a loan (i'arah) instead.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, a void contract is treated as non-existent and does not transform into another contract, though a minority view supported by the maxim of purposes permits transformation through figurative interpretation.

The Void Contract Cannot Be Ratified
It is a legal maxim (qa'ida) from Hanafi juristic methodology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a void contract (batil) from a suspended contract (mawquf) and apply the rule that void contracts are legally non-existent and cannot be cured by subsequent ratification.

Core Idea
A void contract is legally non-existent; ratification cannot breathe life into nothing.

Students often assume that any flawed contract can be fixed by later approval. This lesson clarifies a fundamental distinction: ratification (ijaza) only applies to suspended contracts that are valid in themselves, not to void ones. We will explore why a void contract is irreparable and how this rule operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
State the rule clearly: 'A void contract cannot be ratified.' Commit this to memory as an absolute principle.
2
Understand Ratification (Ijaza)
Ratification is a unilateral act that confirms a pre-existing suspended contract. It applies only to contracts that are valid in their pillars but whose effect is pending on a third party's consent.
The third party may either ratify (making the contract effective) or reject (voiding it).
3
Distinguish Void from Suspended
A void contract is invalid from its inception due to missing essential elements (e.g., subject matter non-existent). It has no legal existence – 'as if it never occurred.' Unlike a suspended contract, there is nothing to ratify.
Think of the analogy: a suspended contract is a messenger blocked by a fence; ratification is permission to cross. A void contract has no messenger at all.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Imagine a sale of alcohol (void due to illegality). Later the seller obtains a license and wants to ratify. According to the rule, the original contract cannot be ratified; only a new valid contract can be made.
This demonstrates the practical consequence: ratification is powerless for void contracts.

Source Text

١٠/٥ - (ثالثاً : العقد الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة :
فالإجازة إنما هي في الحقيقة إرادة منفردة يقرر بها الإنسان ويُمضي تصرفاً قائماً متوقفاً جعل الشرع له فيه حق التقرير والرفض.
فالنظر الفقهي أن محل الإجازة إنما هو العقد المنعقد الموقوف الذي يمس حقاً لغير العاقدين. فلذلك الغير أن يجيزه إن شاء فينفذ عليه، أو أن يرفضه فيبطل. وعلى كل حال يعتبر العقد الموقوف عقداً صحيحاً لكنه معقول، أي محتبس عن السريان. فبإجازة صاحب العلاقة يحل عقاله فيجتاز محبسه وينفذ إلى هدفه. فكأنه رسول ساع إلى غاية بينه وبينها أرض محمية ،الحدود، فإذا أذن له صاحبها اجتازها بالإجازة وإن منعه عاد أدراجه. (ر: «البدائع» ١٤٨/٥ - ١٤٩).
والعقد الباطل، كما رأينا ليس قائماً متوقفاً ليمكن إنفاذه إلى غايته بالإجازة، بل هو لغو عديم الاعتبار في نظر التشريع، فكأنه لم يوجد أصلاً ر البدائع (۳۰٥/٥ فإذا ما أريدت إجازته لينفذ إلى الغاية الحقوقية
Third: The void contract does not accept ratification. Ratification is essentially a unilateral will by which a person confirms and executes a pre-existing suspended transaction that the law has given him the right to confirm or reject. The juristic view is that the subject of ratification is only a concluded suspended contract that affects a right of a third party. So the third party may ratify it if he wishes, and it becomes binding on him, or reject it and it becomes void. In any case, the suspended contract is considered a valid but restrained contract—i.e., withheld from taking effect. Through the ratification of the concerned party, its restraint is removed, it passes its obstacle, and reaches its goal. It is like a messenger hurrying to a goal between him and which there is a protected land; if its owner permits him, he crosses it with the ratification, and if he prevents him, he returns. (Ref: al-Bada'i 5/148-149). The void contract, as we have seen, is not existing and suspended so that it can be executed to its goal by ratification; rather, it is nugatory and without legal consideration, as if it never existed originally. (Ref: al-Bada'i 5/305). So if one wishes to ratify it so that it reaches the legal goal...
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This is a rule distinguishing void contracts from suspended contracts regarding ratification. Ratification (ijaza) applies only to suspended contracts (mawquf) that are valid in essence but whose effect is contingent on a third party's consent. The author explains that a suspended contract is like a messenger blocked by a protected land; with permission, it proceeds. In contrast, a void contract has no legal existence; it cannot be ratified because there is nothing to ratify. This principle is fundamental in Hanafi contract law and is referenced from al-Bada'i'.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Unilateral ratification by the person whose right is affected, making a suspended contract effective.
العقد الموقوف (al-aqd al-mawquf): A contract that is valid in its pillars but its effect is suspended pending consent of a third party.
العقد الباطل (al-aqd al-batil): A contract that is invalid from its inception due to missing essential elements or conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi law, why cannot a void contract be ratified?
A) Because it is a valid contract that only needs consent.
B) Because it lacks legal existence and is considered as if it never occurred.
C) Because ratification requires a court order.
D) Because the parties must renegotiate the terms.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ratification (ijaza) only applies to suspended contracts (mawquf); a void contract (batil) has no legal existence and cannot be brought to life by subsequent consent.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Legal Effects of a Void Contract: No Rescission, No Prescription
Explaining legal consequences of a void contract under Hanafi theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three legal consequences of a void (bāṭil) contract under Hanafi theory and apply them to a practical fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
A void contract is legally nonexistent; it requires no rescission and is never cured by time.

In Hanafi law, a void contract (عقد باطل) is not merely flawed—it is legally nonexistent from its inception. This radical nullity carries specific consequences that differ fundamentally from a defective (fāsid) or voidable contract. The passage from al-Zarqa's Madkhal clarifies that a void contract needs no rescission (فسخ), cannot be used as evidence in court, and is immune to prescription (تقادم). Understanding these points is essential for any mufti assessing the validity of past transactions or marriages.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish void from voidable
First, understand the Hanafi distinction: a void (bāṭil) contract is legally non-existent, while a voidable (fāsid) contract exists but has a defect that can be cured. This distinction underlies all subsequent rules.
If you confuse bāṭil with fāsid, the rules of rescission and prescription will be misapplied.
2
Analyze why no rescission is needed
The passage argues that rescission (faskh) is only meaningful for an existing contract with an option for termination. Since a void contract never existed, 'removing' it is logically impossible. The parties may simply treat it as null without any legal act.
Contrast this with faskh in khiyār al-ʿayb (option of defect) where the contract is valid until rescinded.
3
Understand the court's role
Although theoretically no annulment is needed, practically a party may need a court declaration to confirm the nullity, especially if the contract was executed. The court's ruling does not create nullity but merely declares the pre-existing voidness.
This means the judge is not 'annulling' but 'declaring'—a crucial nuance for procedural fatwa.
4
Apply the no‑prescription rule
Prescription (taqādum) is the lapse of time that extinguishes claims or rights. The passage states that prescription does not apply to voidness because the contract is inherently null from the start; time cannot validate what never existed.
This is a major difference from many modern legal systems where prescription can cure certain defects.

أما العقد الباطل فهو معدوم لم يوجد؛ وإزالة المعدوم تحصيل للحاصل، وهذا عند علماء المعقول مستحيل .

على أنه إذا كان من الناحية النظرية لا حاجة بمن يتمسك بالبطلان إلى إقامة الدعوى لأجل الإبطال فمن الناحية العملية قد يحتاج إلى إقامة الدعوى لتقرير البطلان، ولاسيما إذا كان العقد الباطل قد تم تنفيذه. وذلك كما لو كان بيعاً أعقبه تسليم المبيع ويريد البائع استرداده. فهو مضطر في هذه الحال إلى التقاضي إذا لم يستطع الاسترداد بالتراضي. وكذا إذا كان سبب البطلان فيه خفاء واشتباه، فإنَّ الطرف المتمسك بالبطلان يحتاج إلى المبادرة بالادعاء ليطمئن إلى رأي القاضي ،وتقديره كي لا يبني تصرفه على تقدير البطلان، ثم تحكم المحكمة بخلافه فينقض تصرفه .
لكن يلحظ في جميع هذه الأحوال التي قد يحتاج فيها المتمسك بالبطلان إلى إقامة الدعوى به أنَّ القاضي لا يبطل العقد في هذه الأحوال إبطالاً كما يفسخ العقد المستحق للفسخ بحيث إذا لم يفسخه يكون قائماً نافذاً في الواقع، وإنما يقضي القاضي في هذه الحال بأن العقد قد صدر من أصله أي ان القضاء بالبطلان عند التنازع فيه لا ينشىء الإبطال، بل يظهر البطلان ويقرره تقريراً.

٥٣ / ١٢ - خامساً: البطلان لا يسري عليه حكم التقادم :
التقادم هو مرور الزمن الذي تقدمت الإشارة إليه في عدة مناسبات (۱) . فلا يمتنع التمسك ببطلان العقد الباطل لمنع تنفيذه ولو مضت (۱) ر ف ٣/١٠ و ٤/٢٣.
۷۲۵
The seventh chapter: Theory of Legal Invalidities in Fiqh. A - As for it not needing rescission, because rescission is only needed for an existing contract where one of the two parties has the option to dissolve its bond or keep it. Then the party with the option may independently rescind, as in the option of condition in sale, where the owner of the option rescinds during the stipulated period without need for consent or judicial ruling. And rescission may need consent or judicial ruling, as in the option of defect. In any case, if the contract deserving rescission is not rescinded, it remains in effect and operative. As for the void contract, it is nonexistent, never existed; and removing the nonexistent is obtaining what already exists, and this according to rational scholars is impossible. So each of the two contracting parties may consider the void contract as nonexistent and act based on this consideration: - If the void contract is, for example, a sale, the seller may sell the sold item again or use and consume it, because it is still his property. - If the void contract is a marriage, the woman may marry another husband (1). If the buyer or the husband in the void contract files a lawsuit before the court demanding delivery of the sold item or demanding the woman's compliance, the other party can repel the claim by asserting the contract's invalidity. Thus, the one adhering to invalidity is spared the trouble of filing a lawsuit to annul the void contract, and legally and Islamically suffices with a passive position by which he repels the opponent's claim if attacked. However, although theoretically the one adhering to invalidity does not need to file a lawsuit for annulment, practically he may need to file a lawsuit to establish the invalidity, especially if the void contract has been executed. For example, if it is a sale followed by delivery of the sold item and the seller wants to reclaim it, he is forced in this situation to litigate if he cannot reclaim by mutual consent. Also, if the reason for invalidity is obscure and ambiguous, the party adhering to invalidity needs to initiate a lawsuit to be assured of the judge's opinion and assessment, lest he base his action on the estimation of invalidity and then the court decides otherwise, overturning his action. However, it should be noted that in all these cases where the one adhering to invalidity may need to file a lawsuit, the judge does not annul the contract in these cases as he would rescind a contract deserving rescission, such that if he does not rescind it, it would remain existing and effective in reality. Rather, in this case, the judge rules that the contract was void from its origin, meaning that the judgment of invalidity when disputed does not create the invalidity, but rather reveals and confirms it. B - As for the void contract not being accepted as proof before the court, that is a necessary consequence of its inherent invalidity without need for annulment. It entails that the judge rejects reliance on the void contract if an interested party claims or relies on it, even if the opponent does not raise the invalidity and is not aware of it. This is because if the void does not need annulment, then establishing its invalidity when relied upon does not depend on a request. 53/12 - Fifth: Invalidity is not subject to prescription: Prescription is the passage of time mentioned in several contexts (1). So there is no bar to asserting the invalidity of a void contract to prevent its enforcement, even if time has passed. (1) Cf. vol. 3 p. 10 and vol. 4 p. 23. 725
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's Madkhal explains the legal effects of a void (bāṭil) contract under Hanafi theory. The author first clarifies that a void contract does not require rescission (faskh) because rescission presupposes an existing contract that is potentially effective. A void contract, by contrast, is legally non-existent; it is as if it never occurred. Therefore, the concept of 'removing' it is logically absurd. Each party may treat it as nonexistent: the seller can resell the item, and the woman can remarry (though if the void marriage was consummated, she must observe idda to avoid lineage confusion). Practically, a party may need to go to court to affirm the invalidity, especially if the contract was executed or the defect is hidden, but the court's ruling merely declares the original voidness, not creates it. Second, a void contract cannot be used as evidence before a judge; the judge must reject it even if neither party raises the issue. Third, the invalidity is not subject to prescription; the passage of time does not make a void contract enforceable. This is a core distinction between bāṭil (void ab initio) and fāsid (defective) in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
العقد الباطل (al-ʿaqd al-bāṭil): A contract that is void ab initio due to a defect in its pillars or conditions; it produces no legal effects.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission of an existing contract by a party with an option, by mutual consent, or by court order.
التقادم (al-taqādum): The lapse of time that bars a claim or right (prescription).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a void contract not need rescission (faskh)?
A) Because rescission is only for valid contracts with option clauses.
B) Because a void contract is legally nonexistent, so rescission would be removing what is already absent.
C) Because the parties can agree to ignore it without any legal process.
D) Because the judge automatically annuls it.

Key Takeaway
A void contract is legally nonexistent; it requires no rescission, cannot be used in court, and is never validated by the passage of time.

Consequences of Voidness: Lawsuit and Judicial Declaration
Explains consequences of voidness regarding lawsuits and judicial role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between judicial annulment (ibṭāl) and judicial declaration of voidness, and apply the rule that a void contract cannot be invoked in court.

Core Idea
A void contract is inherently void; the judge only declares it, not creates it.

While a void contract is legally nonexistent from the start, practical disputes often require a lawsuit to establish its voidness, especially if the contract has been executed or the defect is hidden. The judge does not 'annul' the contract but merely declares its pre-existing voidness. This lesson explores the first two consequences of voidness: the role of litigation and the rule that a void contract cannot be relied upon in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two consequences
Read the passage and find the two main consequences (أ and ب) of voidness discussed.
2
Understand the need for a lawsuit
The passage explains that even though a void contract is inherently void, a lawsuit may still be needed when the contract has been performed or the cause of voidness is ambiguous. Recognize that litigation establishes the fact of voidness, not the legal status.
3
Distinguish the judge's role
Contrast the judge's action in void contracts (declaring existing voidness) with rescission of a rescissible contract (where the judge's action creates the termination). The passage emphasizes that voidness is not created by the court.
4
Apply the second consequence
The passage states that a void contract cannot be invoked before the court, even if the opponent does not raise the issue. Understand that the judge must reject it ex officio because voidness is intrinsic.

الفصل (٥٣) نتائج البطلان
الدعوى لتقرير البطلان، ولاسيما إذا كان العقد الباطل قد تم تنفيذه. وذلك كما لو كان بيعاً أعقبه تسليم المبيع ويريد البائع استرداده. فهو مضطر في هذه الحال إلى التقاضي إذا لم يستطع الاسترداد بالتراضي. وكذا إذا كان سبب البطلان فيه خفاء واشتباه، فإنَّ الطرف المتمسك بالبطلان يحتاج إلى المبادرة بالادعاء ليطمئن إلى رأي القاضي ،وتقديره كي لا يبني تصرفه على تقدير البطلان، ثم تحكم المحكمة بخلافه فينقض تصرفه .

Chapter (53): Consequences of Voidness. The lawsuit is to establish voidness, especially if the void contract has been executed. That is as if it were a sale followed by delivery of the sold item and the seller wants to reclaim it. He is forced in this case to litigate if he cannot reclaim by mutual agreement. Likewise, if the cause of voidness is hidden and ambiguous, then the party adhering to voidness needs to initiate the claim to be assured of the judge's opinion and his assessment, so that he does not build his action on an estimation of voidness, then the court rules otherwise and his action is overturned. But it is observed in all these cases where the adherent to voidness may need to file a lawsuit for it that the judge does not annul the contract in these cases as he rescinds a contract subject to rescission, such that if he does not rescind it, it remains effective in reality. Rather, the judge in this case rules that the contract was void ab initio, meaning that the judgment of voidness when contested does not create annulment, but rather manifests voidness and establishes it. (End of section A). B - As for the fact that a void contract cannot be invoked before the court, that is a necessary consequence of its voidness in itself without need for annulment. It follows from this that the judge rejects the invocation of a void contract if an interested party claims or adheres to it before him, even if the opponent did not plead voidness and did not notice it. That is because if a void thing does not need annulment, then establishing its voidness when invoked does not depend on a request.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage discusses the first two consequences (أ and ب) of voidness (بطلان) in contracts. The author, a contemporary Hanafi scholar, explains that although a void contract is legally nonexistent, a party may still need to file a lawsuit to have it declared void, especially when the contract has been executed (e.g., a void sale where the buyer took possession and the seller wants the item back). The judge does not 'annul' the void contract as he would rescind a valid but defective contract; rather, he merely declares the existing voidness. This is because voidness is inherent, not created by judicial action. The second point emphasizes that a void contract cannot be relied upon in court. The judge must reject it even if the opposing party does not raise the issue. This follows from the fact that voidness is intrinsic and needs no formal annulment.

Key Terms
بطلان (butlan): Legal voidness; a contract that is legally nonexistent ab initio due to a fundamental defect in its elements or conditions.
إبطال (ibṭāl): The act of annulling something; here, the judicial act of creating voidness, which does not apply to void contracts as they are already void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the void contract is already void ab initio and does not require annulment.
B) Because the judge lacks jurisdiction over void contracts.
C) Because the parties must first agree to annulment.
D) Because voidness only becomes effective after a court order.

Key Takeaway
A void contract is intrinsically void; a lawsuit may be needed to establish the fact of voidness, but the judge only declares it, and the contract cannot be invoked in court even if not challenged.

Voidness and Prescription: The Right to Recover vs. Voidness Itself
Clarifies that prescription does not affect voidness itself but may affect post-execution recovery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that voidness is not subject to prescription to distinguish between the perpetual defense of voidness and the time-barred right of recovery.

Core Idea
Voidness is forever; prescription can only bar the remedy, not the underlying nullity.

This lesson clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi legal theory: the difference between the voidness of a contract and the right to reclaim property after execution. While voidness itself is never erased by time, the personal right to recover may be lost through prescription. Understanding this distinction is essential for issuing correct fatwas in cases involving old transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two elements
Recognize that voidness (batal) is a status of legal non-existence, while prescription (taqadum) is a procedural bar that applies to personal claims.
Voidness is objective; prescription is a defense that prevents hearing a case.
2
Understand the maxim
The author states that the void is nonexistent, and nonexistence cannot turn into existence by mere passage of time. Internalize this principle.
Maxim: الباطل لا يزول بالتقادم (Voidness is not removed by prescription).
3
Distinguish the two scenarios
Separate the pre-execution defense (parties have not yet exchanged countervalues) from post-execution recovery (exchange has occurred and time has passed).
Pre-execution: voidness defense always available. Post-execution: recovery claim may be barred.
4
Apply the rule to each scenario
In pre-execution, either party can raise voidness to refuse performance even after fifteen years. In post-execution, the right to reclaim the thing or price is a personal claim subject to the same prescription period.
Key: the defense of voidness is perpetual; the action for recovery is not.
5
Recognize the thamarah (practical consequence) for fatwa
A mufti must determine whether the case involves a defense (voidness can be invoked) or a claim for return (may be time-barred).
Fatwa will differ based on which right is being exercised.

Source Text

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
على العقد مدة التقادم، وهي خمسة عشر عاماً. ذلك لأن الباطل معدوم.
والعدم لا ينقلب وجوداً بطول الأمد.

فلو فرض في البيع الباطل مثلاً أنَّ المشتري لم يكن قد قبض المبيع
حتى انقضى خمسة عشر عاماً، ثم رفع البائع الدعوى على المشتري ليلزمه بقبض المبيع وأداء الثمن. فإن للمشتري أن يدفع دعوى البائع بحجة بطلان البيع. وكذا لو رفع المشتري الدعوى على البائع طالباً تسليم المبيع بعد التقادم، فإن للبائع أن يدفع دعواه ببطلان البيع ويقبل من كل منهما هذا الدفع رغم تركه الادعاء بالبطلان مدة التقادم ذلك لأنَّ كلاً منهما في الأصل غير مكلف بالادعاء والتقاضي لإبطال العقد، لما قدمنا أن الباطل لا يحتاج إلى إبطال .
وذلك بخلاف ما لو كان البيع الباطل قد تم تنفيذه بالتقابض، ومضت مدة التقادم، ثم رفع البائع الدعوى بعد خمسة عشر عاماً يطلب استرداد المبيع بحجة بطلان البيع. فإنَّ للمشتري في هذه الحال أن يدفع دعوى البائع بتقادم العين في يده دون أن يعترف بأنه تلقاها منه بشراء باطل.
وعندئذ يُقضى برد دعوى الاسترداد، لأنه إذا كان البيع الباطل لا يحتاج إلى دعوى لإبطاله فإن استرداد المبيع بعد تنفيذ البيع بالتسليم يحتاج إلى دعوى كما سلَفَ بيانه فيخضع حق الاسترداد للتقادم لأن ترك الدعوى فيما تجب فيه من الحقوق الخاصة مدة التقادم يمنع سماعها . فيعتبر حكم التقادم هنا سارياً على حق ،الاسترداد، لا على بطلان العقد
·
ومثل ذلك يقال فيما لو رفع المشتري الدعوى على البائع في هذه الحال طالباً استرداد الثمن لبطلان البيع بعد مضي مدة التقادم على أداء الثمن، فإنَّ للبائع أن يدفع الدعوى بتقادم الحق المدعى به.
ويظهر الفرق جلياً بين سريان حكم التقادم على حق الاسترداد وعدم سريانه على بطلان العقد فيما لو تأخر التقابض في البدلين عن تاريخ العقد حتى مضت مدة التقادم على العقد الباطل، ولم تمض على التقابض. ففي
٧٢٦
Fifthly: Prescription does not apply to voidness. Prescription is the passage of time which has been referred to on several occasions. So it is not prohibited to invoke the voidness of a void contract to prevent its execution even if the period of prescription, which is fifteen years, has passed over the contract. That is because the void is nonexistent, and nonexistence does not turn into existence with length of time. For example, suppose in a void sale that the buyer had not taken possession of the sold item until fifteen years elapsed, then the seller sued the buyer to compel him to take possession and pay the price. Then the buyer may defend the seller's suit by the argument of the voidness of the sale. Likewise, if the buyer sued the seller demanding delivery of the sold item after prescription, then the seller may defend his suit by the voidness of the sale, and this defense is accepted from each of them despite their leaving the claim of voidness during the prescription period. That is because each of them originally is not obligated to claim and litigate to annul the contract, as we have stated that the void does not need annulment. This is contrary to the case where the void sale had been executed by mutual delivery and the prescription period passed, then the seller sued after fifteen years seeking recovery of the sold item on the ground of voidness of the sale. Then in this case the buyer may defend the seller's suit by the prescription of the asset in his hand without acknowledging that he received it from him by a void purchase. Thereupon the claim for recovery is dismissed. Because if the void sale does not need a lawsuit to annul it, then recovery of the sold item after execution of the sale by delivery needs a lawsuit, as explained earlier, so the right of recovery is subject to prescription. Because abandoning the lawsuit regarding what is required of private rights for the prescription period prevents it from being heard. So the ruling of prescription is considered here to apply to the right of recovery, not to the voidness of the contract. The same is said in the case where the buyer sues the seller in this situation demanding recovery of the price due to voidness of the sale after the prescription period has passed over payment of the price, then the seller may defend the lawsuit by the prescription of the claimed right. The difference is clear between the application of prescription to the right of recovery and its non-application to the voidness of the contract, in the case where delivery of the countervalues was delayed from the date of the contract until the prescription period passed over the void contract but did not pass over the delivery. So in [this case].
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage deals with the fifth consequence of voidness: that prescription (تقادم) does not apply to the voidness itself. Since a void contract is considered legally nonexistent, the passage of time cannot transform it into an existing valid contract. Therefore, a party can always raise the defense of voidness even after fifteen years. However, if the void contract has been fully executed (both parties delivered), the right to reclaim the subject matter or the price becomes a personal right that is subject to prescription. The author distinguishes between the voidness defense (which is perpetual) and the action for recovery (which can be barred by prescription). This is a nuanced Hanafi position that balances legal certainty with the stability of transactions.

Key Terms
تقادم (taqādum): The lapse of time (prescription) that bars the hearing of a lawsuit regarding a personal right; here, fifteen years.
استرداد (istirdād): The legal right to reclaim property or money that was transferred under a void contract, which is a personal right subject to prescription after execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a void sale that has been fully executed (both parties exchanged goods and price), and fifteen years have passed since the exchange, can the seller sue to recover the sold item claiming voidness?
A) Yes, because voidness is not subject to prescription.
B) No, because the right to recover is subject to prescription and has been lost.
C) Yes, because the buyer acknowledges the voidness.
D) No, because the contract becomes valid after fifteen years.

Key Takeaway
Voidness is never extinguished by time; only the right to reclaim after execution is subject to prescription.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Nullity is Not Affected by Prescription
Ruling on the effect of prescription on void contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Nullity is not affected by prescription' to distinguish between raising nullity as a defense and filing a lawsuit for return, and identify the procedural consequences.

Core Idea
Nullity is eternal: a void contract never becomes valid over time, but the right to sue for return may expire.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to fiqh explains a crucial distinction: the permanent invalidity of a void contract versus the procedural time limit for claiming return. This distinction has direct implications for modern legal systems and fatwa practice. We will unpack the maxim and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substantive right
Recognize that invalidity (bāṭil) is a permanent defect; the contract never becomes valid. Any party can reclaim their property at any time because the basis (the void contract) is legally non-existent.
2
Distinguish daf' (defense) from da'wa (claim)
The defense of nullity (raised when the other party demands execution) is never time-barred, because the contract is void ab initio. However, an affirmative lawsuit to reclaim property after execution may be subject to statutory prescription (taqādum) because procedural rules may impose time limits on judicial actions.
3
Apply the maxim in fatwa
In a fatwa, when asked whether a void contract can be challenged after many years, the answer depends on the posture: if the contract is still being enforced or a party demands performance, nullity can always be raised. If both parties consider the contract executed and one now wants to undo it, the claim may be time-barred by civil codes, not by Islamic law.

Source Text

Chapter (53) Results of Invalidity. In this case, each of the two contracting parties has the right to demand the return of what he delivered on the grounds of the contract's invalidity, and the other party cannot plead the passage of time on the contract. That is because (1) invalidity is not affected by prescription, and the right to reclaim has not prescribed. (1) It is mentioned in Article 142 of the Syrian Civil Code issued in 1949 that the lawsuit for invalidity is extinguished after fifteen years from the time of the contract. And in Article 141 of its Egyptian source. And what is meant by 'lawsuit for invalidity' is filing the lawsuit, not raising it as a defense. For claiming invalidity after execution of the void contract in order to reclaim what was executed is what is subject to the rule of prescription. As for raising invalidity as a defense against one who demands execution of the contract, prescription does not affect it, legally or according to Sharia, as we have clarified. And this is what the opinion settled upon, and this text was formulated accordingly when it was discussed in the revision committee of the Egyptian Parliament. And thereby the poor wording of the mentioned article becomes apparent. Ref: Book of Egyptian Civil Code by Jamal al-Din al-Atifi, 1/265, footnote 2. 727.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on consequences of contract nullity. The author states a fundamental legal principle: a void contract (bāṭil) produces no legal effects, and each party may reclaim what they gave. The defense of prescription (taqādum / lapse of time) cannot prevent this reclaim. The reason is that 'invalidity is not affected by prescription' – meaning the passage of time does not cure a void contract. However, the author distinguishes between raising nullity as a defense (dafʿ) versus filing a lawsuit (daʿwā) to enforce reclaim. If the other party sues to enforce the void contract, you can always raise nullity as a defense; no statute of limitations applies. But if you want to proactively sue for return of what you delivered after execution, that affirmative claim may be subject to the civil code's prescription period (15 years). This distinction is critical for muftis and judges: nullity itself is eternal, but procedural rights to sue may be time-barred. The author criticizes the Syrian Civil Code for conflating these two scenarios, noting that the correct understanding was adopted by the Egyptian review committee.

Key Terms
بطلان (buṭlān): Nullity; a contract that is fundamentally defective such that it never becomes valid
تقادم (taqādum): Prescription; lapse of time that extinguishes a legal claim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, can a party plead prescription (lapse of time) to prevent the other party from reclaiming property given under a void contract?
A) Yes, because the contract was executed long ago.
B) No, because nullity is not affected by prescription, but the party can plead it if the other sues for execution.
C) No, because prescription never applies in any case.
D) Yes, but only for the right to reclaim, not for nullity.

Key Takeaway
Nullity is eternal; it can always be used as a defense, but actively reclaiming property may be subject to procedural time limits.

Meaning of Fasad Linguistically and Terminologically
Definition of fasad linguistically and terminologically, distinguishing Hanafi usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi technical term 'fasād', distinguish it from 'buṭlān', and explain its intermediate status between validity and nullity.

Core Idea
Fasād in Hanafi law: a contract defective in subsidiary aspects, neither fully valid nor completely void.

This lesson unpacks the concept of fasād as it appears in classical Hanafi jurisprudence. Starting from its linguistic roots, we trace how early Hanafi jurists gave fasād a precise technical meaning in contract law, creating a unique three-tier classification of contracts: ṣaḥīḥ (valid), fāsid (corrupt), and bāṭil (void). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti, as it determines whether a contract can be rectified or must be abandoned entirely.

Guided Path
1
Parse the linguistic root
Identify the original meaning of fasād from the Arabic lexicon: change from soundness, decay, opposite of ṣalāḥ. The text gives examples like spoiled milk.
This sets the baseline before the technical shift.
2
Recognize the Quranic usage
See how fasād is used in the Quran for broader moral and social corruption (e.g., 'Corruption has appeared on land and sea'). This is still general.
Not yet the legal term.
3
Discover the Hanafi technical shift
Note that the Hanafi jurists appropriated fasād for a specific legal meaning: a contract defective in subsidiary aspects.
This is a key innovation.
4
Distinguish from buṭlān
Understand that fasād is unlike buṭlān (void) because the essential pillars are present; the defect is in non-essential attributes. The contract is not completely null.
Many schools treat them as synonyms.
5
Memorize the intermediate status
Conclude that fasād occupies a middle ground between ṣiḥḥa (validity) and buṭlān (nullity). It can be rectified.
This has direct implications for fatwa.

Chapter Fifty-Four: Theory of Corruption: 1 - Meaning of fasad linguistically and terminologically. 2 - Origin of the theory of fasad and disagreement concerning it. 3 - Transactions in which fasad differs from buṭlān. 4 - Definition of fasad. First: Meaning of fasad linguistically and terminologically. 1/54 - Fasad in the original language is the change of a thing from its sound state and its departure from moderation; it is the opposite of ṣalāḥ (soundness). One says: the milk, meat, fruit, and air spoiled when they undergo change or rot until they become unsuitable. Then it was used linguistically for all things and matters outside the system of uprightness, such as transgression, injustice, and sedition. Upon this is the saying of Allah Most High: 'Corruption has appeared on land and sea' [Surah ar-Rum 30:41] and His saying: 'And Allah knows the corruptor from the amender' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:220]. (Ref: Mufradāt al-Rāghib al-Iṣfahānī, al-Miṣbāḥ, al-Qāmūs al-Muḥīṭ, Asās al-Balāgha). Then when jurisprudential ijtihāds and their terminologies arose, the Ḥanafī jurists applied fasad to a new civil legislative meaning. They used it to indicate a state in which they consider the contract defective in some of its subsidiary aspects, a defect that places it in a rank between validity and nullity, so it is neither void (bāṭil) nor fully contracted, because its violation of its system is between
Source pages p. 687

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the Theory of Corruption (Nazariyyat al-Fasād) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author first provides the linguistic meaning of fasād: change from a sound state, decay, opposite of ṣalāḥ (soundness). He supports this with Quranic verses. Then he explains that Hanafi jurists gave fasād a technical meaning in contract law: a contract that is defective in its subsidiary (non-essential) elements. This places the contract in an intermediate status: it is not completely void (bāṭil) because the essential pillars are present, but it is also not fully valid (ṣaḥīḥ) due to a defect that can potentially be rectified. The passage emphasizes that this Hanafi distinction between fasād and buṭlān is unique, as other schools often consider them synonymous. The upcoming sections promise to discuss the origin of this theory, points of difference, and precise definition.

Key Terms
فساد (fasād): A technical term in Hanafi fiqh meaning a contract that is defective in subsidiary aspects, making it neither fully valid nor completely void
صحة (ṣiḥḥa): Validity; a contract that is complete in all essential and subsidiary requirements
بطلان (buṭlān): Nullity; a contract that lacks an essential pillar or condition, thus legally non-existent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do Hanafi jurists define 'fasād' in technical terms?
A) A contract that is completely void from the outset.
B) A contract that is defective in a subsidiary aspect, placing it between validity and nullity.
C) Any transaction that involves sin or injustice.
D) A contract that is valid but makrūh.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi concept of fasād is an intermediate legal category for contracts defective in subsidiary aspects, distinct from buṭlān and unique to the school.

The Hanafi Theory of Invalidity (Fasad) – A Unique Third Category
Introduction to the linguistic and technical meaning of fasad and the unique Hanafi intermediate category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasad, explain how it differs from butlan, and identify why the Hanafi school uniquely adopts this intermediate category.

Core Idea
Hanafis classify a contract with a subsidiary defect as فاسد (invalid) – neither fully valid nor completely void.

This lesson introduces the distinctive Hanafi concept of fasad (invalidity) in contracts. Unlike the majority of jurists who see a contract as either valid or void, Hanafis recognize a middle ground: a contract that violates the legal system in a subsidiary, non-essential aspect. Understanding this threefold classification is essential for a mufti when assessing contract validity and issuing fatwas in complex transactions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic origin
The term fasad linguistically means a change from a sound state. Recognize how this informs the legal meaning: a contract that has 'changed' from the sound contractual system but not completely broken it.
This sets the stage for the intermediate category.
2
Identify the Hanafi technical definition
A contract defective in a subsidiary aspect, placing it between validity and nullity. Confirm that all pillars and essential conditions are present.
3
Contrast with the majority view
For the jumhur (especially Hanbali), any violation of Sharia in a contract leads to nullity (batil). Hanafis distinguish due to differing degrees of violation.
This is the core ikhtilaf.
4
Grasp the rationale behind the Hanafi position
The strength of the legal consequence must be proportionate to the degree of violation. Fundamental defects cause butlan; subsidiary defects cause fasad.
This is a principle of proportionality based on talil.
5
Note the implication of legal prohibition (nahy)
The disagreement stems from whether a prohibition necessarily implies nullity (majority) or only invalidity depending on whether the prohibition relates to the essence or a subsidiary aspect (Hanafi).
The passage begins this point but is cut off; the core idea is clear.

ولفظ الفساد يشعر بذلك لأنه ينبيء بمعنى التغير والاختلال في شيء
ثانياً : منشأ نظرية الفساد والاختلاف فيها :
٢/٥٤ - إنَّ نظرية فساد العقود حنفية المنشأ كما أشرنا إليه، فإن الاجتهاد الحنفي هو الذي قررها وانفرد بها من بين سائر الاجتهادات الأخرى التي لا تجعل بين البطلان والصحة مرتبة ثالثة .
فالعقد عند جمهور المجتهدين - بالنسبة إلى وجوده الاعتباري وعدمه هو بين حالتين اثنتين إما صحيح، وهو المنعقد، وإما باطل أو فاسد، وهو غير المنعقد، وذلك عندما يخالف العقد الأمر والنهي الشرعيين في نظام التعاقد غير أن الاجتهاد الحنفي لحظ أن صور المخالفات ليست في درجة واحدة، بل منها مخالفات أساسية، ومنها فرعية فلا ينبغي أن تكون النتيجة واحدة في الحالين لأنَّ قوة الجزاء المؤيد يجب أن تتناسب مع درجة المخالفة للنظام. والعقد المخالف لنظامه في ناحية فرعية فقط هو موافق للنظام المشروع في جميع النواحي الأساسية وتوافرت فيه أركانه وسائر مقوماته وشرائطها. فيجب أن يكون في مرتبة بين البطلان والصحة.
·
٣/٥٤ - سبب الاختلاف في مرتبة الفساد:
وأصل ذلك أنهم اختلفوا في مقتضى النهي الشرعي عن التصرفات ذات الوجود الاعتباري كالعقود.
مقتضی النهي
- فذهبت بعض الاجتهادات وخاصة الحنبلي، إلى أن هو البطلان، لأنه ينافي مشروعية الفعل المنهي عنه مطلقاً، دون تمييز بين
Chapter Fifty-Four: The Theory of Invalidity (Fasad). 1 - The meaning of fasad linguistically and terminologically. 2 - The origin of the theory of invalidity and the disagreement about it. 3 - The transactions in which invalidity differs from nullity. 4 - Definition of invalidity. First: The meaning of fasad linguistically and terminologically. 54/1 - Fasad in the original language is the change of a thing from its sound state and its departure from moderation, so it is the opposite of righteousness. It is said: milk, meat, fruit, and air become corrupt when they undergo change or decay until they become unfit. Then it was used linguistically for all things and matters that deviate from the system of uprightness, such as transgression, injustice, and sedition. Based on this is His statement: 'Corruption has appeared on land and sea' [Surah al-Rum: 30/41] and His statement: 'And Allah knows the corrupt from the reformer' [Surah al-Baqarah: 2/220]. (Reference: Mufradat al-Raghib al-Isfahani, al-Misbah, al-Qamus al-Muhit, and Asas al-Balaghah). Then, when jurisprudential ijtihads and their terminologies emerged, the Hanafi jurists applied fasad to a new civil legislative meaning, using it to denote a condition in which they consider the contract defective in some of its subsidiary aspects, a defect that places it in a rank between validity and nullity. So it is neither null (bāṭil) and unconcluded, because its violation of the legal system is not a violation in an essential aspect as in the case of nullity; nor is it completely valid and recognized, because it involves a breach of the contractual system, even if this breach is in a non-essential subsidiary aspect. The term fasad indicates this because it conveys the meaning of change and disorder in something. Second: The origin of the theory of invalidity and the disagreement about it: 54/2 - The theory of invalidity of contracts is Hanafi in origin, as we have indicated. The Hanafi ijtihad established it and was unique among all other ijtihads that do not establish a third rank between nullity and validity. For the contract according to the majority of mujtahids - regarding its legal existence and non-existence - is between two states: either valid (ṣaḥīḥ), which is concluded, or null (bāṭil) or invalid (fāsid), which is not concluded. That is when the contract violates the Sharia commands and prohibitions in the contractual system. However, the Hanafi ijtihad observed that the types of violations are not of one degree; rather, there are fundamental violations and subsidiary ones, so the result should not be the same in both cases, because the strength of the supporting consequence must be proportionate to the degree of violation of the system. A contract that violates the system only in a subsidiary aspect is in conformity with the prescribed system in all fundamental aspects, and its pillars and all its essential elements and their conditions are present. Therefore, it must be in a rank between nullity and validity. 54/3 - The reason for the disagreement about the rank of fasad: The basis of that is that they differed regarding the implication of the legal prohibition (al-nahy al-sharʿī) concerning actions that have legal existence, such as contracts. Some ijtihads, especially the Hanbali, held that it is nullity, because it contradicts the legality of the prohibited act absolutely, without distinguishing between
Source pages p. 687

Commentary
This passage introduces the Hanafi theory of al-fasad (corruption/invalidity) in contracts. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by defining the term linguistically as a change from a sound state, then notes that Hanafi jurists gave it a specific technical meaning: a contract that has a defect in a subsidiary (non-essential) aspect, placing it in an intermediate rank between validity (sahih) and nullity (butlan). The passage explains that this middle rank is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools consider contracts either valid or null. The author then discusses the origin of this theory, stating that Hanafis observed that violations of the contractual system vary in degree—some fundamental, some subsidiary—so the legal consequence should vary accordingly. The passage ends with the beginning of the discussion on the cause of disagreement: different views on the legal implication (muqtada) of a Sharia prohibition. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the key point is that Hanafis distinguish between basic and subsidiary defects, leading to a threefold classification.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A contract defective in a subsidiary aspect, ranking between validity and nullity.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Nullity: a contract ineffective due to a fundamental violation of the legal system.
النظام الشرعي (al-niẓām al-sharʿī): The legal system of Sharia governing contracts.
النهي (al-nahy): A legal prohibition; the effect on contracts is debated among schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory of fasad, why is a contract that violates a subsidiary rule not considered completely void (batil)?
A) Because all contracts are presumed valid until proven otherwise.
B) Because the pillars and essential conditions of the contract are present.
C) Because the prohibition is not from the Quran or Sunnah.
D) Because Hanafis do not recognize nullity in contracts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely recognizes an intermediate category of invalidity (fasad) for contracts with subsidiary defects, requiring a mufti to assess the degree of violation before issuing a ruling.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Hanafi Theory of Fasād: A Third Category Between Valid and Void
Explaining the Hanafi theory of contractual corruption and its origin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi theory of fasād to distinguish between void (bāṭil) and corrupt (fāsid) contracts based on the cause of the prohibition.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school uniquely recognizes a middle legal category: a contract can be corrupt (fāsid) but not void (bāṭil) if the defect is subsidiary.

This lesson explores the Hanafi theory of contractual corruption. Unlike other schools, the Hanafis developed a third category between valid and void: the corrupt (fāsid) contract. The key is the nature of the legal prohibition: if the prohibition attaches to a subsidiary aspect, the contract remains valid in essence but defective in that aspect. This theory has profound implications for how judges and muftis handle contracts with prohibited conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The Hanafis distinguish valid, corrupt, and void contracts. Understand the definition of each.
Focus on the corrupt (fāsid) category as an intermediate state.
2
Analyze the prohibition
Determine whether the prohibition (nahy) is due to the act being inherently unlawful or due to a subsidiary defect.
Use the examples of usury (inherent) vs. prohibited conditions (subsidiary).
3
Apply the proportionality principle
The strength of the legal consequence must match the degree of violation.
This is the rationale behind the Hanafi gradation.
4
Contrast with the Hanbali view
The opposing school treats all prohibitions as leading to nullity. Understand their reasoning.
Hanbalis cite the same Quranic and hadith evidence but interpret it differently.
5
Derive the practical fatwa
Given a contract with a prohibited condition, state whether it is void or corrupt, and what corrective action is possible.
Practice with scenarios from the passage.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه الشرعي ليست مخالفة في ناحية جوهرية كما في حالة البطلان؛ ولا هو بالصحيح التام الاعتبار، لأن فيه إخلالاً بنظام التعاقد ولو أن هذا الإخلال في ناحية فرعية غير جوهرية. ولفظ الفساد يشعر بذلك لأنه ينبيء بمعنى التغير والاختلال في شيء.
ثانياً : منشأ نظرية الفساد والاختلاف فيها :
٢/٥٤ - إنَّ نظرية فساد العقود حنفية المنشأ كما أشرنا إليه، فإن الاجتهاد الحنفي هو الذي قررها وانفرد بها من بين سائر الاجتهادات الأخرى التي لا تجعل بين البطلان والصحة مرتبة ثالثة .

- فذهبت بعض الاجتهادات وخاصة الحنبلي، إلى أن هو البطلان، لأنه ينافي مشروعية الفعل المنهي عنه مطلقاً، دون تمييز بين النواحي التي يتعلق بها النهي. وعلى هذا حكموا ببطلان عقود الربا، للنهي القرآني فيها بقوله تعالى : فإن لَّمْ تَفْعَلُوا فَأذَنُوا بِحَرْبٍ مِّنَ اللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ وَإِن تُبْتُمْ فَلَكُمْ رُءُوسُ أَمْوَالِكُمْ لا تَظْلِمُونَ وَلَا تُظْلَمُونَ ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢٧٩/٢]. وحكموا كذلك ببطلان العقد المقترن بشرط ممنوع للنهي عنه في السنة النبوية. وقد يبطلون الشرط دون العقد، كما تقدم في نظرية العقود. وذهب الاجتهاد الحنفي إلى أن مجرد النهي عن الفعل لا يدل على عدم مشروعية أصله حتماً؛ بل قد تجتمع مشروعية أصل الفعل مع عنه، كما يظهر من التفصيل التالي:
٤/٥٤ - اختلاف آثار النهي الشرعي باختلاف أسبابه :
فما نهى عنه الشارع من التصرفات نهياً دون أن ينص على بطلانه نصاً، فإن نتيجة النهي تختلف فيه باختلاف سبب النهي، أي باختلاف نوع المخالفة للنظام الشرعي.

وفي مثل هذا يعتبر الفعل المنهي عنه باطلاً محضاً لا يفيد صاحبه ثمرة أو منفعة وعلى هذا لا يثبت في الزنى نسب ولا مهر، ولا يرث القاتل من قتيله كما لا يملك الغاصب المغصوب.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh. [The corrupt contract] is not a fundamental violation like in the case of nullity; nor is it perfectly valid, because there is a defect in the contract system, even if this defect is in a subsidiary non-essential aspect. The word 'fasād' indicates this because it conveys the meaning of change and defect in something.
Secondly: Origin of the theory of fasād and the difference in it:
2/54 - The theory of corruption of contracts is Hanafi in origin, as we indicated. It was the Hanafi ijtihad that established it and was unique among other ijtihads that do not posit a third rank between validity and nullity.
For the majority of jurists, a contract - in terms of its legal existence or non-existence - is between two states: either valid, meaning it is concluded, or void (bāṭil) or corrupt (fāsid), meaning it is not concluded, when the contract violates the command and prohibition of the Lawgiver in the system of contracting. However, the Hanafi ijtihad observed that the forms of violations are not all of one degree; rather, there are fundamental violations and subsidiary violations, so the result should not be the same in both cases because the strength of the confirming legal consequence must be proportional to the degree of violation of the system. A contract that violates its system only in a subsidiary aspect is compliant with the legislated system in all fundamental aspects, and its pillars and all other elements and conditions are present. So it must be in a rank between nullity and validity.
3/54 - Reason for difference in the rank of fasād:
The basis is their difference regarding the requirement of a legal prohibition (nahy) concerning actions with legal existence, like contracts.
- Some ijtihads, especially the Hanbali, held that it requires nullity, because it contradicts the legality of the prohibited act absolutely, without distinguishing between the aspects to which the prohibition relates. Accordingly, they ruled the invalidity of usury contracts due to the Quranic prohibition: 'But if you do not, then be warned of war from Allah and His Messenger. And if you repent, you have your capital sums. Do not oppress and be not oppressed' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:279]. Similarly, they ruled the invalidity of a contract coupled with a prohibited condition due to the prohibition in the Sunnah. They might invalidate the condition without the contract, as earlier in the theory of contracts. The Hanafi ijtihad held that mere prohibition of an action does not necessarily indicate the lack of legality of its origin; rather, the legality of the origin may coexist with the prohibition, as appears from the following detail:
4/54 - Different effects of legal prohibition according to its causes:
For actions prohibited by the Lawgiver without explicit textual indication of their nullity, the result of the prohibition varies according to the cause of the prohibition, i.e., according to the type of violation of the legal system.
a - The Lawgiver may prohibit an action because it is not legal in its origin, being a base evil in itself - like adultery, murder, and usurpation. In such a case, the prohibited action is considered purely void, conferring no benefit or utility to its doer. Accordingly, no lineage or dower is established in adultery, the killer does not inherit from his victim, and the usurper does not own the usurped property. Of this kind is the prohibition in the Sunnah of selling 'malāqīḥ' and 'maḍāmīn' as earlier in the theory of contracts, for the Lawgiver did not consider them valid objects of contract. Likewise, the prohibition of selling by 'mulāmasa' and 'munābadha', because they do not express valid consent. Thus these contracts lack some of their constituents, such as a valid object or pillar, and are considered void.
b - And the Lawgiver may prohibit an action that is originally legal, then the prohibition turns to...
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on 'Theory of Legal Sanctions' in al-Zarqa's work. It explains the Hanafi theory of fasād (corruption/invalidity) in contracts. The author begins by defining the corrupt contract as neither fundamentally defective like a void contract nor perfectly valid; it is an intermediate category. He then states that this theory is uniquely Hanafi: other schools only recognize two states—valid or void—while the Hanafis added a third, corrupt, to accommodate violations that are subsidiary. The reason for this difference lies in their interpretation of the legal prohibition (nahy). According to the Hanbali school (and others), any prohibition leads to absolute nullity, because it contradicts the legality of the act. The Hanafis, however, distinguish based on the cause of the prohibition. If the prohibition is because the act is inherently unlawful (like adultery or usury), then it is void (bāṭil). But if the act is originally lawful but a subsidiary aspect is prohibited, then only that aspect is corrupted, making the contract fāsid but not void ab initio. The author provides examples: contracts of usury, sale of unborn animals (malāqīḥ), and sales lacking proper consent are void; contracts with prohibited conditions are corrupt but may be rectifiable. This nuanced approach is a hallmark of Hanafi legal theory, showing their attention to proportionality and gradation in legal effects.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A middle state between validity and voidness; a contract that is basically sound but has a defect in a subsidiary matter.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Absolute nullity; the contract is legally nonexistent from its inception.
النهي (al-nahy): Legal prohibition from the Lawgiver; its effect on a contract depends on the reason for prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory of fasād, what is the legal status of a contract of sale that includes a prohibited condition (e.g., a condition that the buyer will resell at a fixed profit), assuming all essential pillars are present?
A) The contract is absolutely void (bāṭil) because the condition is prohibited.
B) The contract is corrupt (fāsid) and can be rectified by removing the condition.
C) The contract is valid and the condition must be enforced.
D) The contract is voidable at the option of either party.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis uniquely recognize a corrupt (fāsid) contract as an intermediate category between valid and void, determined by the cause of the legal prohibition.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Void vs. Defective Contracts: The Hanafi Theory of Prohibition’s Effect
Theoretical exposition of the Hanafi distinction between void and defective contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a prohibited contract as void (bāṭil) or defective (fāsid) by analyzing whether the prohibition targets the essence (aṣl) or a description (waṣf) of the act.

Core Idea
A prohibited contract may be void if the prohibition strikes its essence, or defective if it strikes a description.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi distinction between void (bāṭil) and defective (fāsid) contracts. While a casual reader might assume any prohibited contract is void, the Hanafi school teaches that many prohibited contracts are in fact legitimate in origin but flawed in description. This distinction has profound implications for the validity and effects of contracts in Islamic law, and is a cornerstone of Hanafi fatwā methodology in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition source
Locate the evidence (Quran, Sunnah) that prohibits the act. Note whether the prohibition is explicit about invalidity or merely forbids the act.
For example, Quran 2:279 prohibits ribā without explicitly stating the contract is void.
2
Determine if the act is legitimate in origin
Ask: Is the act, stripped of the prohibited element, lawful? Buy/sell, loan, and lease are legitimate in origin. Adultery, murder, usurpation are not.
If the essential features of the act are lawful, the origin is legitimate.
3
Find the target of the prohibition
Examine whether the prohibition aims at one of the five essential elements (parties, subject matter, object, pillar) or at a subsidiary description (e.g., excess in ribā, a condition in a sale).
Essential elements: contracting parties, subject matter, object, pillar (offer & acceptance).
4
Apply the five-element test
If the prohibition targets an essential element, the contract is void (bāṭil). If all essentials are sound and the prohibition targets a side description, the contract is defective (fāsid).
5
Classify and note effects
Void: no legal effects. Defective: contract is concluded but flawed; can be rectified or may become effective if an impediment prevents rescission.
