الفصل (٥٤) نظرية الفساد: نظرة عامة
النواحي التي يتعلق بها النهي. وعلى هذا حكموا ببطلان عقود الربا، للنهي القرآني فيها بقوله تعالى : ﴿فَإِن لَّمْ تَفْعَلُوا فَأْذَنُوا بِحَرْبٍ مِّنَ اللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ وَإِن تُبْتُمْ فَلَكُمْ رُءُوسُ أَمْوَالِكُمْ لا تَظْلِمُونَ وَلَا تُظْلَمُونَ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢٧٩/٢].
وحكموا كذلك ببطلان العقد المقترن بشرط ممنوع للنهي عنه في السنة النبوية. وقد يبطلون الشرط دون العقد، كما تقدم في نظرية العقود. وذهب الاجتهاد الحنفي إلى أن مجرد النهي عن الفعل لا يدل على عدم مشروعية أصله حتماً؛ بل قد تجتمع مشروعية أصل الفعل مع النهي عنه، كما يظهر من التفصيل التالي:
٤/٥٤ - اختلاف آثار النهي الشرعي باختلاف أسبابه :
النهي
فما نهى عنه الشارع من التصرفات نهياً دون أن ينص على بطلانه نصاً، فإن نتيجة النهي تختلف فيه باختلاف سبب النهي، أي باختلاف نوع المخالفة للنظام الشرعي.
أ - فقد ينهى الشرع عن الفعل لأنه ليس بمشروع من أصله، إذ يكون من المفاسد القبيحة في ذاتها - كالزنى والقتل والغصب.
وفي مثل هذا يعتبر الفعل المنهي عنه باطلاً محضاً لا يفيد صاحبه ثمرة أو منفعة. وعلى هذا لا يثبت في الزنى نسب ولا مهر، ولا يرث القاتل من قتيله كما لا يملك الغاصب المغصوب.
ومن هذا القبيل النهي الوارد في السنة النبوية عن بيع الملاقيح والمضامين كما تقدَّم في نظرية العقود (ر) ف ٤/٤٧ الحاشية)، فإنَّ الشرع لم يعتبرها محلاً صالحاً للعقد. وكذا النهي عن بيع الملامسة والمنابذة ر: ف (٦/٢٧ لأنه لم يعرب عن تراض صحيح. فتكون هذه العقود فاقدة بعض مقوماتها من محل أو ركن. فتعتبر باطلة.
ب - وقد ينهى الشارع عن الفعل المشروع الأصل، فينصرف النهي إلى وصف يستقبحه الشارع فيه ويريد تجريده عنه. ذلك لأن الأصل لما كان مشروعاً لم يبق إلا الوصف هدفاً للنهي. وذلك كالنهي الشرعي عن الربا. فإنَّ عقد الربا هو بيع أو قرض فهو مشروع الأصل. ولكن فيه وصفاً زائداً يسقتبحه الشارع هو اشتماله على فضل بلا عوض. وكذلك النهي عن اشتراط بعض شروط خاصة في عقد البيع والإجارة. فإن عقدهما في أصله مشروع، ولكن الشرط هو وصف غير مشروع. فالعقد المنهي عنه إذا كان من هذا القبيل - أي مشروع الأصل - لا يفيد النهي بطلانه كغير المشروع أصلاً، بل يعتبر منعقداً انعقاداً فاسداً، أي مختلاً في ناحية فرعية منه اختلالاً يجعله مستحقاً للإبطال ما لم يمنع من إبطاله مانع . فإذا منع من إبطاله مانع، كما سنرى استقرَّ اعتباره وثبت حكمه. أما الباطل فإنه لا يترتب عليه شيء من نتائج العقد الصحيح كما تقدم.
وهذا معنى قول الفقهاء في تعريف العقد الفاسد: أنه «هو العقد المشروع بأصله لا بوصفه». بخلاف العقد الباطل، فإنه عندهم غير مشروع بأصله ولا بوصفه كما تقدم في بحث البطلان (ف ١/٥٢)(١) .
(۱) ولتمييز عدم مشروعية أصل العقد عن عدم مشروعية وصفه ينظر إلى مقومات العقد الخمسة وهي العاقدان، والمحل والموضوع والركن (الركن هو الإيجاب والقبول المعبران عن التراضي أو ما يقوم مقامهما في هذا التعبير). فإذا كان النهي منصباً على أحد هذه المقومات ومؤثراً في صلوحها أفاد عدم المشروعية في أصل العقد فيبطل. وإذا كان منصباً على ناحية أخرى فرعية من العقد، وكانت مقوماته هذه كلها سليمة فالعقد حينئذ مشروع الأصل غير مشروع الوصف وهذا يستوجب الفساد لا البطلان.
وعلى هذا، فالنهي عن بيع الملاقيح والمضامين قد تعلّق بالمحل وهو المبيع. والنهي عن بيع الملامسة والمنابذة قد تعلق بالركن وهو التراضي لعدم قيام طريقة المنابذة والملامسة مقام الإيجاب والقبول في نظر الشارع. وبما أن المحل والركن هما من مقومات العقد كان هذا النهي مفيداً عدم مشروعية أصله فيبطل.
Chapter (54) Theory of Invalidity: An Overview.
The aspects to which the prohibition relates. On this basis they ruled the invalidity of usurious contracts, due to the Quranic prohibition in it: His statement, the Exalted: 'If you do not, then take notice of war from Allah and His Messenger. But if you repent, you shall have your capital sums. Deal not unjustly, and you shall not be dealt with unjustly.' [Surah Al-Baqarah 2:279].
And they also ruled the invalidity of a contract coupled with a prohibited condition due to the prohibition in the Prophetic Sunnah. And they may invalidate the condition without the contract, as previously mentioned in the theory of contracts. The Hanafi ijtihad held that mere prohibition of an act does not necessarily indicate the non-legitimacy of its origin; rather, the legitimacy of the origin of the act may coexist with the prohibition, as will appear from the following detail:
4/54 - Variation in the effects of legal prohibition according to its causes:
The prohibition.
As for those transactions that the Lawgiver prohibited without explicitly stating their invalidity, the result of the prohibition varies according to the cause of the prohibition, i.e., according to the type of violation of the legal system.
A - The Lawgiver may prohibit an act because it is not legitimate in origin, being of inherently reprehensible evils - such as adultery, murder, and usurpation.
In such a case, the prohibited act is considered absolutely void, yielding no fruit or benefit to its doer. Thus, in adultery, lineage and dower are not established, the murderer does not inherit from his victim, and the usurper does not own the usurped property.
Of this kind is the prohibition in the Prophetic Sunnah of selling al-malaqīḥ and al-maḍāmīn (unborn animals) as previously mentioned in the theory of contracts (see section 4/47 footnote), for the Law did not consider them valid subject matter for a contract. Likewise, the prohibition of selling by touch (mulāmasah) and by throwing (munābadhah) (see section 6/27) because it did not express valid mutual consent. Thus, these contracts are lacking some of their essential elements, either subject matter or pillar. They are considered void.
B - The Lawgiver may prohibit an act that is legitimate in origin, so the prohibition applies to a description that the Lawgiver deems reprehensible and wishes to remove from it. This is because since the origin is legitimate, nothing remains except the description as the target of prohibition. For example, the legal prohibition of usury. Indeed, the usury contract is a sale or loan, so it is legitimate in origin. But it has an additional description that the Lawgiver deems reprehensible, namely its inclusion of excess without consideration. Likewise, the prohibition of stipulating certain specific conditions in a sale or lease contract. The contracts themselves are legitimate in origin, but the condition is a non-legitimate description. Thus, if the prohibited contract is of this kind - i.e., legitimate in origin - the prohibition does not render it void like something non-legitimate in origin; rather, it is considered a defective (fāsid) conclusion, i.e., impaired in a subsidiary aspect in a way that makes it subject to rescission unless an impediment prevents its rescission. If an impediment prevents its rescission, as we shall see, it becomes established and its ruling is confirmed. As for the void, none of the effects of a valid contract follow from it, as previously.
And this is the meaning of the jurists' definition of the defective contract: that it is 'the contract that is legitimate in its origin but not in its description.' Unlike the void contract, which according to them is not legitimate in its origin nor in its description, as previously discussed in the treatment of voidness (section 1/52).
(1) To distinguish the non-legitimacy of the origin of a contract from the non-legitimacy of its description, one looks at the five essential elements of the contract: the two parties, the subject matter, the object, and the pillar (the pillar is the offer and acceptance expressing mutual consent or what stands in their place in this expression). If the prohibition is directed at one of these essential elements and affects their validity, it indicates non-legitimacy in the origin of the contract, and it becomes void. If it is directed at another, subsidiary aspect of the contract, and all its essential elements are sound, then the contract is legitimate in origin but not in its description, and this necessitates defectiveness, not voidness.
Accordingly, the prohibition of selling al-malaqīḥ and al-maḍāmīn pertains to the subject matter, i.e., the sold item. And the prohibition of selling by touch (mulāmasah) and by throwing (munābadhah) pertains to the pillar, i.e., mutual consent, because the method of munābadhah and mulāmasah does not take the place of offer and acceptance in the view of the Lawgiver. Since the subject matter and the pillar are among the essential elements of the contract, this prohibition indicates the non-legitimacy of its origin, and it becomes void.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical exposition of the Hanafi theory of invalidity (fasād) in contracts, specifically how the effect of a legal prohibition (nahy) varies depending on whether the prohibition targets the essence (aṣl) of the act or a mere description (waṣf). The author, Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, begins by stating that the Hanafi school has ruled that usurious contracts are void (bāṭil) due to the Quranic prohibition in Sūrat al-Baqarah (2:279). Similarly, contracts with prohibited conditions may be void or the condition alone may be void. He then explains the Hanafi position that mere prohibition does not automatically imply that the act itself is illegitimate in essence; it may be legitimate in essence but prohibited due to a secondary description.

The author then details two categories: (A) Acts prohibited because they are inherently evil and not legitimate in origin (e.g., adultery, murder, usurpation). Such acts are absolutely void and produce no legal effects; for example, in adultery no lineage or mahr is established, a murderer does not inherit, and a usurper does not own. Contracts like the sale of unborn animals (al-malāqīḥ wa al-maḍāmīn) or sales by touch or throwing (mulāmasah and munābadhah) are void because they lack a valid subject matter or a valid pillar (offer and acceptance). (B) Acts that are legitimate in origin but prohibited due to a specific description, such as usury (ribā) or prohibited conditions in a sale. In such cases, the contract is not void but defective (fāsid), meaning it is valid in essence but flawed in a subsidiary aspect. It can be rectified or, if an impediment prevents rescission, it may become effective. The Hanafi definition of a fāsid contract is 'the contract that is legitimate in origin but not in description,' whereas a bāṭil contract is not legitimate in origin nor in description.

The author provides a key for distinguishing: one must examine the five essential elements (muqawwimāt) of a contract: the two parties, subject matter, object, and pillar (offer and acceptance). If the prohibition targets one of these, it renders the contract void; if it targets a subsidiary aspect, it renders it defective. He applies this to the examples: the prohibition of selling unborn animals targets the subject matter, and the prohibition of mulāmasah/munābadhah targets the pillar (mutual consent), hence both are void. This analysis is foundational to Hanafi contract law and informs the fatwā process when evaluating transactions.

Key Terms
باطل (bāṭil): عقد غير مشروع بأصله ولا بوصفه، لا يترتب عليه أي أثر
فاسد (fāsid): عقد مشروع بأصله غير مشروع بوصفه، يترتب عليه بعض الآثار
نهي (nahy): طلب الكف عن الفعل على وجه الاستعلاء، وأثره يختلف حسب تعلقه بأصل الفعل أو وصفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory presented, a contract of usury (ribā) is classified as:
A) Bāṭil (void) because it is prohibited in the Quran.
B) Fāsid (defective) because it is a sale or loan in origin but has an extra description of excess without consideration.
C) Valid but sinful.
D) Not a contract at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between void (bāṭil) and defective (fāsid) contracts by examining whether the prohibition targets the essence or a subsidiary description; this distinction is crucial for issuing accurate fatwas.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Distinction: Corrupt vs Void Contracts
Definition and examples of fasid vs batil contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a prohibited contract as corrupt (fasid) or void (batil) by analyzing its five constituents and determining whether the prohibition targets the origin or a description.

Core Idea
A contract prohibited in its origin is void; a contract prohibited in its description is corrupt and potentially rectifiable.

The Hanafi school makes a unique distinction between void (bāṭil) and corrupt (fāsid) contracts, critical for fatwa and judicial rulings. This lesson unpacks the theory behind this distinction using the five constituents of a contract, enabling you to classify any prohibited contract correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five constituents
The contract has five constituents: two contracting parties, subject matter, object, and pillar (offer and acceptance). These define the contract's origin.
2
Determine the target of the prohibition
If the prohibition is directed at one of these constituents, the contract's origin is unlegislated → void (batil). If directed at a subsidiary aspect, the origin remains legislated.
3
Apply to fasid contracts
For fasid contracts (e.g., riba), all constituents are sound. The prohibition targets a subsidiary description (excess without consideration). The contract is corrupt, not void.
4
Check for an impediment to annulment
A fasid contract is subject to annulment unless an impediment (e.g., necessity) prevents it. If so, it becomes effective.
5
Works through examples
Analyze given examples: malaqih/madamin (void due to subject matter), mulamasah/munabadhah (void due to pillar), riba (fasid).

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Prohibitions in Fiqh. [It refers] to a characteristic that the Lawgiver deems reprehensible and wishes to strip it away, because since the origin is legislated, only the characteristic remains as the target of the prohibition. Such as the legal prohibition of riba. For the contract of riba is a sale or loan, so its origin is legislated. But it contains an additional characteristic that the Lawgiver deems reprehensible, namely that it includes an excess without consideration. Similarly, the prohibition of stipulating certain special conditions in contracts of sale and lease; their contracts are legislated in origin, but the condition is an unlegislated characteristic. So a prohibited contract that is of this kind—i.e., its origin is legislated—the prohibition does not render it void like something unlegislated in origin; rather, it is considered concluded as a corrupt conclusion (fasid), meaning defective in a subsidiary aspect, making it subject to annulment unless an impediment prevents annulment. If an impediment prevents annulment, as we shall see, it becomes established and its ruling is confirmed. As for the void (batal) contract, no consequences of a valid contract follow from it, as previously stated. This is the meaning of the fuqaha's definition of the corrupt contract: it is 'the contract whose origin is legislated but not its characteristic.' Unlike the void contract, which according to them is neither legislated in origin nor in characteristic, as earlier in the discussion of nullity. (1) To distinguish between the lack of legislativity of the contract's origin and of its characteristic, one looks at the five constituents of the contract: the two contracting parties, the subject matter, the object, and the pillar (the pillar is offer and acceptance expressing mutual consent or what stands in their place in this expression). If the prohibition is directed at one of these constituents and affects its validity, it entails lack of legislativity in the contract's origin, so it becomes void. And if it is directed at another subsidiary aspect while all these constituents are sound, then the contract is legislated in origin but unlegislated in characteristic, which necessitates corruption (fasad), not nullity. Based on this, the prohibition of selling al-Malaqih and al-Madamin pertained to the subject matter (the sold item). And the prohibition of mulamasah and munabadhah pertained to the pillar, i.e., mutual consent, because the method of munabadhah and mulamasah does not stand in place of offer and acceptance in the view of the Lawgiver. Since the subject matter and the pillar are among the constituents of the contract, this prohibition indicates the lack of legislativity of its origin, so it becomes void. C. And the Lawgiver may prohibit a legislated act that has no violation of its system in its origin or essential attributes, and the cause of the prohibition is purely an external matter, such as the Qur'anic prohibition of sale at the time of the call to Friday prayer. This prohibition does not necessitate nullity or corruption, because the sale—as a civil contract—is complete in its constituents and essential conditions; the prohibition is only for an external reason, namely distraction from the obligatory worship. Its consequence is merely religious prohibition, just as if one engages in business transactions instead of another prayer until its time passes. That is prohibited religiously but does not affect the validity of the contracts he practiced instead of the worship in its time. Similar to this is the prohibition in the prophetic tradition of proposing marriage over the proposal of one's brother, and negotiating over his negotiation unless he permits, and similar prohibitions. So this prohibition on marriage and purchase occurring in such forms does not necessitate nullity or corruption from a civil judicial perspective; rather, it necessitates religious dislike for a moral meaning external to the elements of contract formation. Likewise, the contract of an insane person, for example; the lack of capacity of the contracting party is like the absence of the contracting party. And the contracting party is among the constituents of the contract, so the contract of an insane person is void. This is contrary to the riba contract, because all its constituents are sound—its contracting parties, its subject matter, its pillar, and its object which is a sale or loan. The target of the prohibition is only the excess in one of the compensations, which is a subsidiary aspect beyond the five constituents. Thus the riba contract is legislated in its origin, but the legislative prohibition indicates the lack of legislativity of the excess it includes without consideration, which is among its characteristics, so the contract becomes corrupt (fasid), not void (batal).
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal theory distinguishing between void (bāṭil) and corrupt (fāsid) contracts. The author, Shaykh Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, explains that the key distinction lies in whether the prohibition attacks the origin (aṣl) of the contract or merely a descriptive element (waṣf). The contract's origin is considered legislated if its five fundamental constituents—the two contracting parties, the subject matter (maḥall), the object (mawḍūʿ), and the pillar (rukn: offer and acceptance)—are valid. If a prohibition targets one of these, the contract is void (bāṭil); if it targets a subsidiary aspect, the contract is corrupt (fāsid) and potentially rectifiable. Examples are given: ribā contracts are fāsid because the added excess is a subsidiary aspect, whereas contracts like mulāmasah are bāṭil because they lack proper offer and acceptance. The passage also covers prohibitions with purely external causes (e.g., sale during Friday adhān), which do not affect contract validity but entail religious sin. This theory is fundamental to Hanafi transaction law and fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
العقد الفاسد (al-ʿaqd al-fāsid): العقد المشروع بأصله لا بوصفه، أي أن مقوماته الخمسة سليمة ولكن فيه وصفاً غير مشروع
العقد الباطل (al-ʿaqd al-bāṭil): العقد غير المشروع بأصله ولا بوصفه، لخلل في أحد مقوماته الخمسة
مقومات العقد (muqawwimāt al-ʿaqd): الأركان الأساسية التي يتكون منها العقد: العاقدان، المحل، الموضوع، الركن (الإيجاب والقبول)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory, which of the following contracts would be classified as fasid?
A) Sale of wine
B) Sale with undetermined price
C) Riba contract
D) Marriage without witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract prohibited due to a subsidiary description is fasid and rectifiable, while a contract prohibited in its origin is batil and produces no legal effects.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

External Prohibitions: Religious Sin without Contractual Invalidity
Discussion of external prohibitions (sale during Friday adhan, proposing over brother's proposal) and their effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intrinsic contract defects and external prohibitions, and state the rule that external prohibitions do not render a contract fasid or batil.

Core Idea
Prohibitions that target external factors (like time or competition) do not affect contract validity; they only entail religious sin.

Not every prohibition in fiqh invalidates a contract. Some prohibitions are purely moral, relating to circumstances outside the contract's essential elements. This lesson examines such prohibitions and their Hanafi classification as valid contracts that are sinful to perform.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Is the prohibition based on an external factor (e.g., time, competition) or on the contract's own elements? If external, proceed.
2
Check the contract's constituents
Are the five constituents sound? If yes, the contract is valid in civil law.
3
Determine the ruling
The contract is valid but performing it is sinful (haram li-ghayrihi).
4
Apply to examples
Work through examples: sale during Friday adhan, proposing over brother's proposal, negotiating over brother's negotiation.
5
Contrast with intrinsic prohibitions
Compare with riba (intrinsic description) or mulamasah (intrinsic pillar defect) to solidify the distinction.

إلى وصف يستقبحه الشارع فيه ويريد تجريده عنه ذلك لأن الأصل لما كان مشروعاً لم يبق إلا الوصف هدفاً للنهي. وذلك كالنهي الشرعي عن الربا. فإنَّ عقد الربا هو بيع أو قرض فهو مشروع الأصل. ولكن فيه وصفاً زائداً يسقتبحه الشارع هو اشتماله على فضل بلا عوض. وكذلك النهي عن اشتراط بعض شروط خاصة في عقد البيع والإجارة فإن عقدهما في أصله مشروع، ولكن الشرط هو وصف غير مشروع. فالعقد المنهي عنه إذا كان من هذا القبيل - أي مشروع الأصل - لا يفيد النهي بطلانه كغير المشروع أصلاً، بل يعتبر منعقداً انعقاداً فاسداً، أي مختلاً في ناحية فرعية منه اختلالاً يجعله مستحقاً للإبطال ما لم يمنع من إبطاله مانع . فإذا منع من إبطاله مانع، كما سنرى استقرَّ اعتباره وثبت حكمه. أما الباطل فإنه لا يترتب عليه شيء من نتائج العقد الصحيح كما تقدم .

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Prohibitions in Fiqh. [It refers] to a characteristic that the Lawgiver deems reprehensible and wishes to strip it away, because since the origin is legislated, only the characteristic remains as the target of the prohibition. Such as the legal prohibition of riba. For the contract of riba is a sale or loan, so its origin is legislated. But it contains an additional characteristic that the Lawgiver deems reprehensible, namely that it includes an excess without consideration. Similarly, the prohibition of stipulating certain special conditions in contracts of sale and lease; their contracts are legislated in origin, but the condition is an unlegislated characteristic. So a prohibited contract that is of this kind—i.e., its origin is legislated—the prohibition does not render it void like something unlegislated in origin; rather, it is considered concluded as a corrupt conclusion (fasid), meaning defective in a subsidiary aspect, making it subject to annulment unless an impediment prevents annulment. If an impediment prevents annulment, as we shall see, it becomes established and its ruling is confirmed. As for the void (batal) contract, no consequences of a valid contract follow from it, as previously stated. This is the meaning of the fuqaha's definition of the corrupt contract: it is 'the contract whose origin is legislated but not its characteristic.' Unlike the void contract, which according to them is neither legislated in origin nor in characteristic, as earlier in the discussion of nullity. (1) To distinguish between the lack of legislativity of the contract's origin and of its characteristic, one looks at the five constituents of the contract: the two contracting parties, the subject matter, the object, and the pillar (the pillar is offer and acceptance expressing mutual consent or what stands in their place in this expression). If the prohibition is directed at one of these constituents and affects its validity, it entails lack of legislativity in the contract's origin, so it becomes void. And if it is directed at another subsidiary aspect while all these constituents are sound, then the contract is legislated in origin but unlegislated in characteristic, which necessitates corruption (fasad), not nullity. Based on this, the prohibition of selling al-Malaqih and al-Madamin pertained to the subject matter (the sold item). And the prohibition of mulamasah and munabadhah pertained to the pillar, i.e., mutual consent, because the method of munabadhah and mulamasah does not stand in place of offer and acceptance in the view of the Lawgiver. Since the subject matter and the pillar are among the constituents of the contract, this prohibition indicates the lack of legislativity of its origin, so it becomes void. C. And the Lawgiver may prohibit a legislated act that has no violation of its system in its origin or essential attributes, and the cause of the prohibition is purely an external matter, such as the Qur'anic prohibition of sale at the time of the call to Friday prayer. This prohibition does not necessitate nullity or corruption, because the sale—as a civil contract—is complete in its constituents and essential conditions; the prohibition is only for an external reason, namely distraction from the obligatory worship. Its consequence is merely religious prohibition, just as if one engages in business transactions instead of another prayer until its time passes. That is prohibited religiously but does not affect the validity of the contracts he practiced instead of the worship in its time. Similar to this is the prohibition in the prophetic tradition of proposing marriage over the proposal of one's brother, and negotiating over his negotiation unless he permits, and similar prohibitions. So this prohibition on marriage and purchase occurring in such forms does not necessitate nullity or corruption from a civil judicial perspective; rather, it necessitates religious dislike for a moral meaning external to the elements of contract formation. Likewise, the contract of an insane person, for example; the lack of capacity of the contracting party is like the absence of the contracting party. And the contracting party is among the constituents of the contract, so the contract of an insane person is void. This is contrary to the riba contract, because all its constituents are sound—its contracting parties, its subject matter, its pillar, and its object which is a sale or loan. The target of the prohibition is only the excess in one of the compensations, which is a subsidiary aspect beyond the five constituents. Thus the riba contract is legislated in its origin, but the legislative prohibition indicates the lack of legislativity of the excess it includes without consideration, which is among its characteristics, so the contract becomes corrupt (fasid), not void (batal).
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal theory distinguishing between void (bāṭil) and corrupt (fāsid) contracts. The author, Shaykh Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, explains that the key distinction lies in whether the prohibition attacks the origin (aṣl) of the contract or merely a descriptive element (waṣf). The contract's origin is considered legislated if its five fundamental constituents—the two contracting parties, the subject matter (maḥall), the object (mawḍūʿ), and the pillar (rukn: offer and acceptance)—are valid. If a prohibition targets one of these, the contract is void (bāṭil); if it targets a subsidiary aspect, the contract is corrupt (fāsid) and potentially rectifiable. Examples are given: ribā contracts are fāsid because the added excess is a subsidiary aspect, whereas contracts like mulāmasah are bāṭil because they lack proper offer and acceptance. The passage also covers prohibitions with purely external causes (e.g., sale during Friday adhān), which do not affect contract validity but entail religious sin. This theory is fundamental to Hanafi transaction law and fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
نهي خارجي (nahy khārijī): النهي الذي يتعلق بأمر خارج عن مقومات العقد وشرائطه الذاتية، كالبيع وقت الأذان
حرمة دينية (ḥurma dīniyya): التحريم الذي لا يترتب عليه أثر في صحة العقد من الناحية المدنية، بل إثم فقط

A) Batil (void)
B) Fasid (corrupt)

Key Takeaway
External prohibitions (e.g., time of prayer, competition) do not affect the validity of a contract; they only create religious responsibility.

Hanafi Classification of Prohibitions: Fasad vs Butlan
Explains the Hanafi distinction between corruption and invalidity due to prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of prohibitions to determine whether a contract is valid, corrupt (fāsid), or void (bāṭil).

Core Idea
Not every prohibition invalidates a contract; only those affecting essential elements cause nullity.

In Hanafi contract law, the effect of a legal prohibition (nahy) on a transaction depends on the object of the prohibition. This lesson unpacks the landmark distinction between corruption (fasād) and invalidity (buṭlān). You will see why selling during Friday adhan is sinful but valid, while a riba contract is corrupt but not void, and a madman's contract is outright invalid. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for issuing sound fatwas on contemporary transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of prohibition
The text distinguishes prohibitions that are external (do not affect the contract), those that attack subsidiary attributes (cause fasad), and those that attack essential elements (cause butlan).
2
Map each example to its category
Friday adhan sale (external) → valid; riba (subsidiary) → fasid; madman's contract (essential) → batil.
3
Determine the legal consequence
For each category, state the effect: sin vs enforceability vs nullity.
4
Apply the framework to a new scenario
Use the riba example to see how a corrupt contract can be corrected by removing the excess.

Chapter 54: Theory of Corruption: General Overview. C - The Law sometimes prohibits a permissible act which in its origin or essential attributes has no violation of its system, and the cause of the prohibition is a purely external matter, like the Quranic prohibition of selling during the Friday adhan. This prohibition does not cause invalidity or corruption, because the sale - insofar as it is a civil contract - is complete in its essential elements and conditions; the prohibition is for an external meaning, namely being distracted by the contract from the obligatory worship, so its effect is merely religious prohibition, just as if a person occupied himself with his transactions instead of another prayer until its time passed. That is religiously unlawful but does not affect the validity of the contracts he performed instead of the worship in its time. Of this kind is the prohibition in the Sunnah of a person proposing to a woman already proposed to by his brother, or negotiating a sale while his brother is negotiating, unless he permits him, and similar prohibitions. Thus, these prohibitions in marriage and purchase occurring likewise do not cause invalidity or corruption from the civil judicial perspective, but only cause religious dislike for an ethical meaning external to the elements of the contract. Similarly, the contract of a madman, for example; the lack of capacity of the contracting party is like the absence of the contracting party. And the contracting party is among the essential elements of the contract, so the madman's contract is invalid (batil). This is unlike the contract of riba because its essential elements are all sound from its two parties, its subject matter, its pillar, and its subject which is a sale or loan. The object of prohibition is only the excess in one of the two countervalues, which is a subsidiary aspect additional to the five essential elements; so the riba contract is valid in its origin, but the legislative prohibition indicates the lack of validity of the excess it contains that is without consideration, which is among its attributes; so the contract becomes corrupt (fasid) and not invalid (batil). ... This is the gist of the view of the usul scholars and jurists on the basis of the theory of corruption adopted by Hanafi ijtihad, which we have presented here with simplification and clarification. Al-Qarafi al-Maliki said in his book al-Furuq about the basis of this distinction between corruption and invalidity in Abu Hanifa's theory: 'It is good fiqh.' The Maliki and Shafi'i schools, contrary to the Hanbali, have in some places followed the Hanafi distinction between a prohibition that corrupts the essence of the act and a prohibition related to an external matter that does not corrupt it, but in any case, like other schools, they did not distinguish between corruption and invalidity in meaning and consequences. ... Thus it becomes clear what was previously indicated, that the corrupt contract (al-'aqd al-fasid) in the view of the jurists is considered in a rank between invalidity and validity. It is neither invalid, devoid of consideration, nor fully valid, when the contract fulfills the conditions of its pillar and all its essential elements, which are the basic necessary conditions for its formation, but it is contrary in that it contains what it should be free of, like prohibited conditions, or is void of what it must contain, like the knowability of the subject matter; for if it is not known, the contract becomes corrupt. Third: Acts in which corruption differs from invalidity: 6/54 - We saw in the previous discussion that invalidity occurs in all types...
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi theory of corruption (fasād) and its distinction from invalidity (buṭlān). The author, Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, begins by categorizing types of prohibitions in transactions. He explains that not every prohibition renders a contract invalid. Using the example of selling during the Friday adhan (Qur'anic prohibition), he shows that the prohibition is external—the act itself is otherwise valid. The sin is in neglecting prayer, not in the contract's essence. Similarly, the Prophetic prohibition against outbidding or out-proposing a brother (interfering with an ongoing negotiation) only makes the transaction disliked (makrūh) religiously; it remains legally valid. In contrast, a mad person's contract is invalid (bāṭil) because capacity is an essential element (rukn). For ribā (usury), the contract's essentials are sound, but the extra amount (faḍl) is a subsidiary attribute; thus the contract is corrupt (fāsid)—valid in principle but defective due to the prohibited excess. The Hanafi school holds that a corrupt contract occupies a middle ground: it is neither completely void nor completely valid. The author then notes that the Mālikī and Shāfiʿī schools, unlike the Ḥanbalī, have sometimes followed the Hanafi distinction in practice but do not formally separate fasād and buṭlān in terminology and legal effects. The passage concludes by reiterating that a corrupt contract fulfills the pillars and essential conditions but violates something that should be absent (e.g., prohibited condition) or lacks something that should be present (e.g., knowledge of the subject matter). This passage is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract law, emphasizing that the cause (ʿilla) of the prohibition determines the legal effect on the contract.

Key Terms
فساد (fasād): A contract that fulfills all essential elements but has a defect in a subsidiary attribute that the prohibition targets
بطلان (buṭlān): A contract lacking an essential element (e.g., capacity, subject matter), thus legally void and without effect
نهي (nahy): A legal prohibition that may affect a contract's validity depending on the object of prohibition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory of corruption (fasād), what is the legal effect of selling during the Friday adhan?
A) The sale is invalid (bāṭil) because of the Quranic prohibition.
B) The sale is corrupt (fāsid) but can be corrected.
C) The sale is valid but the act is sinful; the prohibition is external to the contract.
D) The sale is valid and recommended as it helps people avoid the prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely classifies prohibitions into three categories: those that invalidate (essential), those that corrupt (subsidiary), and those that are merely sinful (external); the mufta bihi position applies this tripartite distinction.

Theory of Fasād: Definition, Scope, and Distinction from Buṭlān
Explaining the Hanafi theory of fasād, its definition, scope, and a proposed rule for its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of a fāsid contract in Hanafi law, identify its scope using the ḍābiṭ rule, and apply it to distinguish fasād from buṭlān in given scenarios.

Core Idea
The Hanafi fasād is a middle category applicable only to financial contracts with mutual obligations.

This passage introduces the unique Hanafi doctrine of fasād (invalidity) as a middle category between validity (ṣiḥḥa) and nullity (buṭlān). Unlike other schools, Hanafi jurists distinguish between a prohibited condition that corrupts the essence and one that does not, allowing for rectification. The author then derives a rule limiting this distinction to financial contracts that create mutual obligations or transfer ownership.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of al-ʿaqd al-fāsid
Read the passage to grasp that a fāsid contract is one that fulfills its essential pillars but contains a prohibited condition or lacks a necessary attribute (e.g., knowledge of the subject matter). It is neither fully valid nor entirely void.
The definition is central to the theory.
2
Distinguish between fasād and buṭlān in meaning and scope
Note that buṭlān applies to all defective transactions, but fasād is narrower. The passage emphasizes that the distinction is substantive: a fāsid contract can be rectified, whereas a bāṭil one cannot.
Some schools treat both as synonymous, but the Hanafi view is more nuanced.
3
Identify the scope: the ḍābiṭ rule
The author presents a rule (ḍābiṭ) that the fasād/buṭlān distinction only applies to financial contracts creating mutual obligations or transferring ownership. Acts of worship and unilateral dispositions are excluded.
This rule is derived by induction from Hanafi texts.
4
Apply the rule to examples and exceptions
Work through the furūʿ (subcases) provided: sale of unknown item (fāsid), lease with impermissible condition (fāsid). Also note the istithnāʾ (exception) for acts of worship, where terms are used interchangeably.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is whether the contract can be corrected.

ثالثاً : التصرفات التي يفترق فيها الفساد عن البطلان : ٦/٥٤ - رأينا في البحث السابق أن البطلان يجري في جميع أنواع التصرفات من فعلية ،وقولية عقدية كانت أو غير عقدية. (ر: ٣/٥١ - 60/01) أما الفساد فمجاله أضيق من ذلك، فإنَّ التمييز بين البطلان والفساد لا يشمل جميع التصرفات بل إن منها ما ليس له إلا مرتبتان الصحة والبطلان، فلا فرق فيه بين فاسد وباطل في عدم الاعتبار الشرعي. غير أن فقهاء المذهب الحنفي الذي أسس نظرية الفساد إلى جانب البطلان في نطاق المؤيدات الشرعية لم يضع فقهاؤه إلى اليوم ضابطاً يقيم حداً واضحاً لما يجري فيه هذا التمييز بين البطلان والفساد وما لا يجري، ولكنهم قد فصلوا أسباب الفساد وأحكامه تفصيلاً مستوعباً في الفصول التي ميزوا بين بطلانها ،وفسادها كالبيع والإجارة والهبة والمخارجة وغيرها : وهم يصرحون في بعض الفصول الأخرى أنها لا فرق فيها بين فاسد وباطل كما في العبادات من صلاة وصيام وحج وإيتاء الزكاة. فإنها جميعاً تكون إما صحيحة مبرئة لذمة المكلف، أو غير صحيحة فلا يسقط بها الواجب، وفي هذه الحال يقال إنها فاسدة أو باطلة بمعنى واحد.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Sharia Affirmations in Fiqh. And if we equate the outcomes in all cases of prohibition without considering the cause and purpose of the prohibition, we would be equating a complete, sound contractual essence with a defective one. 54/5 - This is the gist of the view of the usul scholars and fuqaha regarding the basis of the theory of invalidity (fasād) adopted by the Hanafi ijtihad, presented here with simplification and clarification. (1) And al-Qarāfi al-Mālikī said in his book 'al-Furūq' regarding the basis of this distinction between fasād and buṭlān in Abu Hanifa's theory: 'It is good fiqh.' And the Mālikī and Shāfiʿī ijtihads, unlike the Ḥanbalī, have followed the Hanafi distinction in some places but not others regarding a prohibition that corrupts the essence of the act and a prohibition related to an external matter that does not corrupt it, but in any case they, like other ijtihads, did not distinguish between fasād and buṭlān in meaning and outcomes. (Ref: 'al-Furūq' by al-Qarāfi, vol. 2, al-Farq 70). Thus it becomes clear from what was previously indicated that the invalid contract (al-ʿaqd al-fāsid) in the view of the fuqaha is considered an intermediate rank between buṭlān and ṣiḥḥa. It is neither the null and void (bāṭil) that is entirely disregarded nor the fully valid (ṣaḥīḥ) that is completely considered. This occurs when the contract fulfills the conditions of its pillar and all its essential elements, which are the basic necessary conditions for its formation, but it is contrary in terms of containing something it should be free from, such as prohibited conditions, or in terms of lacking something it should include, such as the knowledge of the subject matter of the contract. For if it is not known, the contract becomes invalid. Third: Transactions in which fasād differs from buṭlān: 6/54 - We saw in the previous discussion that buṭlān applies to all types of transactions, whether actual or verbal, contractual or non-contractual. (Ref: 3/51 - 60/01). As for fasād, its scope is narrower than that; the distinction between buṭlān and fasād does not include all transactions, but rather some of them have only two ranks: validity and nullity. There is no difference between invalid (fāsid) and void (bāṭil) in terms of lack of legal consideration. However, the fuqaha of the Hanafi school, which established the theory of fasād alongside buṭlān in the realm of sharia affirmations, have not to this day established a rule (ḍābiṭ) that sets a clear boundary for where this distinction between buṭlān and fasād applies and where it does not. But they have detailed the causes and rulings of fasād comprehensively in the chapters where they distinguished between buṭlān and fasād, such as sale, lease, gift, muzāraʿa, and others. And they explicitly state in some other chapters that there is no difference between invalid and void, as in acts of worship like prayer, fasting, hajj, and payment of zakah. For all of these are either valid, discharging the obligation from the responsible person, or invalid, so the duty is not discharged, and in this case it is said they are invalid (fāsida) or void (bāṭila) with the same meaning. (Ref: Radd al-Muḥtār, vol. 1, beginning of chapter on invalidators of prayer). 7/54 - The rule (ḍābiṭ) for where the theory of fasād applies and where it does not. Now, by studying the texts of the fuqaha and usul scholars and following their discussions on the distinction between buṭlān and fasād in various fiqh chapters related to civil rulings, the following rule appears to us regarding the locus of this distinction: The distinction between fasād and buṭlān applies only to financial contracts that create mutual obligations or transfer ownership. (a) Entering into this rule are contracts of sale, lease, pledge, assignment, settlement regarding property, muzāraʿa, partition, partnership, sharecropping, and the like, because they create mutual obligations.
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Hanafi theory of fasād (invalidity) and its distinction from buṭlān (nullity). The author begins by emphasizing that merely equating outcomes of all prohibitions without examining the cause and purpose leads to conflating a sound contract with a defective one. He then summarizes the core of the Hanafi view, noting that al-Qarāfi praised it as 'good fiqh.' The Mālikī and Shāfiʿī schools, while sometimes following the Hanafi distinction, did not consistently separate fasād and buṭlān in meaning and results. The passage then defines a fāsid contract: it is intermediate between buṭlān and ṣiḥḥa—neither entirely void nor fully valid. This occurs when the essential pillars are present but a prohibited condition or missing requirement (like knowledge of the subject matter) exists. The author then discusses the scope: buṭlān applies to all types of transactions, but fasād is narrower. The Hanafi scholars have not formulated a comprehensive rule (ḍābiṭ), but by induction, the distinction only applies to financial contracts that create mutual obligations or transfer ownership. Examples include sale, lease, pledge, and partnership. Acts of worship are excluded; there, invalid acts are simply called bāṭil or fāsid interchangeably. The passage thus provides a clear framework for understanding when a contract is merely fāsid (and can be corrected) versus bāṭil (void ab initio).

Key Terms
العقد الفاسد (al-ʿaqd al-fāsid): An intermediate category between valid and void; the contract has its essential pillars but contains a defect such as a prohibited condition or lack of necessary attribute (like knowledge of subject matter).
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Nullity; the contract or act is void ab initio, having no legal effect.
الصحة (al-ṣiḥḥa): Validity; the act is legally sound and produces its intended effects.
الضابط (al-ḍābiṭ): A legal rule derived by induction limiting the application of the fasād/buṭlān distinction to financial contracts that create mutual obligations or transfer ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which of the following contracts does the distinction between fasād and buṭlān apply?
A) Prayer (ṣalāh) with a missing essential condition
B) Sale (bayʿ) with a prohibited condition
C) Fasting (ṣawm) with an invalidating act
D) Zakah when payment is delayed

Key Takeaway
The fasād/buṭlān distinction is a unique Hanafi doctrine that preserves contractual validity where possible, applying only to financial mutual obligations.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

General Rule of Fasad and Its Scope in Hanafi Fiqh
Explaining the scope of fasad and listing exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that fasad only applies to contracts transferring ownership and identify which transactions fall outside this rule, assessing the mufta bihi position for each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, fasad is a middle state only for ownership-transferring contracts; all others are either valid or void.

This passage establishes a foundational distinction in Hanafi mu'amalat: the concept of fasad (invalidity) as a third state between sahih (valid) and batil (void) is reserved exclusively for contracts that transfer ownership, such as sale, loan, and gift. Understanding this rule is crucial for any mufti, as misclassifying a transaction as fasid when it should be batil (or vice versa) leads to incorrect legal consequences. The passage then delineates four categories of exceptions where fasad does not exist, and critically examines classical enumerations of fasid transactions—including a correction of the claim that fasad equals butlan in certain contracts. Finally, it shows how this theory was applied analogically to litigation by the Hanafi jurist Ali Haidar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of fasad
Extract from the text the core principle: fasad is a meaningful intermediate state only in contracts that transfer ownership (like sale, gift, loan). In all other transactions, there are only two states: validity or voidness.
2
List the four exceptions to the rule
The text enumerates four categories of actions that exit from the rule: actual acts, unilateral verbal acts (divorce, manumission, etc.), non-financial contracts (marriage, agency, etc.), and financial contracts without mutual obligations (deposit, loan for use). Note that some of these may be affected by fasad when they become analogous to ownership-transferring contracts.
3
Analyze the special case of marriage
Read the footnote carefully: an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) may produce effects like lineage and dower due to shubha, while a void marriage (nikah batil) produces none. This is a crucial nuance for iftaa. Understand the criteria for shubha.
4
Critique the classical enumeration
The text notes that some Hanafi jurists listed 21 transactions affected by fasad and equated fasad with butlan in agency, guardianship, endowment, cancellation, and currency exchange. The author rejects this, stating the distinction must be upheld. Identify which items are correctly or incorrectly included according to the author.
5
Apply the theory to litigation via qiyas
Consider Ali Haidar's application: lawsuits (da'wa) are divided into valid (sahih), invalid (fasid), and void (batil). Understand how the same tripartite division is analogically extended to a non-contractual area, and what conditions make a lawsuit valid or invalid.

العلامة علي حيدر قد أجرى نظرية الفساد هذه في الدعوى قياساً على العقود. فقسم الدعوى في كتابه أصول استماع الدعوى» إلى ثلاثة أنواع :
۱ - دعوى صحيحة وهي الدعوى المستوفية جميع شرائطها وتتضمن طلباً مشروعاً، كطلب ثمن مبيع أو استرداد مغصوب. وهذه يترتب عليها سير المحكمة في إجراءاتها من تكليف الخصم بالجواب عنها، وتكليف المدعى بإثبات مدعاه إذا أنكر خصمه إلخ...
۲ - وفاسدة، وهي الدعوى المستوفية شرائطها الأساسية بحيث تكون صحيحة من حيث الأصل، ولكنها مختلة في بعض أوصافها الخارجية
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Support in Fiqh. The contract of loan and gift also enter, because they transfer ownership. So all of that, in which its fasad is distinguished from its butlan, is considered fasad as contracted. B - And all the following other actions exit from this rule: A - Actual actions absolutely. 2 - Verbal actions that are not contracts, but from solitary will actions like divorce, manumission, endowment, release, suretyship, and also acknowledgment. 3 - Non-financial contracts like marriage, agency, guardianship, and arbitration, for agency, guardianship, and arbitration are delegation contracts. 4 - Financial contracts that do not create mutual obligations and do not transfer ownership like deposit and loan for use. So much so that the jurists do not conceive of fasad in loan for use except when it is for compensation, then it takes the ruling of hire. So all these types of actions that exit from that rule have only two degrees: existence and non-existence, or validity and nullity, and there is no third degree of fasad between them. Rather, their fasad and their butlan are one in meaning in indicating their lack of legal existence in the view of the Law (1). (1) Note here what was previously explained in the discussion of nullity that an invalid marriage may entail some effects of a valid marriage like lineage, dower, and waiting period if intercourse follows it, because there is considered in it a sufficient ambiguity to establish lineage, which the Law is lenient in establishing as a precaution, and the dower and waiting period follow that (see Fath al-Qadir 4/53). This effect is what led some jurists to distinguish between an invalid marriage and a void marriage, not from the perspective of formation or not, for both are not formed, but from the perspective of establishing these effects or not. So the invalid marriage according to them is that which may contain an ambiguity of permitting enjoyment, with which lineage, dower, and waiting period are established even if the marriage is not formed, like marriage without witnesses and marriage of a woman in her waiting period before its expiry, and a man marrying his divorced wife after three divorces before the occurrence of the causes of her new lawful status. As for the void marriage, it is that which has no ambiguity at all, as if a man marries one of his mahrams, like his daughter or sister, knowing the prohibition, then it is like pure fornication, indeed more heinous, so no effect of lineage or dower is established, and the punishment of the fixed penalty is obligatory according to the most preponderant opinion in the madhhab (see: Radd al-Muhtar in the chapter of dower 2/350-351, and in the chapter of the fixed penalty for fornication 3/353-354). [PAGE_695] Section (54) Theory of Fasad: General Overview. The jurists have enumerated in some contexts the actions that are affected by fasad, an enumeration that varies in increase and decrease, reaching twenty-one, and they versified them, and some declared the equivalence of the meaning of fasad and butlan in agency, guardianship, endowment, cancellation, and currency exchange (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, chapter of dower in marriage 2/352-354). However, we note two observations: 1 - They did not cover all that in which fasad and butlan are equivalent. 2 - Their counting of cancellation and currency exchange among that in which fasad and butlan are equivalent is not accepted. Rather, the difference between al-fasid and al-batil is what is in accordance with the principles. Observation 8/54: The scholar Ali Haidar applied this theory of fasad to litigation by analogy to contracts. He divided litigation in his book 'Usul Istima' al-Da'wa' into three types: 1 - Valid litigation, which is that fulfilling all its conditions and contains a lawful request, like requesting the price of a sold item or recovery of a usurped item. This entails the court proceeding in its procedures from requiring the opponent to answer, requiring the plaintiff to prove his claim if his opponent denies, etc. 2 - Invalid litigation, which is that fulfilling its essential conditions such that it is valid in principle, but defective in some of its external attributes.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the Hanafi theory of legal invalidity (fasad) and its scope. The author first states the general rule: fasad (invalidity) is only conceivable in contracts that transfer ownership, such as sale, loan, and gift. Then he lists exceptions: actual actions, unilateral statements (like divorce, manumission), non-financial contracts (marriage, agency), and financial contracts that do not create mutual obligations or transfer ownership (like deposit). In these exceptions, there is no intermediate state of fasad; they are either valid or void (batil). The footnote elaborates on the special case of marriage: an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) may produce some effects like lineage and dower if intercourse occurs due to the presence of a plausible legal ambiguity (shubha), while a void marriage (nikah batil) produces no effects and incurs the fixed penalty. This distinction is based on the effects, not on formation. The author then notes that classical jurists enumerated up to twenty-one transactions affected by fasad, but some claimed equivalence of fasad and butlan in agency, guardianship, endowment, cancellation, and currency exchange. The author critiques this, stating that not all cases were covered and that cancellation and currency exchange should not be considered equivalent; they maintain a distinction. Finally, a note on Ali Haidar's application of this theory to lawsuits, classifying them as valid, invalid (fasid), or void (batil) by analogy to contracts.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): حالة وسطى بين الصحة والبطلان في العقود الناقلة للملكية، حيث يكون العقد منعقداً لكنه معيب
البطلان (al-bultan): عدم انعقاد التصرف أصلاً، فلا أثر له شرعاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is correct regarding the Hanafi treatment of the contract of loan for use (i'arah)?
A) I'arah is always subject to the three states: sahih, fasid, batil.
B) I'arah is only sahih or batil, and even when compensated, it remains so.
C) I'arah is only sahih or batil, but if it is for compensation, it takes the ruling of ijara, allowing fasad.
D) I'arah is batil if defective, as fasad does not apply at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of fasad is a precise tool: it only applies to contracts that transfer ownership, and a mufti must never confuse it with butlan in those contracts, nor extend it to the exceptions without proper analogy.

Technical Definition of Fasad in Hanafi Contract Law
Analyzing the Hanafi definition of a defective contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a defective contract (al-'aqd al-fasid) and distinguish it from a void contract (batil), and identify the three components of the refined definition.

Core Idea
A defective contract is concluded but voidable due to a subsidiary flaw in its shariah compliance.

In Hanafi contract law, the distinction between void (batil) and voidable (fasid) is fundamental. This passage examines the technical definition of fasad, starting from the classical formulation and then offering a refined definition that clarifies the nature, cause, and effect of defectiveness. As a mufti or legal analyst, understanding this definition is crucial for correctly classifying contractual defects and issuing proper rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its classical definition
Locate the term 'fasad' and note the classical definition cited by the author: 'العقد المشروع بأصله لا بوصفه' (the contract legitimate in its essence but not in its description). Understand that this definition only points to the cause of fasad.
This classical definition is from earlier Hanafi jurists who established the theory.
2
Critique the classical definition
Observe the author's critique: the definition does not give a clear picture of the reality of fasad, only its cause, and contains ambiguity. This motivates the need for a refined definition.
The author considers the classical definition insufficient for practical application.
3
Analyze the proposed new definition
Read the author's new definition: 'اختلال في العقد بمخالفة نظامه الشرعي في ناحية فرعية متممة يجعله مستحقاً للفسخ' (a disruption in the contract that contradicts its shariah system in a subsidiary, completing aspect, making it subject to rescission). Break it into three parts: the flaw, the subsidiary aspect, and the effect.
This definition is more comprehensive, covering cause, nature, and consequence.
4
Understand the three distinguishing features
The author lists three advantages: (1) it depicts the reality of fasad as a notional flaw, (2) it identifies the effect as voidability (subject to rescission), and (3) it implies that the defective contract is concluded (منعقد) – meaning it produces some temporary effects.
Point (3) is critical: a fasid contract is not void ab initio; it exists until rescinded.
5
Distinguish fasad from butlan
Contrast the definition: a fasid contract violates a subsidiary rule, while a batil contract violates an essential pillar or condition. Confirm that fasad leads to voidability, not automatic nullity.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools may treat both as void.

ونرى أنَّ هذا التعريف لا يعطي صورة واضحة عن حقيقة معنى الفساد، وإنما يكشف عن سببه فقط، كما أن فيه غموضاً يورث كثيراً من الاشتباه. وبناءً على ما تقدم يمكننا أن نعرف الفساد في العقد بمعناه المقصود في اصطلاح الفقه الحنفي تعريفاً آخر بأنه: هو اختلال العقد في المخالف لنظامه الشرعي في ناحية فرعية متممة يجعله مستحقاً للفسخ. وهذا التعريف بعدما سلف من بيان أصبح واضحاً. وهو يمتاز : ١ - بأنه يصور حقيقة الفساد التي هي خلل اعتباري في العقد، ويشير إلى سببه الموجب له وهو مخالفة نظام التعاقد في ناحية فرعية . ٢ - بأنه يصف تأثير الفساد في العقد بأنه يجعله مستحقاً للفسخ والإبطال، فيشير إلى نتيجة الفساد التي سنبحث عنها قريباً.

Fourth: The Technical Definition of Fasad
9/54 - We have indicated previously that the Hanafi jurists who established the theory of fasad alongside butlan in civil transactions define the defective contract as: the contract that is legitimate in its essence but not in its description. (See: F 7/54)
We see that this definition does not give a clear picture of the true meaning of fasad; rather, it only reveals its cause, and it contains ambiguity that leads to much confusion. Based on the preceding, we can define fasad in the contract in its intended meaning in Hanafi terminology with another definition: it is a disruption in the contract that contradicts its shariah system in a subsidiary, completing aspect, making it subject to rescission. This definition, after the preceding explanation, has become clear. It is distinguished by: 1. It depicts the reality of fasad, which is a notional flaw in the contract, and points to its causing factor, which is contravening the contractual system in a subsidiary aspect. 2. It describes the effect of fasad on the contract as making it subject to rescission and annulment, thus pointing to the result of fasad, which we will discuss shortly.
3. It indicates that the defective contract is concluded. That is by way of describing it as...
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'fasad' (defectiveness) in Hanafi contract law. The author begins by citing the classical Hanafi definition: a defective contract is 'legitimate in its essence but not in its description' (العقد المشروع بأصله لا بوصفه). However, he criticizes this as only indicating the cause of fasad, not its reality. He then proposes a more precise definition: a contract is defective when it has a flaw in a subsidiary, complementary aspect that violates the shariah system, making it voidable (مستحق للفسخ). The author then highlights three advantages of his definition: (1) it describes the true nature of fasad as a conceptual flaw, (2) it identifies the effect as voidability, and (3) it implies that the defective contract is nonetheless concluded (منعقد) – meaning it produces some temporary effects until rescinded. This aligns with the Hanafi distinction between fasad (voidable) and butlan (void ab initio).

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): A notional flaw in a contract due to violating a subsidiary shariah rule, making it voidable.
العقد الفاسد (al-'aqd al-fasid): A contract legitimate in origin but defective in a subsidiary aspect, subject to rescission.

Key Takeaway
A defective (fasid) contract in Hanafi law is concluded and voidable, not void ab initio, distinguishing it from a batil contract.

Defining Fasad (Invalidity) in Hanafi Contract Theory
Defining fasad and its distinction from butlan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasad and distinguish it from butlan by identifying its three key features.

Core Idea
Fasad is a defect in a contract that violates Shari'ah in a subsidiary aspect, making it subject to rescission.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents a refined definition of fasad (invalidity) in Hanafi contract theory. The author critiques the classical definition and offers a clearer one that highlights the nature, cause, and effect of fasad. Understanding this definition is crucial for the mufti to correctly classify contracts and advise on remedies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classical definition and its weakness
The author states that Hanafi jurists define a fasid contract as 'the contract that is legitimate in its essence, not in its description.' He criticizes this definition for being unclear and only revealing the cause, not the essence of fasad.
This classical definition lacks precision and causes confusion.
2
Extract the author's refined definition
The author proposes: 'Fasad is a defect in the contract that violates its Shari'i system in a subsidiary, complementary aspect, making it subject to rescission.' This definition includes the cause, the nature, and the effect.
Key components: defect (ikhtilal), violation in subsidiary aspect (mukhalafa fi nahiya far'iyya), consequence (mustahiqq li al-faskh).
3
Break down the three distinguished features
The author highlights three features: 1) It depicts fasad as a conceptual defect and points to its cause (violation in a subsidiary aspect). 2) It describes the effect as making the contract subject to rescission. 3) It implies the contract is concluded (mun'aqid) because rescission applies only to existing contracts.
These features together provide a complete picture.
4
Understand the nuance: rescission is not always obligatory
The author notes that entitlement to rescission does not mean rescission is mandatory in all cases; an impediment (mani') may prevent it in certain circumstances. This flexibility is a hallmark of the Hanafi theory.
This nuance anticipates cases where the contract has been executed or other factors override rescission.

الفصل (٥٤) نظرية الفساد: نظرة عامة
الدعوى خاضعة لرسوم مالية ومراسيم قانونية وردها يضيع على المدعي تكاليف وجهوداً وزمناً.
فيجب التمييز في الدعاوى غير الصحيحة بين دعوى مشروعة في أصلها قابلة للتصحيح فتكون فاسدة فساداً، ويكلف صاحبها تصحيحها، وبين دعوى غير مشروعة أصلاً لفقدان شريطة أساسية، فتكون باطلة بطلاناً. وبناءً على ذلك تضاف الدعوى إلى زمرة ما تجري فيه نظرية الفساد وتعتبر مستثناة من الضابط الذي نصبناه فإنها من التصرفات القولية غير ذلك يميز فيها بين الفساد والبطلان.

باستحقاق الفسخ، إذ لا يستحق الفسخ إلا ما كان منعقداً ذا وجود اعتباري قائم في نظر الشرع .
هذا، وإن استحقاق الفسخ لا يفيد معنى تحتيم الفسخ في جميع الأحوال، بل يتلائم هذا التعبير مع الحالات التي يمتنع فيها فسخ العقد الفاسد شرعاً بمانع في بعض الأحوال، كما سنرى في بحث نتيجة الفساد.

Chapter (54) The Theory of Invalidity: An Overview. The lawsuit is subject to financial fees and legal procedures, and its rejection wastes costs, efforts, and time for the plaintiff. Therefore, in irregular lawsuits, a distinction must be made between a lawsuit that is legitimate in its origin and capable of correction, thus being invalid (fasid) and its owner is required to correct it, and a lawsuit that is illegitimate from the start due to the loss of a fundamental condition, thus being void (batil). Accordingly, the lawsuit is added to the category where the theory of invalidity applies and is considered excepted from the principle we established, for it is among verbal acts—other than that, a distinction is made between invalidity and nullity. Fourth: The Technical Definition of Invalidity. 9/54 - We previously indicated that the jurists of the Hanafi school, who established the theory of invalidity alongside nullity in civil transactions, define the invalid contract as: 'the contract that is legitimate in its essence, not in its description' (cf. para 7/54). We see that this definition does not give a clear picture of the reality of the meaning of invalidity, but only reveals its cause, and it also contains ambiguity that gives rise to much confusion. Based on the foregoing, we can define invalidity in a contract according to its intended meaning in Hanafi juristic terminology with another definition: it is a defect in the contract that violates its shari'i system in a subsidiary, complementary aspect, making it subject to rescission. This definition, after the preceding explanation, has become clear. It is distinguished: 1 - by depicting the reality of invalidity, which is a conceptual defect in the contract, and pointing to its causative reason, which is the violation of the contractual system in a subsidiary aspect. 2 - by describing the effect of invalidity on the contract as making it subject to rescission and nullification, thus pointing to the result of invalidity which we will soon discuss. 3 - by indicating that the invalid contract is concluded. This is by way of describing it as subject to rescission, for rescission is only deserved by that which is concluded, having a conceptual existence in the sight of the Law. However, the entitlement to rescission does not imply that rescission is obligatory in all circumstances; rather, this expression accommodates cases where the rescission of the invalid contract is legally prevented by an impediment in some cases, as we will see in the discussion on the result of invalidity. Thus, from the definition of invalidity, the definition of the invalid contract is understood.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of fasad (invalidity/corruption) in Hanafi contract law, distinguishing it from butlan (nullity). The author begins by applying the theory to lawsuits, then moves to the technical definition. He critiques the classical Hanafi definition ('valid in essence, not in description') as unclear and proposes a refined definition: fasad is a defect in a contract that violates the Shari'ah system in a subsidiary, complementary aspect, rendering the contract subject to rescission (faskh). He highlights three features of this definition: (1) it reveals the true nature of fasad as a conceptual defect, (2) it clarifies the effect (subject to rescission), and (3) it confirms that a fasid contract is nevertheless concluded (mun'aqid), since only a concluded contract can be rescinded. Importantly, entitlement to rescission does not mean rescission is always obligatory; sometimes an impediment may prevent it. This definition sets the stage for further discussion on the consequences of fasad.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A defect in a contract that violates the Shari'ah system in a subsidiary, complementary aspect, making it subject to rescission.
العقد الفاسد (al-‘aqd al-fāsid): A contract that is legitimate in its essence but defective in a subsidiary aspect, thus valid in origin but subject to rescission.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Nullity; a contract that lacks a fundamental condition, making it void ab initio.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or annulment; the legal remedy for a fasid contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fasad: a contract valid in origin but defective in a subsidiary aspect, rendering it subject to rescission but not necessarily void.

General Causes of Contract Invalidity: Jahalah, Gharar, and Ikrah
Classification of invalidating contract defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three general causes of fasad in contracts and apply the Hanafi distinction between general and specific causes.

Core Idea
Three causes—jahalah, gharar, ikrah—bring most cases of contract invalidity under a single framework.

Contracts can be valid in their core yet defective in subsidiary aspects. This lesson explores the systematic classification of such defects into general causes that affect most contracts and specific causes tied to a single contract type. Understanding this framework is essential for a mufti to correctly diagnose the invalidity of a contract and determine the appropriate legal consequence.

Guided Path
1
Define fasad and its effect
Recall that fasad is invalidity in a subsidiary condition. The contract is initially valid (mun'aqid) but defective. The right to rescission exists but may be prevented by an impediment.
Fasad differs from butlan (void ab initio).
2
Distinguish general from specific causes
Not every defect applies to all contracts. The passage distinguishes causes that are 'general' (affecting a wide range of contracts) from 'specific' (varying per contract). Identify the examples given: shuyu' for mortgage, shart mufsid for commutative contracts.
This classification helps the mufti know where to look for the ruling.
3
Enumerate the three general causes
The author inductively lists three: jahalah (uncertainty about a material term), gharar (excessive risk/deception), and ikrah (duress). Understand the essence of each.
Jahalah and gharar often overlap; ikrah is separately debated.
4
Analyze the ikrah dispute
The passage notes disagreement on whether duress causes fasad or tawaqquf (suspension). Review the Hanafi position (fasad) and the opposing view, focusing on the proof and tarjih.
The mufta bihi is that ikrah causes fasad.
5
Apply the framework to a new case
Given a scenario (e.g., a sale where the price is ambiguous), identify which general cause is triggered and state the ruling (fasid). Then consider if any impediment prevents rescission.
Practice supports fatwa formulation.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Invalidators in Fiqh... by entitlement to rescission, for rescission is only entitled for what is contracted having an existence considered in the view of the Law. And this, even though entitlement to rescission does not imply the meaning of compelling rescission in all cases, but this expression is compatible with cases where rescission of a defective contract is legally prevented by an impediment in some cases, as we will see in the discussion of the result of invalidity. And this, and from the definition of fasad the definition of a defective contract is understood. Chapter Fifty-Five: General Causes of Invalidity. 1/50 - Indeed, cases of invalidity, as understood from its definition, refer to a general cause: the contract's violation of its legal system in a subsidiary, supplementary aspect. And this general cause appears in contracts in many forms that jurists grasp and specify in each contract, so they are considered the direct cause of its invalidity. And these different forms of violation that necessitate invalidity include those that have a general invalidating effect on contracts that suffer from invalidity, and those that are specific to some of these contracts and not others. Hence, the causes of invalidity are divided into two types: general and specific. As for the specific causes, knowledge of them is referred to the conditions of validity in each contract individually from books of fiqh, and there is no room to present them here in this general research. For example, common ownership (shuyu') invalidates the mortgage, but does not invalidate the sale (ref: F 1/25 - 8/25). And the prohibited condition called the invalidating condition (shart mufsid) invalidates commutative contracts but does not invalidate the gift (ref: F 14/43), and so on... And those general causes of invalidity, as appears to us by induction, are no more than three: uncertainty (jahalah), risk (gharar), and duress (ikrah), according to the view that considers duress as necessitating invalidity of the contract rather than its suspension (ref: F/185 and 194). 741
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents the theory of legal invalidators, focusing on the general causes of fasad in contracts. The author begins by clarifying the concept of fasad: a contract is considered fasad when it violates its legal system in a subsidiary, supplementary aspect. Such a contract is initially valid (mun'aqid) in that it has a legal existence but is defective. The right to rescission exists, but it may be prevented by certain impediments. The passage then divides causes of fasad into two types: general and specific. General causes affect all contracts (or a wide category), while specific causes vary per contract type. As examples, common ownership (shuyuʿ) invalidates a mortgage but not a sale, and a prohibited condition (shart mufsid) invalidates commutative contracts but not a gift. The author inductively identifies three general causes: jahalah (uncertainty/ignorance), gharar (risk/deception), and ikrah (duress). The inclusion of ikrah is noted as subject to disagreement: some scholars hold that duress does not cause fasad but rather suspends the contract (tawaqquf), while others consider it a direct cause of fasad. The Hanafi position generally views duress as rendering a contract fasid due to lack of free consent. This passage provides a foundational framework for understanding invalidity in Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): Invalidity of a contract due to a defect in a subsidiary aspect; the contract has a legal existence but is defective and subject to rescission.
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Uncertainty or ignorance about a material element of the contract that leads to invalidity.
الغرر (al-gharar): Risk or deception in a contract due to unknown outcomes or hidden defects.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Duress that compels a person to enter a contract against their free will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the general causes of fasad in contracts?
A) Jahalah (uncertainty)
B) Gharar (risk)
C) Riba (interest)
D) Ikrah (duress)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school reduces general contract invalidity to three causes: uncertainty, excessive risk, and duress; all others are specific to individual contracts.

General Causes of Invalidity in Contracts
Classifying causes of contract invalidity into general and specific

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three general causes of contract invalidity (fasad) in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish them from specific causes.

Core Idea
All contract invalidity stems from violation in a subsidiary element; the three general causes are jahala, gharar, and ikrah.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa classifies the causes of contract invalidity into general and specific. He states that all invalidity ultimately arises from a deviation in a complementary aspect of the contract. Then he extracts three universally applicable causes by induction: ignorance (jahala), uncertainty (gharar), and coercion (ikrah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching cause of fasad
The passage states that all invalidity traces back to one general cause: the contract's violation of its legal system in a subsidiary, complementary aspect. Understand that fasad is a defect in a secondary element, not a fundamental pillar.
Fasad differs from butlan; fasad can sometimes be rectified.
2
Distinguish general vs specific causes
Causes of fasad are either general (common to many contracts) or specific (unique to certain contracts). Specific causes are found in the conditions of validity per contract type.
Examples given: co-ownership invalidates a pledge but not a sale; a prohibited condition invalidates commutative contracts but not gifts.
3
List the three general causes
The author identifies three general causes by induction: jahala (ignorance), gharar (uncertainty), and ikrah (coercion). Memorize these as the core triggers of general invalidity.
These are not exhaustive for all contracts; some contracts have additional specific causes.
4
Note the nuance regarding coercion
The passage mentions that some scholars view coercion as causing suspension rather than invalidity. The Hanafi position is that it causes fasad, but this is a point of ikhtilaf.
The practical effect: under suspension, the contract can be ratified; with fasad, it is flawed ab initio.
5
Apply the classification
When analyzing a contract for invalidity, first check if any general cause (jahala, gharar, ikrah) is present. If not, examine specific conditions relevant to that contract type.
A mufti uses this framework to systematically identify defects.
