Chapter Fifty-Five: General Causes of Invalidity. 1/50 - Indeed, cases of invalidity, as understood from its definition, trace back to a general cause: the contract's violation of its legal system in a subsidiary, complementary aspect. This general cause manifests in contracts in numerous forms that jurists grasp and specify in each contract, considered the direct cause of its invalidity. These various forms of violation necessitating invalidity include those that have a generally corrupting effect on contracts that are affected by invalidity, and those that are specific to some contracts but not others. Consequently, the causes of invalidity are divided into two types: general and specific. As for the specific causes, knowledge of them is to be found in the conditions of validity for each contract individually from the books of fiqh, and there is no room to present them here in this general study. For example, co-ownership invalidates a pledge but does not invalidate a sale (see: F 1/25 - 8/25). And a prohibited condition, called a corrupting condition, invalidates commutative contracts but does not invalidate a gift (see: F 14/43), and so on. And those general causes of invalidity, as appears to us through induction, are no more than three: ignorance (jahala), uncertainty (gharar), and coercion (ikrah), according to the view that coercion necessitates the invalidity of the contract rather than its suspension (see: F 185 h and 194 h).
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the causes of contract invalidity (fasad) in Hanafi fiqh. The author states that all invalidity stems from one general cause: the contract's violation of its legal system in a subsidiary aspect. This general cause manifests in various specific forms. He then divides causes into two types: general (common to many contracts) and specific (unique to certain contracts). He gives examples: co-ownership invalidates a pledge but not a sale; a prohibited condition invalidates commutative contracts (like sale) but not a gift. The author then lists the three general causes as: ignorance (jahala), uncertainty (gharar), and coercion (ikrah), noting that some scholars consider coercion to cause invalidity rather than mere suspension of the contract. This classification sets the framework for the subsequent detailed discussion of each general cause.

Key Terms
فساد (fasad): Invalidity of a contract due to a defect in a subsidiary element; the contract is legally flawed.
جهالة (jahala): Ignorance or lack of knowledge about a material aspect of the contract.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or ambiguity in the essential terms of a contract (e.g., subject matter, price).
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion or duress that compels a party to enter a contract against their will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which of the following is NOT one of the three general causes of invalidity in contracts?
A) Jahala
B) Gharar
C) Ikrah
D) Ta'asis (frustration of purpose)

Key Takeaway
The three general causes of contract invalidity in Hanafi fiqh are ignorance, uncertainty, and coercion; these are distinguished from specific causes unique to certain contracts.

Result of Invalidity (Fasād) and Its Effects
Explains the dual legal result of fasād: rescission right and delayed ruling attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fāsid and bāṭil contract and state the dual effect of fasād: entitlement to rescission and delayed attachment of original rulings.

Core Idea
A fāsid contract is concluded but not binding; rulings attach only upon execution.

In Hanafi law, invalid contracts (fāsid) occupy an intermediate position between valid and void. Unlike void contracts, they are considered concluded and can produce some legal effects, but they lack binding force. This lesson unpacks the precise legal outcome: the contract is subject to rescission, and its original rulings are delayed until execution.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dichotomy
The passage states that a fāsid contract is concluded (mun‘aqid) but subject to rescission. Understand that fasād is an intermediate category between validity and voidness.
Contrast with bāṭil: void contracts produce no effects at all.
2
Identify the immediate effect: rescission
The contract loses binding force; any party can rescind, and the judge may rescind ex officio to protect public order.
This right exists from the moment of conclusion.
3
Identify the delayed effect: original rulings attach upon execution
Unlike a valid contract, original rulings (e.g., ownership transfer) do not happen at formation but only upon execution (e.g., taking possession).
Example: in an invalid sale, buyer does not own the item upon offer alone.
4
Apply to an example: invalid sale
In a fāsid sale, the buyer does not own the sold item at contract formation; ownership transfers upon execution (taking possession). This illustrates the delayed attachment of rulings.
See al-Bada'i' 5/300 for further detail.

واستناداً إلى هذا النظر تقرر في الاجتهاد الحنفي أن فساد العقد ـ بالمعنى الاصطلاحي وهو أنه مرتبة متوسطة بين الصحة والبطلان - نتيجته هي : أن يصبح العقد مستحقاً للفسخ بإرادة كل من الطرفين وبإرادة القاضي أي أنّ العقد الفاسد يفقد قوته الإلزامية. فإذا طولب أحد الطرفين بتنفيذه دفع عن نفسه بالفسخ . فإذا لم يطلب أحد الطرفين الفسخ فإنَّ القاضي يفسخه من تلقاء نفسه دون طلب، كما في حالة البطلان حماية لنظام التعاقد الذي هو من النظام العام، وهذا لا ينافي ترتب الأحكام الأصلية على العقد الفاسد بمقتضى انعقاده فترتب الحكم نتيجة للانعقاد، واستحقاق الفسخ نتيجة للفساد، بحسب التفصيل التالي في هاتين النتيجتين : :أولاً: ترتيب الحكم على العقد الفاسد:

Chapter Fifty-Six: The Result of Invalidity and What Pertains to It. 1/56 - We have seen previously that the invalid contract is considered to be concluded (F 7/54). And we have seen in the definition of invalidity that it makes the contract subject to rescission (F 9/54). It is obvious that the conclusion of a contract necessitates an effect for it, because the contract that does not produce its effect at all is the void (bāṭil) one. Based on this view, it has been established in Hanafi ijtihad that the invalidity of a contract – in its technical sense, which is an intermediate level between validity and voidness – its result is: that the contract becomes subject to rescission by the will of either party and by the judge's will, meaning the invalid contract loses its binding force. If one party demands its execution, the other can defend themselves by rescission. If neither party seeks rescission, the judge rescinds it on his own initiative without a request, as in the case of voidness, to protect the contractual system which is part of public order. This does not conflict with the original rulings being attached to the invalid contract by virtue of its conclusion. So the ruling is attached as a result of conclusion, and entitlement to rescission as a result of invalidity, according to the following detail of these two results: First: Attachment of the ruling on the invalid contract: 2/56 - As for the attachment of the ruling on the invalid contract by virtue of its conclusion, it is not established by the mere contract as in a valid contract; rather it is delayed until the execution of the invalid contract. So if it is, for example, an invalid sale, the buyer does not own the sold item upon the completion of the offer (1) (Refer to al-Bada'i' 5/300).
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage explains the legal outcome (natījah) of a fāsid contract in Hanafi law. The author first establishes two preliminary points: a fāsid contract is considered concluded (mun‘aqid), and it is subject to rescission (faskh). The key distinction: a void (bāṭil) contract produces no effect at all, while a fāsid contract is an intermediate category. The result is that the contract loses its binding force—either party can rescind, and if they do not, the judge may rescind it ex officio to protect public order. However, the contract does produce some original rulings (e.g., ownership, liability) by virtue of its conclusion. These rulings are not immediate like a valid contract; they attach only upon execution of the contract. For example, in an invalid sale, the buyer does not own the item simply upon offer and acceptance; ownership transfers only when the sale is executed (i.e., when possession is taken). The text then divides the discussion into two parts: (1) attachment of original rulings (delayed until execution), and (2) entitlement to rescission (immediate due to defect). The passage ends with the beginning of the first part, stating that the ruling does not attach upon mere contract but upon execution.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A technical term in Hanafi law meaning an intermediate level between validity and voidness; the contract is concluded but defective, making it subject to rescission.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Voidness; a contract that produces no legal effects at all.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract by party action or judicial order due to a defect.
الانعقاد (al-in‘iqād): Conclusion or formation of a contract through offer and acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does a fāsid contract produce its original rulings (e.g., ownership transfer)?
A) Immediately upon the completion of offer and acceptance.
B) Only upon execution (e.g., taking possession).
C) It never produces any original rulings.
D) Only after judicial validation.

Key Takeaway
A fāsid contract is concluded and can produce rulings upon execution, but it remains subject to rescission due to its defect.

Fasid Contracts in Hanafi Fiqh: Rules and Critique of Usufruct Valuation
Author presents Hanafi rulings on fasid partnerships, sale, ijarah, and criticizes the theory of usufruct non-valuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for fasid contracts (partnership, sale, ijarah) and evaluate the criticism of the Hanafi theory of usufruct non-valuation.

Core Idea
In fasid contracts, Hanafi law distinguishes between assets with intrinsic value and usufructs valued only by agreement—a theory the author calls 'weak'.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rulings on fasid (invalid) contracts in partnership, sale, and ijarah. You will see how the school distinguishes between assets with intrinsic value (sold goods) and usufructs (services/benefits) that derive value only from the contract. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents the rules and then delivers a sharp critique of the Hanafi theory of usufruct valuation, opening the door for fatwa modification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three contract types and their fasid consequences
List the rules for fasid capital partnership (profit by capital shares), fasid mudaraba (profit to capital owner, worker gets ujrat al-mithl capped at agreed), fasid sale (market value due), and fasid ijarah (wage limited to agreed amount).
Also note the exception for pledge (rahn) where fasid and sahih are treated alike.
2
Understand the principle behind the distinction
Sales involve physical assets (ayn) with intrinsic value (taqawwum dhati), so the market value is obligatory. Ijarah involves usufructs (manafi‘) which in Hanafi theory have no intrinsic value; their value is conferred by the contract (taqawwum bi al-‘aqd), so the agreed wage binds.
This principle is the key to the whole lesson.
3
Examine the exception: pledge (rahn)
In rahn, the validity or invalidity of the contract does not affect the rights of retention and priority—both are established in either case.
4
Critique the Hanafi theory of usufruct valuation
The author explicitly states that the Hanafi theory is 'weak' (da‘if) and subject to 'severe criticism' from other schools. Those schools treat usufructs like physical assets, requiring the market wage (ujrat al-mithl) in fasid ijarah.
This critique has direct implications for fatwa practice.
5
Apply the mufti's perspective
A mufti today, aware of this critique, must decide whether to follow the classical Hanafi ruling (capped wage) or adopt the stronger view from other schools (market wage). The author suggests the Hanafi theory is flexible and can be modified by ijtihad or the ruler.
Consider also the exceptions for ignorance or coercion.

الفصل (٥٦) نتيجة الفساد وما إليها
وفي الشركة الفاسدة إذا كانت شركة أموال، فالربح الحاصل فيها يستحقه الشركاء بنسبة حصصهم في رأس المال لا بحسب اتفاقهم في عقد الشركة (ر: المجلة / ١٣٦٨).
وإذا كان رأس المال من أحد الطرفين كما في شركة المضاربة إذا فسدت فالربح لرب المال وللمضارب أجر مثل عمله، على أن لا يتجاوز المقدار المشروط له في العقد (ر : المجلة / ١٤٢٦).
٤/٥٦ - ملاحظة : في عقد الرهن لا فرق بين صحيح وفاسد.
تستوي في نظر فقهائنا نتيجة الفساد والصحة في عقد الرهن.
فقد صرَّحوا بأنَّ الرهن الفاسد تثبت فيه - بالنسبة إلى الاستدانة التي تمت بسببه - أحكام الرهن الصحيح من حق احتباس المرهون، والامتياز
=
وهذا بخلاف قيمة المبيع في البيع الفاسد، فإنها تثبت ولو تجاوزت الثمن المسمى فيه . ويعلل فقهاء الحنفية هذا التفريق منهم بين البيع والإجارة الفاسدين بأن الشيء المبيع ذو قيمة بذاته. فتجب في حالة الفساد قيمته المعادلة مهما بلغت.
غير أن المنافع فهي في الاجتهاد الحنفي غير متقومة في ذاتها، وإنما يقومها العقد. فإذا حدد الطرفان الأجر باتفاقهما لا يجوز تجاوزه (ر) البدائع آخر فصل حكم الإجارة ٢١٨/٤). النظرية الحنفية في عدم تقوم المنافع بذاتها هي نظرية ضعيفة. وهي عرضة للنقد المر في المذاهب الاجتهادية الأخرى التي تعتبر المنافع كالأعيان في التقوم الذاتي، كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية (المدخل إلى نظرية الالتزام العامة) في بحث العين والمنفعة من فصل الأموال.
وقد كان ينبغي في فساد العقود أن جميع يعتبر في الحقوق الثابتة فيها ما حدده العاقدان نظير ما قرروا في فساد الإجارة، لأن فساد العقد لا يعدم الرضا بالحقوق المتقابلة المحددة فيه، اللهم إلا إذا كان فساد العقد ناشئاً عن جهالة البدل، أو عن الإكراه عند من يرى الإكراه مفسداً للعقد لا موقفاً لحكمه، فعندئذ يجب أن يثبت العوض المعادل في العقد الفاسد بالغاً ما بلغ. وقد علمت أن نظرية الفساد بكل ما فيها من قواعد وأحكام هي اجتهادية حنفية المنشأ فهي أكثر من غيرها قابلية للتعديل بحسب المصلحة أو اختلاف الأنظار التي يعود تقديرها للاجتهاد ولولي الأمر استناداً إلى رأي الفقهاء الثقات.
Chapter (56): The Result of Invalidity and Its Implications. In an invalid partnership if it is a partnership of capital, the profit earned is owned by the partners in proportion to their shares in the capital, not according to their agreement in the partnership contract (cf. al-Majalla / 1368). If the capital is from one party as in a mudaraba partnership when it becomes invalid, the profit belongs to the owner of capital, and the mudarib gets the equivalent wage of his work, provided it does not exceed the amount stipulated for him in the contract (cf. al-Majalla / 1426). 4/56 - Note: In the contract of pledge there is no difference between valid and invalid. In the view of our jurists, the result of invalidity and validity are equal in the contract of pledge. They have stated that in an invalid pledge, the rulings of a valid pledge are established — with respect to the debt incurred due to it — including the right to retain the pledged property and the privilege. And this is contrary to the value of the sold item in an invalid sale, for it is established even if it exceeds the named price. The Hanafi jurists explain this distinction between invalid sale and invalid ijarah by saying that the sold item has intrinsic value. Thus in case of invalidity, its equivalent value is obligatory however much it may be. However, usufructs in Hanafi ijtihad are not intrinsically valuable; rather the contract gives them value. So if the two parties determine the wage by agreement, it is not permissible to exceed it (cf. al-Bada'i', last chapter on ijarah rulings, 4/218). The Hanafi theory of non-valuation of usufructs in themselves is a weak theory. It is subject to severe criticism in other schools of ijtihad which consider usufructs like physical assets in intrinsic valuation, as we will see in the third part of this jurisprudential series (Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations) in the discussion of assets and usufructs from the chapter on wealth. One should have considered in invalid contracts that all rights established therein should be what the contracting parties specified, similar to what they ruled in invalid ijarah, because invalidity of contract does not nullify consent to the reciprocal rights specified therein, unless the invalidity of the contract arises from ignorance of the consideration, or from coercion according to those who see coercion as invalidating the contract not suspending its ruling. In that case, the equivalent consideration must be established in the invalid contract whatever it may be. And you have learned that the theory of invalidity with all its rules and rulings is Hanafi in origin, so it is more capable of modification according to interest or difference of opinions, the estimation of which is left to ijtihad and the ruler based on the opinion of reliable jurists.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi discusses the consequences of fasid (invalid) contracts. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first presents the Hanafi ruling for invalid partnerships: in a fasid capital partnership, profit is distributed according to capital shares, not the agreed ratio. In fasid mudaraba, profit goes to the capital owner, and the manager gets the equivalent wage (ujrat al-mithl) but capped at the agreed amount. A brief note mentions that in pledge (rahn), fasid and sahih are treated alike. The author then contrasts this with sale: in fasid sale, the buyer owes the market value (qimah), even if it exceeds the agreed price, because the sold item has intrinsic value. In fasid ijarah, however, the wage is limited to the agreed amount because usufructs (manafi') are not intrinsically valuable in Hanafi thought. The author criticizes this Hanafi theory as weak, noting that other schools consider usufructs valuable in themselves. He suggests that the general principle should be to respect the parties' agreement, except in cases of ignorance or coercion. Finally, he notes that the Hanafi theory of fasid is more flexible and subject to modification.

Key Terms
المنافع (al-manāfi‘): Usufructs; benefits or services derived from an asset; in Hanafi law, they are considered non-valuable in themselves.
التقوم (al-taqawwum): The quality of being valuable or having a market value; in Hanafi theory, physical assets have taqawwum dhati (intrinsic value), while usufructs do not.
شركة فاسدة (sharika fāsida): An invalid partnership that does not meet all essential conditions but is not void; it still produces some legal effects, such as distribution of profit by capital shares.
العدل البدل (al-‘iwaḍ al-mu‘ādil): The equivalent consideration; in fasid contracts, the market value or fair compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the main difference between a fasid sale and a fasid ijarah regarding the value owed?
A) In fasid sale, the buyer owes the market value; in fasid ijarah, the wage is limited to the agreed amount.
B) In both, the agreed price/wage is binding.
C) In fasid sale, the agreed price is binding; in fasid ijarah, the market wage is due.
D) In both, the market value/wage is due.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between assets with intrinsic value and usufructs valued by contract leads to different consequences in fasid sale and ijarah, but this theory is weak and open to modification.

Distinction Between Fasid and Batil Pledge (Rahn)
Author contrasts the legal effects of fasid and batil pledges in Hanafi fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given pledge scenario as fasid or batil according to Hanafi fiqh and state the legal consequences for each.

Core Idea
A fasid pledge is treated like a valid one for the debt it secures; a batil pledge has no legal effect at all.

In Hanafi contract theory, not all invalid contracts are equal. The distinction between fasid (invalid) and batil (void) is crucial, especially in pledges (rahn). This passage explains that a fasid pledge still confers the right to retain the pledged property for the debt that caused it, while a batil pledge produces no rights. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of rahn in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of defective pledges
The passage distinguishes between al-rahn al-fāsid (invalid pledge) and al-rahn al-bāṭil (void pledge). Read the definitions and note the key differences in their legal effects.
Fasid violates supplementary conditions; batil violates essential conditions.
2
Understand the legal effect of a fasid pledge
A fasid pledge is treated like a valid pledge regarding the debt that caused it. The creditor retains the right to hold the pledged property until the debt is paid, even if the pledgor rescinds.
This is based on the principle that the debtor obtained the debt only because of the pledge.
3
Recognize the exception for pre-existing debts
If a fasid pledge secures a debt that existed before the pledge, it does not have the same effect. The retention right only applies to debts incurred specifically because of the fasid pledge.
The passage contrasts 'debt that occurred due to this fasid pledge' vs. a prior debt.
4
Understand the legal effect of a batil pledge
A batil pledge does not contract at all and produces no legal effects. No rights of retention arise from it.
Examples include pledgor incompetence, non-pledgeable property, or securing a non-guaranteed right.
5
Apply the distinction to examples
The passage gives examples: pledging an undivided share (shayi‘) is fasid (because delivery is defective); pledging by a madman or for a deposit (wadi‘ah) is batil. Practice classifying similar scenarios.
The key is to identify which condition (essential vs. supplementary) is violated.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
الآراء على في استيفاء الدين منه ولو أنَّ الراهن المستدين فسخ الرهن بمقتضى الفساد. ويكون المرهون في حالة الفساد مضموناً على المرتهن الدائن بما يعادل الدين كما في الرهن الصحيح تماماً، وذلك على الراجح من المذهبية. وذلك لأن الراهن المدين لم يتوصل إلى الاستدانة إلا أساس التوثيق بالرهن فإذا فسخ الراهن الرهن فعليه إعادة الذين قبل استعادة المرهون وإن حق احتباس مال الغير لاستيفاء الحق منه قد يثبت شرعاً في حالات كثيرة، دون رهن عقدي كالوكيل بالشراء إذا اشترى للموكل ما وكله بشرائه، فإنَّ له احتباسه عن الموكل حتى يؤديه الثمن، ولو لم يكن الوكيل قد دفع الثمن من ماله لأنه عرضة للمطالبة (ر: المجلة / ١٩٤٠ - ١٩٤١). فلم يبق فرق بين صحيح الرهن وفاسده من حيث النتيجة، في حق الاستدانة التي وقعت بسبب هذا الرهن الفاسد بخلاف الاستدانة السابقة التي تَمَّت خالية عن الرهن، ثم رهن شيء في مقابلها رهناً فاسداً، فإنه لا تثبت له أحكام الرهن الصحيح. أما الرهن الباطل فإنه لا ينعقد أصلاً، ولا يثبت فيه شيء من أحكام الرهن الصحيح على كل حال (۱). (۱) مثال الرهن الفاسد المخالف لبعض شرائطه الفرعية المتممة رهن الحصة الشائعة في مقابل دين فإن المرهون فيه هو مال محترم قد رهن في مقابل حق مضمون، واستوفي شرائطه الأساسية فينعقد لكن الشيوع يخل بالقبض اللازم فيكون فاسداً (ر؛ ف ٨/٢٥). ومثال الرهن الباطل المخالف لبعض شرائطه الأساسية مالو كان الراهن غير ذي أهلية كالمجنون، أو كان المرهون غير قابل للارتهان كالمال الموقوف. وكذا لو كان الحق المرهون في مقابله غير مضمون كالرهن في مقابل الوديعة فإنّ الوديعة أمانة لا تضمن بالهلاك عند الوديع دون تعد أو تقصير منه، فيكون عقد الرهن عبثاً غير مفيد، إذ لو هلكت الأمانة لا يستوفي شيء من الرهن عنها (ر : ف ٦/٣١ والدر المختار ورد المحتار، آخر كتاب الرهن) ٣٣٨/٥ - ٣٣٩.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Supports in Jurisprudence. Opinions on the recovery of the debt from it, even if the debtor pledgor rescinds the pledge due to invalidity. And in the case of invalidity, the pledged property is held as security by the creditor mortgagee for an amount equivalent to the debt, exactly as in a valid pledge, according to the preponderant view of the school. This is because the debtor pledgor only obtained the debt on the basis of the security of the pledge. So if the pledgor rescinds the pledge, he must repay the debt before taking back the pledged property. And indeed the right to retain another's property to recover a right from it is legally established in many situations without a contractual pledge, such as the agent for purchase if he buys for the principal what he authorized him to buy, he has the right to retain it from the principal until he pays him the price, even if the agent has not paid the price from his own wealth because he is subject to claim (cf. al-Majalla / 1940-1941). So there remains no difference between a valid and an invalid pledge in terms of result, regarding the debt that occurred due to this invalid pledge, unlike a prior debt that was incurred without a pledge, and then something is pledged in return for it with an invalid pledge, for the rulings of a valid pledge do not apply to it. As for the void pledge, it does not contract at all, and nothing of the rulings of a valid pledge applies to it in any case. (1) Example of an invalid pledge that violates some of its supplementary conditions: the pledge of an undivided share (shayi') in return for a debt. The pledged item is respected property pledged in return for a guaranteed right, and it fulfills its essential conditions, so it is contracted, but the co-ownership hinders the required delivery, so it is invalid (cf. Fath al-Qadir 8/25). And an example of a void pledge that violates some of its essential conditions: if the pledgor is incompetent, like a madman, or if the pledged property is not eligible for pledge, such as endowed property. Also, if the right for which it is pledged is not guaranteed, like a pledge in return for a deposit (wadi'ah), for the deposit is a trust that is not guaranteed for the depositee without transgression or negligence, so the pledge contract is futile and useless, for if the trust perishes, nothing is recovered from the pledge for it (cf. Fath al-Qadir 6/31 and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, end of the book of pledge, 5/338-339).
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on 'Theory of Legal Supports' addresses the distinction between fasid (invalid) and batil (void) pledges (rahn). The author first states the ruling: a fasid pledge is treated like a valid pledge in most respects. The creditor may retain the pledged property to recover the debt, even if the pledgor rescinds. This is because the debt was obtained on the strength of the pledge. The author analogizes this to the right of an agent to retain purchased goods until payment. However, this only applies to debts incurred directly due to the fasid pledge; a pre-existing debt that is later secured by a fasid pledge does not enjoy these rights. In contrast, a batil pledge does not contract at all and produces no legal effects. Examples are provided: a fasid pledge occurs when the conditions for validity are essentially met, but a supplementary condition like delivery is defective, e.g., pledging a jointly owned share (shayi‘) where delivery is impossible. A batil pledge occurs when essential conditions are missing, e.g., the pledgor is incompetent, the property is not eligible (like waqf), or the secured right is not a guaranteed debt (like a deposit). In the latter case, the pledge is useless because there is no debt to recover.

Key Terms
الرهن الفاسد (al-rahn al-fāsid): An invalid pledge that meets essential conditions but violates a supplementary condition; it still confers rights similar to a valid pledge for the debt that caused it.
الرهن الباطل (al-rahn al-bāṭil): A void pledge that lacks essential conditions; it does not contract and produces no legal effects.
الاحتباس (al-iḥtibās): The right to retain possession of an asset to secure a debt or claim; in pledge, it is the creditor's right to keep the pledged property until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a batil (void) pledge according to the Hanafi school?
A) Pledging an undivided share (shayi') of a property to secure a debt.
B) Pledging a property by a competent adult to secure a debt.
C) Pledging a property in return for a deposit (wadi'ah).
D) Pledging a property that is delivered to the creditor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid pledge still has legal effect for the debt that caused it, while a batil pledge is entirely void.

The Legal Effect of Defective (Fasid) vs Void (Batil) Contracts: The Case of Rahn
Explaining the legal effects of fasid and batil contracts using rahn as an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a fasid contract produces legal effects regarding the underlying obligation, while a batil contract does not, using the example of rahn.

Core Idea
A fasid pledge secures the debt like a valid one; a batil pledge is a legal nullity.

This passage refines our understanding of contract validity in Hanafi fiqh by distinguishing between two types of invalidity: fasid (defective) and batil (void). Focusing on the contract of pledge (rahn), the preponderant Hanafi view holds that a fasid rahn, though flawed in subsidiary conditions, still makes the pledged property security for the debt. In contrast, a batil rahn, violating essential conditions, has no legal effect. The lesson will break down this rule, examine the reasoning (ta'lil), and apply it to practical scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of invalid pledges
From the passage, note that fasid rahn violates subsidiary conditions (e.g., common ownership affecting possession), while batil rahn violates essential conditions (e.g., lack of capacity, non-pledgeable property).
Essential conditions include legal capacity, pledgeable property, and a guaranteed right.
2
State the Hanafi rule for fasid rahn
According to the preponderant view, a fasid rahn produces the same legal effect as a valid rahn with respect to the debt for which it was given. The pledged property becomes security, and the creditor has the right of retention.
This equivalence applies only when the defective pledge is directly connected to the debt (contemporaneous loan).
3
Analyze the ta'lil (reasoning)
The debtor obtained the loan only because of the pledge; thus, the right to retain the property is established analogously to other cases like an agent retaining goods until paid (al-Majalla 1940-1941).
The reasoning (ta'lil) is based on the purpose of the pledge: securing the debt.
4
Distinguish the exception
If a fasid pledge is given for a pre-existing debt (i.e., the debt existed before the pledge), the rule does not apply, and the fasid pledge has no effect.
This exception ensures the pledge is not used to secure a debt that was already incurred without security.
5
Conclude for batil rahn
A batil rahn does not contract at all and has no legal effects whatsoever. Examples: pledgor is incompetent, property is non-pledgeable, or the right is not guaranteed (e.g., against a deposit).
The distinction between fasid and batil is crucial for fatwa.

The Seventh Chapter: Theory of Legal Supports in Fiqh. Opinions on the recovery of debt from [the pledged property]. Even if the debtor-pledgor rescinds the pledge due to its defectiveness, the pledged property in the case of defectiveness is considered as a security on the creditor-pledgee equivalent to the debt, exactly as in a valid pledge, according to the preponderant opinion of the school. This is because the debtor-pledgor does not obtain the loan except on the basis of securing it with the pledge; so if the pledgor rescinds the pledge, he must return the debt before recovering the pledged property. Indeed, the right to retain the property of another to recover a right from it may be established legally in many cases, without a contractual pledge, such as the agent for purchase if he buys for the principal that which he was authorized to buy, then he has the right to retain it from the principal until he pays the price, even if the agent has not paid the price from his own money because he is subject to demand (see: al-Majalla, articles 1940-1941). So there remains no difference between a valid pledge and a defective one in terms of the result, with respect to the debt that occurred because of this defective pledge, unlike the previous debt that took place free of a pledge, and then something was pledged against it as a defective pledge; then its rulings do not apply. As for a void pledge, it does not contract at all, and none of the rulings of a valid pledge apply to it in any case (1). (1) An example of a defective pledge (fasid) that violates some of its subsidiary completing conditions: the pledge of a common share against a debt; for the pledged property is a respected property pledged against a guaranteed right, and its essential conditions are fulfilled, so it contracts, but the common ownership violates the necessary possession, so it is defective (see: Fath al-Qadir 8/25). And an example of a void pledge (batil) that violates some of its essential conditions: if the pledgor is not legally competent, such as a madman, or the pledged property is not susceptible to being pledged, such as an endowment property. Also, if the right against which the pledge is made is not guaranteed, like a pledge against a deposit; for the deposit is a trust that is not guaranteed upon destruction by the depositary without transgression or negligence, so the pledge contract becomes useless and of no benefit, because if the trust perishes, nothing is collected from the pledge for it (see: Fath al-Qadir 6/31 and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, end of the book of Rahn, 5/338-339).
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding the legal effect of defective (fasid) and void (batil) contracts, specifically using the contract of pledge (rahn) as a case study. The author first states that according to the preponderant opinion in the Hanafi school, a defective pledge (rahn fasid) produces the same legal effect as a valid pledge with respect to the debt for which it was given. This means the pledged property remains a security for the debt, and the creditor (pledgee) has the right to retain it. The justification is that the debtor obtained the loan solely on the strength of the pledge, so if he rescinds, he must first repay the debt. The author then broadens the rule by noting that the right to retain another's property to recover a right is established in many non-contractual situations as well, such as an agent retaining goods until paid. However, this equivalence only applies when the defective pledge is directly connected to the debt; if a prior debt existed without a pledge and then a defective pledge is given later, the ruling does not apply. In contrast, a void pledge (batil) has no legal effect whatsoever. The footnotes clarify the distinction: a defective pledge violates subsidiary conditions (e.g., common ownership affecting possession), while a void pledge violates essential conditions (e.g., lack of capacity, non-pledgeable property, or non-guaranteed right). Thus, the passage teaches that not all invalid contracts are treated alike; the level of defect determines the legal consequences.

Key Terms
الرهن الفاسد (ar-rahn al-fāsid): A pledge that meets essential conditions but violates subsidiary ones; it contracts but has a defect
الرهن الباطل (ar-rahn al-bāṭil): A pledge that violates essential conditions; it does not contract at all
الراجح من المذهبية (ar-rājiḥ min al-madhhabiyya): The preponderant opinion within the Hanafi school

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant Hanafi view, what is the legal effect of a fasid (defective) pledge (rahn) compared to a valid pledge?
A) It has no effect at all
B) It has the same effect as a valid pledge regarding the debt
C) It nullifies the debt automatically
D) It only applies if the pledge is in writing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a defective (fasid) contract is not void; it can still produce legal effects regarding the underlying obligation, unlike a void (batil) contract.

Conditions for Rescinding a Fasid Contract
Setting forth the two conditions for the right to rescind a fasid contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for the right to rescind a fasid contract and apply the rule to scenarios involving transformation of the object or third-party rights.

Core Idea
The right to rescind a fasid contract is lost if the object changes form or third-party rights have been acquired.

In Hanafi law, a fasid (defective) contract is initially rescindable, but this right is not absolute. This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work lays out two critical conditions that must be present for rescission to be permissible. If either condition is absent, the contract's effects, such as ownership, become stable and cannot be undone. Understanding these conditions is essential for a mufti who must advise on contract disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the first two paragraphs of the Arabic text. Condition 1: the object of the contract (al-ma‘qūd ‘alayh) must remain in its original state. Condition 2: rescission must not invalidate third-party rights.
These are shurut for the existence of the right to faskh.
2
Examine the examples for Condition 1
The text lists cases where the object is consumed, changed in form (e.g., wheat ground into flour, cloth dyed), or increased (e.g., building a house, planting trees). In each case, rescission is blocked.
Transformation includes both physical change and increase (ziyādah).
3
Examine the examples for Condition 2
If the buyer, after taking possession, transfers the object to a third party via a valid contract (sale, gift, pledge, endowment), the original seller cannot rescind. Third-party rights act as a legal impediment (māni‘).
This stabilizes the contract to protect acquired rights.
4
Understand the rationale
The passage explicitly states the purpose: to ensure stability of transactions (istiqrār al-ta‘āmul) and protect acquired rights. This is a manifestation of the maqāṣid of the Sharī‘a.
This reasoning (ta‘līl) is key to applying the rule to new cases.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Consider a case where a buyer receives a car in a fasid sale, then repaints it. Condition 1 may be contested: does repainting change the 'name' of the car? In Hanafi law, such a transformation likely blocks rescission. Similarly, if the buyer sells the car to a third party, Condition 2 blocks rescission.
Analogical reasoning (qiyās) extends the examples.

Source Text

Chapter (56): The Result of Defectiveness and Related Matters. Secondly: The Right to Rescind and Its Impediments: 56/5- As for the right to rescind due to defectiveness, it is restricted by two conditions: First: The object of the contract remains in its original state after the execution of the contract. So the rescission of a defective sale is prevented if the sold item perishes in the possession of the buyer, or he consumes it, or changes its form in a way that changes its name, such as if it was wheat and he ground it, or it was flour and he baked it, and also if he brings about an increase, such as if the sold item was a house and he built it, or land and he planted trees, or a cloth and he dyed it. The prevention of rescission in all these cases and their likes results in the stability of ownership or the ruling that was established by the execution of the defective contract. Second: That the rescission of the defective contract should not lead to the invalidation of rights that have been acquired by third parties in the object of the contract. Otherwise, the right that arises for a third party becomes a legal impediment to rescinding the prior contract due to defectiveness. Based on this, if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a defective sale until he becomes considered its owner by possession, then he sells it, gives it as a gift, pledges it in a valid contract, or endows it, then the rescission of the first defective sale is prevented and the ruling established by its execution is stabilized (see: al-Majalla, article 372) (1) and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar on the rulings of defective sale, 4/125-127 and 130-131, and al-Bada'i', 5/300-302. The purpose of the jurists in preventing rescission despite defectiveness when these two conditions are not present is simply to ensure stability of transactions and to protect acquired rights. 6/5- To whom does the authority to rescind in a defective contract belong? The authority to rescind is established for both parties to the contract, provided that it is done in the presence [of the other party?] (1) [Translator: "bimaḥḍar" likely means in the presence of the other party or in a session?] (1) That is, the transfer of ownership to the buyer and his liability for the value of the sold item.
Source pages p. 710

Commentary
This passage outlines the Hanafi rules governing when the right to rescind (faskh) a fasid (defective) contract is available and when it is lost. The author presents two conditions that must be satisfied for the right to rescind to exist. If either condition is absent, the rescission is blocked and the contract's effects, including ownership, become stable. The first condition is that the object of the contract must remain unchanged in the hands of the buyer. If the object perishes, is consumed, or is transformed (e.g., wheat ground into flour, or cloth dyed), the contract cannot be rescinded; the buyer's ownership becomes final. This is because the object no longer exists in its original form, making return impossible. The second condition is that rescinding the contract must not invalidate rights acquired by third parties. If the buyer, after taking possession under a defective sale, transfers the object to another person through a valid contract (e.g., sale, gift, pledge, or endowment), then the original defective sale becomes irrevocable. The third-party rights take precedence, and the original seller cannot rescind. The wisdom behind these rules, as stated, is to ensure stability in transactions and protect acquired rights—a key maqasid principle in Hanafi jurisprudence. The passage concludes by noting that the authority to rescind belongs to both parties, but with the requirement that rescission be done in the presence of the other (likely meaning in a session or before a judge).

Key Terms
الفسخ (al-faskh): The legal dissolution or rescission of a contract, restoring the parties to their pre-contract state
المعقود عليه (al-maʿqūd ʿalayh): The object of the contract, i.e., the subject matter (here, the sold item)
حقوق الغير (ḥuqūq al-ghayr): Rights of third parties that have been validly acquired in the object of the contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following scenarios would prevent rescission of a fasid sale?
A) The buyer has not yet taken possession of the sold item.
B) The buyer consumes the sold item.
C) The seller demands rescission immediately after the contract.
D) The object of sale is still in its original condition.

Key Takeaway
A fasid contract becomes irreversible if the object is transformed or if third-party rights have been acquired, ensuring transactional stability and protection of acquired rights.

The Hanafi Theory of Fasād: Protecting Third Parties through Possession
Analysis of fasād theory and possession rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi concept of fasād as a distinct legal category and apply the rule that possession (qabd) transfers ownership in a fasid sale to protect third-party rights.

Core Idea
Fasād is a third category between sahīh and bātil; possession transfers ownership to protect good-faith third parties.

In the Hanafi school, a contract that is 'fasid' (invalid) is not void (bātil) but defective, requiring a right of rescission. A key pragmatic rule is that possession of the subject matter in a fasid sale transfers ownership to the buyer, thereby protecting third parties who subsequently deal with the asset. This lesson unpacks the rationale and debates around this rule, essential for a mufti navigating complex contractual disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three contract categories
In Hanafi fiqh, contracts are sahīh, fāsid, or bātil. Fasād is a distinct intermediate category—the contract is concluded (mun‘aqid) but defective, giving a right of rescission.
Other schools like Shafi‘i collapse fasād into bātil.
2
Understand the right of rescission
Abu Hanifa gives the judge general authority to rescind any fasid contract. Imam Muhammad adds: if the defect is a condition benefiting one party, only that party can rescind, because they can drop the condition to validate the contract.
This nuance affects who can terminate the contract.
3
Analyze the possession rule
In a fasid sale, physical possession (qabd) transfers ownership to the buyer. This is a legal device to protect third parties who deal with the buyer in good faith, since there is no public registry for defects.
The rule stabilizes commercial transactions.
4
Evaluate the critique and response
Professor Shafiq Shahata argues that fasād is really bātil and the possession rule is a fiction. The author refutes by citing Hanafi texts that treat fasād as a third category and explaining apparent counterexamples (kafala, shuf'a).
The mufta bihi is the classical Hanafi view.

Source Text

لكن ذهب صاحبه الإمام محمد إلى أن الفساد إذا كان ناشئاً عن شرط مفسد مشروط لمصلحة أحد الطرفين على الآخر فحق الفسخ ينحصر بالطرف الذي كان الشرط المفسد مشروطاً لمصلحته، لأنه هو القادر على تصحيح العقد بحذف الشرط، فلو ساغ للآخر الفسخ لأبطل حقه في التصحيح وتثبيت العقد (ر: البدائع ٣٠٠/٥).

الرسالة المذكورة ف/ ١٦٩ - ١٧٠ / بتلخيص). أقول: يتضح بعد ما أسلفناه من مباحث الفساد المؤيدة بالنصوص أن هذا النظر من الأستاذ شفيق شحاتة غير مستقيم للأسباب التالية .

The other, whatever the cause of invalidity in the view of Abu Hanifa and likewise the judge has this authority (R. 1/56 and al-Bada'i 5/301). But his companion Imam Muhammad held that if the invalidity arises from a corrupt condition stipulated for the benefit of one of the two parties over the other, the right of rescission is confined to the party for whose benefit the corrupt condition was stipulated, because he is able to correct the contract by deleting the condition. If the other party were allowed to rescind, it would nullify his right to correct and affirm the contract (see: al-Bada'i 5/300). 7/56 - Professor Shafiq Shahata says in his research on invalidity, the summary of which: The statement that ownership transfers to the buyer by possession in an invalid sale is a purely Hanafi conceptualization, and it is the essence of the theory of invalidity, and its purpose in reality is to protect the rights of third parties. So it is not permissible to argue the invalidity of the contract against this third party, and the owner of the thing that left his ownership through an invalid contract cannot reclaim it if it has passed through hands, because if that were allowed for him, contractual relations would be destabilized. The causes of invalidity are many, and in Islamic fiqh there are no methods for publicizing transactions (1), so the third party remains ignorant of the defects in the ownership of the person from whom ownership was transferred to him. If he buys, his ownership is shaky. For this reason, the jurists created a greatly beneficial rule by which one can dispense with methods of publicity, namely that they considered that disposal of the asset purifies it from the defects that taint the owner's ownership. But how can a person give ownership of more than he owns? It appears that this problem prompted the jurists to conceive of the transfer of ownership to the buyer by possession of the sold item in an invalid sale, and that he can transfer its ownership to others or mortgage it. In these cases, the owner (1) cannot reclaim it from the third party. Rather, he can only claim from the first buyer the compensation, which is the value of the sold item. (From the treatise 'General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Law' by Professor Shafiq Shahata, pp. 172-173) (2) This is good speech and sound understanding in the theory of invalidity (2). (1) By 'owner' here is meant the seller in the first invalid sale, considering what was, because his ownership has transferred. (2) Professor Shafiq Shahata holds other views in analyzing the theory of invalidity that we do not approve. For example, he holds: That invalidity is in reality nullity, and that the invalid contract is in fact void with no effect between the contracting parties because the jurists have stated that if the guarantor pays under an invalid guarantee contract, he can reclaim what he paid, etc. And executing an invalid contract does not remove the invalidity. As for the jurists' statement that possession transfers ownership in an invalid sale, it is merely a conceptualization intended to validate the buyer's disposal of the sold item, thereby achieving protection of the third party's right. And supporting the non-transfer of ownership to the buyer in an invalid sale is that the sold real estate is not taken by pre-emption (shuf'a) despite possession. (From the mentioned treatise, pp. 169-170, summarized). I say: It becomes clear after what we have previously presented of the discussions on invalidity supported by texts that this view of Professor Shafiq Shahata is not correct for the following reasons: 1. Invalidity, as we have explained, is a third rank between validity and nullity (see: al-Bada'i 5/299), so the invalid contract cannot be considered void, even between the contracting parties. How so, when the texts of the jurists explicitly state that the invalid contract is concluded (mun'aqid), while the void one is not concluded! 2. Guarantee (kafala) is among the transactions in which there is no distinction between invalid and void; its invalidity in their terminology means nullity, as we have previously explained (see: f. 7/54). 3. If executing an invalid contract does not remove its invalidity, this does not necessitate that it be void between the contracting parties, because the requirement of invalidity is that the contract is subject to rescission, and this is not prevented by execution; rather, it is prevented by the attachment of a third party's right to the subject matter. 4. Real estate sold in an invalid sale is not taken by pre-emption from the buyer not because ownership has not transferred...
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi legal theory of fasād (invalidity) as a distinct category between sahīh (valid) and bātil (void). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that according to Abu Hanifa, a judge has authority to rescind a fasid contract regardless of the cause of fasād. Then he presents Imam Muhammad's view: if the fasād arises from a condition inserted for the benefit of one party, only that party has the right to rescind, because he can remedy the contract by dropping the condition; allowing the other party to rescind would deprive him of that right. The passage then quotes Professor Shafiq Shahata's analysis: the Hanafi rule that possession (qabd) transfers ownership in a fasid sale is a conceptual tool to protect third-party rights. Since there is no system for publicizing ownership defects in Islamic law, a third party dealing with the buyer in good faith may be unaware of the defect. To protect him, the jurists deemed that possession purifies the asset of defects, and the owner (original seller) cannot reclaim it from a third-party purchaser; he can only claim compensation from the first buyer. Shafiq Shahata, however, argues that fasād is really bātil, and the transfer of ownership by possession is just a fiction. The author then refutes this, citing that (1) fasād is a distinct, intermediate category; (2) kafala (guarantee) is a special case where 'fasād' refers to bātil; (3) execution of a fasid contract does not make it valid but does not render it void between parties—it remains subject to rescission unless a third-party right intervenes; (4) the non-application of pre-emption (shuf'a) in fasid sales of real estate is not due to non-transfer of ownership but because pre-emption rights attach only after complete transfer, and in fasid sales, the transfer is considered conditional.

Key Terms
فساد (fasād): A legal category between validity (sihha) and nullity (buṭlān); the contract is concluded (mun‘aqid) but defective, giving a right of rescission (faskh).
قبض (qabd): Possession; in a fasid sale, physical receipt of the sold item transfers ownership (according to the Hanafi theory).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory explained in the passage, what is the primary purpose of considering ownership transferred by possession (qabd) in a fasid sale?
A) To protect the seller's right to reclaim the item.
B) To protect the rights of third parties who deal with the buyer in good faith.
C) To make the fasid sale valid retroactively.
D) To allow the buyer to avoid paying the price.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of fasād uses possession to transfer ownership in defective sales, safeguarding third-party rights and ensuring contractual stability.

Fasād: The Hanafi Third Category and Its Defence
Defence of fasād as a third contractual category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that fasād is a distinct third contractual category and refute the claim that it is equivalent to buṭlān.

Core Idea
Fasād is not nullity: a corrupt contract is concluded but defective, with some legal effects.

This passage confronts a modern scholar who collapses the Hanafi distinction between fasād and buṭlān. Mustafa al-Zarqa defends the classical position, demonstrating that fasād occupies an intermediate rank between validity and nullity. You will learn the key proofs and the practical stakes of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
Shafiq Shahata argues that fasād is actually buṭlān and that a corrupt contract has no effects between the parties.
This is a modern deviation from the classical Hanafi view.
2
State the Hanafi counter-position
Fasād is a third rank: the contract is concluded (mun'aqid) but defective, producing some effects before rescission.
Refer to al-Bada'i' 5/299.
3
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Hanafi texts explicitly call a corrupt contract 'mun'aqid' and a void contract 'ghayr mun'aqid'. Possession transfers ownership, and the purchaser can be a pre-emptor.
These rulings confirm ownership transfer.
4
Analyse the opposing arguments
Shahata uses: (1) guarantee repayment, (2) execution not curing corruption, (3) no pre-emption on real estate from purchaser.
Each is addressed separately.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi responses
Guarantee is a special case; execution does not preclude rescission; lack of pre-emption is due to defeasible ownership, not absence of ownership.
The purchaser still becomes a pre-emptor for adjacent property.

ثالثة
٩/٥٦ - هذه هي نظرية الفساد في الفقه الإسلامي مفصلاً فيها ميناه النظري وأسبابه ونتائجه وهي ترينا أن الفساد في المؤيدات المدنية مرتبة بين مرتبتي الصحة والبطلان يكون العقد معها منعقداً مختلاً، فيستحق الفسخ وتثبت له قبل الفسخ آثار بصورة تختلف عما في حال الصحة (ر: البدائع ٢٩٩/٥ وما بعدها) .

Chapter 56: The Consequence of Corruption and What Pertains to It. The purchaser, by taking possession of the sold item in a corrupt sale, and that he may transfer its ownership to another or pawn it. In such cases, the owner (1) cannot reclaim it from the third party. Rather, he may only claim compensation from the first purchaser, which is the value of the sold item. (From the treatise 'The General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Law' by Professor Shafiq Shahata, pp. 172-173). (2) This is good speech and sound understanding in the theory of corruption. (1) By 'owner' here is meant the seller in the first corrupt sale, considering what was, because his ownership has transferred. (2) Professor Shafiq Shahata holds other views in analyzing the theory of corruption which we do not approve. Among them, he holds: that corruption is in reality nullity, and that the corrupt contract is in fact void, having no effect between the contracting parties, because the jurists have stated that if a guarantor pays under a corrupt guarantee contract, he may reclaim what he paid, etc., and that executing the corrupt contract does not remove the corruption. As for the jurists' statement that possession transfers ownership in a corrupt sale, it is merely a depiction intended to validate the purchaser's disposal of the sold item, thereby protecting the right of a third party. And supporting the non-transfer of ownership to the purchaser in a corrupt sale is that the sold real estate is not taken by pre-emption despite possession, etc. (The aforementioned treatise, pp. 169-170, summarized). I say: It is clear after what we have previously presented of the discussions on corruption, supported by texts, that this view of Professor Shafiq Shahata is incorrect for the following reasons: 1. Corruption, as we have explained, is a third rank between validity and nullity (see: al-Bada'i' 5/299). Thus, a corrupt contract cannot be considered void, not even between the contracting parties. How can this be, when the texts of the jurists explicitly state that the corrupt contract is concluded, while the void contract is not concluded! 2. Guarantee is among the transactions in which no distinction is made between corrupt and void; its corruption in their terminology means nullity, as we have previously explained (see: f 7/54). 3. If executing the corrupt contract does not remove its corruption, this does not necessitate that it be void between the contracting parties. For the requirement of corruption is that the contract is subject to rescission, and this is not prevented by execution; rather, it is prevented by the attachment of a right of a third party to the subject matter. 4. Real estate sold in a corrupt sale is not taken by pre-emption from the purchaser, not because ownership has not transferred to him by possession, but because his ownership therein is susceptible to being annulled by rescission of the sale due to corruption. Thus, his seller's right to reclaim it has not ceased (see: Murshid al-Hayran, articles 106 and 115). Rather, they have explicitly stated that the purchaser of real estate in a corrupt sale, if he takes possession, becomes a pre-emptor (shafi') in adjacent property by virtue of having become an owner, even though his ownership in the first property which is the object of pre-emption is susceptible to rescission due to corruption! (See: Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on corrupt sale, 4/124-125). Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Confirmations in Jurisprudence. 8/56 - Corruption is not removed by ratification: This, and it must be noted that the corruption of a contract, like its nullity, is not removed by ratification by one or both of the contracting parties, because it arises from violating the system of the contract, and no one has the right to confirm this violation. Rather, the corrupt contract remains subject to rescission even if the contracting parties ratify it. However, corruption can be removed by removing its cause, such as if the corruption is due to a prohibited invalid condition and the parties abandon the condition in the session of the contract, and likewise if the corruption is due to ignorance of one of the counter-values in a sale, or ignorance of the profit ratio in a partnership, and the parties specify the unknown counter-value or profit. In this case, the corruption is removed and the contract becomes valid; for it is among the established rules that 'when the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns' (see: f 28/7). Third. 9/56 - This is the theory of corruption in Islamic jurisprudence, detailed in its theoretical foundation, causes, and consequences. It shows us that corruption in civil confirmations is a rank between the ranks of validity and nullity, with the contract being concluded but defective, subject to rescission, and having effects before rescission in a manner different from that in the case of validity (see: al-Bada'i' 5/299 and after). 10/56 - Order of the states of the contract according to the types of civil confirmations: It must be observed, regarding the states of the contract according to all those civil confirmations, the following order:
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Hanafi theory of contractual corruption (fasād) as developed by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It begins by affirming the ruling that in a corrupt sale, possession transfers ownership to the purchaser, enabling him to transfer or pawn the item; the original seller can only claim compensation. The author then engages in a critical refinement of a modern view proposed by Shafiq Shahata, who incorrectly equates fasād with buṭlān (nullity). Al-Zarqa refutes this by demonstrating the classical Hanafi position: fasād is a distinct third category between ṣiḥḥa (validity) and buṭlān. A corrupt contract is 'mun'aqid' (concluded) but defective, whereas a void contract is not concluded at all. He addresses specific arguments: the guarantee contract's treatment is exceptional and not generalizable; execution does not preclude rescission unless a third-party right intervenes; and the rule on pre-emption (shuf'a) in corrupt sales—real estate is not subject to pre-emption from the purchaser not because ownership is absent, but because it is defeasible. Yet the purchaser becomes a pre-emptor for adjacent property, confirming ownership is transferred albeit defeasibly. The passage then clarifies that fasād cannot be cured by subsequent ratification (ijāza), as it violates the contract's system. However, if the cause of corruption is removed during the contract session (e.g., parties abandon an invalid condition or specify an unknown term), the contract becomes valid. This is supported by the qawida 'When the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns.' Finally, the author summarizes the theory and provides a hierarchy of contractual states based on the types of legal confirmations, emphasizing that fasād is intermediate between validity and nullity, with its own distinct effects.

Key Terms
فساد (fasād): A defect in a contract that makes it subject to rescission but does not render it void; the contract is concluded but defective, with some legal effects.
بطلان (buṭlān): Absolute nullity; the contract is not concluded and produces no legal effects.
إجازة (ijāza): Subsequent ratification or approval of a contract by one or both parties after its conclusion.
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption; the right of an adjacent owner to purchase property sold to another on the same terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory presented, which of the following is NOT an effect of possession (qabḍ) in a corrupt sale?
A) The purchaser can transfer ownership to a third party.
B) The original seller can no longer reclaim the item from any third party.
C) The contract becomes valid retroactively.
D) The purchaser can become a pre-emptor for adjacent property.

Key Takeaway
Fasād is a distinct third category: the contract is concluded but defective, with ownership transferred by possession, subject to rescission.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
Rules of Corrupt Contracts: Ratification and Removal of Cause
Exposition of rules on ratification and removal of cause in corrupt contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that fasād is not cured by ratification (ijāza) but can be cured by removing its cause during the contract session, using the qawida 'When the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns.'

Core Idea
Ijāza cannot cure fasād, but removing the cause in the session revives validity.

Having established that fasād is a distinct category, we now examine its two key rules: (1) ratification does not cure the defect, and (2) removal of the cause within the contract session restores validity. This lesson focuses on the qawida that governs this transformation and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule on ratification
Fasād, like buṭlān, is not removed by subsequent approval (ijāza) because it violates the contract's system.
No one can legitimize a violation of the contract's structure.
2
Identify the exception: removal of cause
If the cause of fasād is removed in the contract session, the contract becomes valid. Examples: abandoning a prohibited condition, specifying an unknown price or profit ratio.
This applies only if the defect is curable (e.g., jahāla, not illegality of subject matter).
3
State the governing qawida
The maxim 'When the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns' (إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع) captures this rule.
This is a sub-maxim (dabt) in Hanafi legal theory.
4
Apply the qawida to furuat
Work through the three examples given in the text: sale with invalid condition, sale with unknown price, partnership with unknown profit ratio.
In each case, removal of the defect in the session validates the contract.
5
Recognize the exception to the qawida
If the corruption is due to illegality of the subject matter itself (e.g., selling wine), removing the cause later does not cure the contract.
The impediment is inherent and cannot be removed retroactively.

(See: Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on corrupt sale, 4/124-125). Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Confirmations in Jurisprudence. 8/56 - Corruption is not removed by ratification: This, and it must be noted that the corruption of a contract, like its nullity, is not removed by ratification by one or both of the contracting parties, because it arises from violating the system of the contract, and no one has the right to confirm this violation. Rather, the corrupt contract remains subject to rescission even if the contracting parties ratify it. However, corruption can be removed by removing its cause, such as if the corruption is due to a prohibited invalid condition and the parties abandon the condition in the session of the contract, and likewise if the corruption is due to ignorance of one of the counter-values in a sale, or ignorance of the profit ratio in a partnership, and the parties specify the unknown counter-value or profit. In this case, the corruption is removed and the contract becomes valid; for it is among the established rules that 'when the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns' (see: f 28/7). Third. 9/56 - This is the theory of corruption in Islamic jurisprudence, detailed in its theoretical foundation, causes, and consequences. It shows us that corruption in civil confirmations is a rank between the ranks of validity and nullity, with the contract being concluded but defective, subject to rescission, and having effects before rescission in a manner different from that in the case of validity (see: al-Bada'i' 5/299 and after). 10/56 - Order of the states of the contract according to the types of civil confirmations: It must be observed, regarding the states of the contract according to all those civil confirmations, the following order:
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Hanafi theory of contractual corruption (fasād) as developed by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It begins by affirming the ruling that in a corrupt sale, possession transfers ownership to the purchaser, enabling him to transfer or pawn the item; the original seller can only claim compensation. The author then engages in a critical refinement of a modern view proposed by Shafiq Shahata, who incorrectly equates fasād with buṭlān (nullity). Al-Zarqa refutes this by demonstrating the classical Hanafi position: fasād is a distinct third category between ṣiḥḥa (validity) and buṭlān. A corrupt contract is 'mun'aqid' (concluded) but defective, whereas a void contract is not concluded at all. He addresses specific arguments: the guarantee contract's treatment is exceptional and not generalizable; execution does not preclude rescission unless a third-party right intervenes; and the rule on pre-emption (shuf'a) in corrupt sales—real estate is not subject to pre-emption from the purchaser not because ownership is absent, but because it is defeasible. Yet the purchaser becomes a pre-emptor for adjacent property, confirming ownership is transferred albeit defeasibly. The passage then clarifies that fasād cannot be cured by subsequent ratification (ijāza), as it violates the contract's system. However, if the cause of corruption is removed during the contract session (e.g., parties abandon an invalid condition or specify an unknown term), the contract becomes valid. This is supported by the qawida 'When the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns.' Finally, the author summarizes the theory and provides a hierarchy of contractual states based on the types of legal confirmations, emphasizing that fasād is intermediate between validity and nullity, with its own distinct effects.

Key Terms
فساد (fasād): A defect in a contract that makes it subject to rescission but does not render it void.
إجازة (ijāza): Subsequent ratification or approval of a contract.
إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع (idhā zāla al-māniʿ uʿida al-mamnūʿ): When the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Payment of the price after the session.
D) Any mutual agreement after the session.

Key Takeaway
Fasād is not curable by ratification, but can be cured by removing its cause in the contract session, per the maxim 'When the impediment is removed, the prevented thing returns.'

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
The Hierarchy of Contract States in Hanafi Law
Presenting a classification of contract states according to civil invalidities and their terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the Hanafi classification of contract states (mun'aqid, sahih, fasid, nafidh, mawquf, lazim) and apply the corresponding invalidity terms precisely.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a contract's state follows a precise hierarchy: concluded vs. void, valid vs. invalid, effective vs. suspended, binding vs. option-giving.

Understanding the precise classification of contracts is essential for a mufti. A contract is not simply valid or void; there are intermediate states that affect its enforceability and legal effects. This lesson unpacks the hierarchical taxonomy and its corresponding terminology, as presented by Mustafa al-Zarqa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Overarching Dichotomy
Start at the top: every contract is either 'mun'aqid' (concluded) or 'batil' (void). A void contract never comes into existence.
This is the first and most fundamental division.
2
Distinguish Valid vs. Invalid within Concluded Contracts
A concluded contract is either 'sahih' (valid) or 'fasid' (invalid). A fasid contract is concluded but defective due to a non-essential condition.
In Hanafi law, fasad is an intermediate category between valid and void.
3
Distinguish Effective vs. Suspended within Valid Contracts
A valid contract is either 'nafidh' (effective) or 'mawquf/mutawaqqif' (suspended). Nafidh is immediately binding without third-party ratification; mawquf requires ratification.
Suspension occurs when a third-party right is attached.
4
Distinguish Binding vs. Non-Binding within Effective Contracts
An effective contract is either 'lazim' (binding) or 'ghayr lazim' (non-binding, e.g., khiyar options). Lazim cannot be unilaterally rescinded without cause.
Ghayr lazim allows rescission within an option period.
5
Map the Corresponding Invalidity Terms
Each state has a corresponding invalidity: conclusion ↔ nullity, validity ↔ invalidity, effectiveness ↔ suspension, bindingness ↔ option/non-bindingness. Using terms imprecisely leads to confusion.
This mapping is crucial for accurate fatwa drafting.

[PAGE_714]
Chapter (56): The Result of Invalidity and What Pertains to It
The contract: either concluded, or void.
And the concluded: either valid, or invalid.
And the valid: either effective or suspended(1).
And the effective: either binding or option-giving, or non-binding.
See: al-Majalla, articles 105-115, and Radd al-Muhtar 4/100.
And attention must be paid to this correspondence between the names of these states and the names of the invalidities corresponding to them in the terminology of the jurists:
Thus, conclusion corresponds to nullity.
And validity corresponds to invalidity.
And effectiveness corresponds to suspension.
And bindingness corresponds to option or non-bindingness.
And it is not permissible to use any of these words in other than their proper place, nor to correlate them with other than their correlates, because of the confusion and ambiguity that would result from that, since each state differs from the other in concept and rulings.
(1) The effective contract is one that does not depend on the ratification of a non-contracting party because no right of anyone else is attached to it. The suspended contract is one that needs ratification from a non-contracting party because it affects his right, as previously discussed in the general effects of the contract in the theory of contracts. (See: F, 2/38).
Vov
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage presents a hierarchical classification of contracts based on their legal state. It begins by summarizing the Hanafi theory that invalidity (فساد) stands between validity and nullity: the contract is concluded but flawed, thus subject to rescission, yet it produces limited effects. The core classification: every contract is either concluded (منعقد) or void (باطل). A concluded contract is either valid (صحيح) or invalid (فاسد). A valid contract is either effective (نافذ) or suspended (موقوف/متوقف). An effective contract is either binding (لازم) or option-giving (مخير). The text also illustrates that even in an invalid sale, certain rights (like preemption) may arise. Finally, it provides a crucial terminological mapping: conclusion vs. nullity, validity vs. invalidity, effectiveness vs. suspension, bindingness vs. option. This mapping must be used precisely to avoid confusion in legal analysis.

Key Terms
منعقد (mun'aqid): A contract that is concluded (meets the basic elements of offer and acceptance).
فاسد (fasid): A concluded contract that is defective due to violation of a non-essential condition.
موقوف (mawquf): A valid contract that is suspended pending ratification by a non-contracting party.
نافذ (nafidh): A valid contract that is immediately effective without needing third-party approval.
لازم (lazim): A binding contract that cannot be unilaterally rescinded without cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi contractual hierarchy is a precise taxonomy that must be used with its corresponding invalidity terms to avoid legal confusion.
